THE RED PAGE.
Why the Boers Must Win.
nyr OT why they should win—that is a ques-
Iyl tion whose answer depends entirely upon
-1. l one’s own opinion as to the eternal justice
of British domination. My reading of
history (writes Rupert Hughes in N. Y. Criterion)
has forced me to the profound belief that the
Boers will not be crushed.
There is Jomini’s axiom that a nation cannot be
successfully invaded and subdued when the in-
habitants are united in spirit. Since the exodus
of the Outlanders, the Transvaal and the Free
State are counted as units for defence, while
Natal and Cape Colony contain many Dutch in-
habitants of very dubious loyalty to Queen Vic-
toria. Think, too, of the large percentage of
Tories that diluted the Americanism of our own
forefathers. It can surely be claimed that the
attainment of American independence was even
more difficult than the retention of Boer integrity.
England’s relative superiority to the thirteen little
colonies was as great then'as her superiority to the
Boers. The Bbers began with victory, and so did
we. But after the evacuation of Boston, the
Americans had yet to endure the bitterness of
Long Island, the Brandywine, Germantown,
Valley Forge, Arnold’s treason and the depreda-
tions of the Indians instigated and sometimes
led by the British. Even when English troops
press hard on the Boers, it must not lie felt that
an end is hereby put to their hopes. Thermopylae
preceded Marathon, and a chronological list of
the battles of our Revolutionary War, with “A”
standing for an American victory and “ B ” for a
British, makes this surprising beadroll divided by
years :
B, B, B—A, B, B, B, A—A, B, A, B, A, B, B,
B, B, A, A, A, B—A, B, A, B—A, B, B, A, B,
B—B, A, B, A, B, B, A—A, B, B, B, A.
Note the predominance of the “B,” and yet
the “A” won.
It must be remembered that England cannot
bring her navy into play now as she could against
the seaboard colonies of 1776, and even though she
has a mortgage on Portugal’s soul and has Delagoa
Bay to use as she will, yet it is a long shot and a
long walk to the battle-arena.
The Boers are not the first people confronted
with the problem of resisting a vastly larger
nation. The Greeks hurled back the Xerxic
horde; Syracuse sent the splendid Greek navy
limping home ; Hannibal’s enterprise failed after
he had crossed the Alps and ravaged Italy, and
knocked at Rome’s very gates ; the English did
not yield to the Armada, though the wind did far
more for them than their cannon ; Russia froze
out Napoleon’s host; the United States recovered
from two British invasions, in both of which they
captured our capital cities (and yet many think
that the marching of F- glish column into Pre-
Hrir " ■ he B .• v..; instantly). How much

another. Jan Potgiefcer is a nimrod. Tommy
Atk>ns is a cockney. The Boer oan live on a piece
of b. ig -days. ; Briton will mutiny at a
atom.
The Boers have not rested content with smoking
their pipes and grunting at English threats.
They have ammunition to burn and last them
three years. This period will be prolonged if they
oan coax a few more mules to stampede. They
have had plans drawn up in detail by German
experts, and have had German and American
military scientists teach them the latest arts.
They have spent millions of pounds buying war
materials which fill enormous warehouses. Their
artillery work has astounded the English, even
though they have not the advantage of lyddite.
They have improved Nature’s own fortresses with
the b est defences possible. They have prepared
great refrigerators against siege. The battles
already fought show that they have all the old
qualities, great solidity with amazing dash,
unanimity with individual initiative of the Yankee
sort, marksmanship, trickery and fearlessness.
They know the value of fighting from cover—a
practice no less important in days of artillery than
m bow-and-arrow times. The English confess
meekly that they have thusfar been outgeneraled
by the Boers, who surely cannot be denied
strategical genius. The English have showed in
many ways that fine old conservatism which has
been such a delight to their enemies. They are
brave with all an Englishman’s bravery, and
stupid with all an Englishman’s stupidity.
The Boers have shown a readiness to grasp the
nettle. Seeing war inevitable, they attacked
Behemoth before he was ready. Many a whale
has been bored to death by an alert swordfish.
The Boers fight like tigers and take victory like
Christian gentlemen. Fifty thousand of them in
a mountainous country equal thrice the array on
the plains. They have shown only one-half their
prowess as attackers. They fight even better on
the defensive; in one of their reported “routs”
they kidnapped their Hussar pursuers. One after
the other they have defeated every British general
brought against them.
Then there is the English side to consider.
Their confidence has been shaken by a series of
distressing humiliations that have killed Cham-
berlain’s popularity with his countrymen. The
English begin to feel that, after a few more
triumphs such as reported, they will be indeed
lost. The English peace party is very strong, and
Ministries are easily changed in England. Be-
sides, the English have always been quick to
recognise and acknowledge when they have had
enough.
They get cold feet easily, to use a metaphor.
To this, in a large measure, they owe their long
life as a power. Then there are, of course, the
diplomatic complications that made Yorktown
possible in 1781, hustled France out of Mexico,
and—the books are full of instances.
Considering all these things, and many others
1S no space to detail, I firmly believe that
the Boers must win. They are volunteers fighting
for liberty against a horde of mercenaries fighting
for a few gold-seekers. They have strategy,
modernity, initiative, a patriotism amounting to
religion, a confidence based on previous victory,
and a theatre of war of their own choosing and
their most intimate acquaintance. And they can
shoot straight.
The British at Omdurman.
FROM two recent sources comes light upon
the recent British campaign in the Soudan,
especially interesting now that Britain is
again at war. The first is Winston
Churchill s book, “ The River War ” ; the second
f. 11 . account of the battle of Omdurman pub-
i i cji ?, n , t “ e American press by Samweel Abdul-
laJi Shibley, an eyewitness of the battle.
Before Randolph Churchill’s brain failed in
general - paralysis he had a grand-mania period
during which his energy and eloquence made him
the foremost man in England. His son Winston
is a lively writer and soldier with much of his
father s force. As a war - correspondent in the
iransvaal, he was taken prisoner by the Boers,
escaped, and has been recaptured—possibly with
awkward results. ‘ ‘ The River War ”is an account
of Kitchener’s re-conquest of the Soudan, and will
always be a standard authority : it is the best
account so-far issued, and may remain the “ final”
book on the subject. The stories of correspon-
dents like G. W. Steevens are interesting and
delusive. Kitchener kept his correspondents well
under control; and at the battle of Omdurman
they were corralled far in the rear, taking notes of
such official reports as Kitchener chose to give
them. Churchill saw a little fighting ;he has had
better sources of information than any other
writer; and he dedicates his book to Lord Salis-
bury, to whose influence he has evidently been
much indebted.
The best of Churchill is that he is honest—as
far as he can be, if his book is to be printed in
Britain. He shows conclusively that Kitchener
made war like a savage, relying on his press
censor to prevent accounts of his barbarities from
reaching the English in time to impress the public
mind ; and relying upon his authority, and upon
British pride, to belittle and belie later accounts.
So, when war-correspondent Williams felt con-
strained to express his horror at the slaughter of
the Dervish wounded, war-correspondent Burleigh
was ready to curry favour with the authorities by
declaring Williams’ story false—so far as he knew.
But Winston Churchill leaves no doubt about the
matter.
A certain number—how many I cannot tell, but cer-
tainly not less than a hundred—wounded Arabs were
despatched, although they threw down their arms and
appealed for quarter. I have examined and listened to a
great deal of evidence on this point, and it does not
appear that there were more than a score of such cases in
all the British division and the 21st Lancers, and in every
case the men were severely reproved by their officers, and
prevented from repeating their brutal acts. . . Nearly
all who perished were killed by the Soudanese and
Egyptian troops, and in particular by Maxwell’s brigade.
Many atrocious acts were also perpetrated by the camp
followers.
And it must be remembered that not even
Churchill can tell all he knows. To pillory a sec-
tion of the British soldiery as hideous brutes—
though in every large army, British or other,
there is a section of hideous brutes—would alienate
his friends and injure his personal prospects. He
has to compromise between his inclination to tell
the whole truth, and his knowledge that the
whole truth would offend and injure the nation of
which he is a citizen. So he tells as much truth
as he can afford.
On the subject of Kitchener’s callous cruelty to
the wounded who were not killed, Churchill
is no less emphatic. Kitchener is a “ good
soldier ’’—that is to say,_ a good savage, taxing
himself and others mercilessly, and insensible to
human suffering as long as lie attains his end. He
does not, like a Red Indian brave, tie his prisoners
to a post, and slowly torture them to death. But,
to save his transport and commissariat, he delibe-
rately reduced his medical staff to insignificant
proportions, so that even his own wounded were
very badly cared for. Says Churchill—
In spite of the devoted efforts of the few medical
officers who had been allowed to accompany the force,
the wounded offioers and soldiers endured the greatest
miseries, and it is certain that several died of their
wounds who might under happier circumstances have
been saved. . . If more attention had been paid to the
subject arrangements might have been made which
would have increased the popularity of the commander
and diminished the sufferings of the troops.
The Dervish wounded were left to die slowly on
the battle-field exposed to a blazing sun. Three
days after the battle, three days during which
the sun had beaten on them mercilessly, there
were still wounded men alive among the dead.
And yet the gullible British public, as an English
paper points out, were assured, and believed, that
“the wounded Dervishes received every delicacy
and attention,” a statement which is, says
Churchill, “so utterly devoid of truth that it
transcends the limits of mendacity, and passes
into the realms of the ridiculous.” Compare with
Kitchener’s neglect the Boer care, which made
Sir George White telegraph, “ The enemy are very
kind to the wounded ”; and brought Lord Methuen’s
endorsement, “The enemy are most kind to the
wounded. And the British, who have attacked
the Boers for using Dum-dum bullets—which the
British themselves invented and first used—em-
ployed Dum-dum bullets relentlessly against the
Dervishes. It was General Gatacre, recently
beaten by the Boers, who ordered the troops to
convert the regular service bullets into improvised
Dum-dum bullets by filing off the tips so as to
expose the heavy core within the outer case.
The results were “found satisfactory as far as
killing power was concerned.” “I have seen a
Dervish,” writes S. A. Shibley, “with a tiny
bullet-hole iff his breast, but in his back a wound
more like that of a cannon-ball, so torn and rent
was his frame with the force of the bullet, which
expanded when it struck the vertebrae. To
recover from such a wound is simply impossible.”
Churchill calls the Mahdi “foremost among the
heroes of his race ” ; and as to ‘ ‘ the glory of Om-
durman,” where the Dervishes were withered by
artillery and rifle-fire, he says that—
The mind turns in disgust from the spectacle of un-
equal slaughter. . . . “The glory of Omdurman" will
seem to any who may five years henoe read this book a
very absurd expression.
The guilt of murdering the wounded is brought
home to Kitchener himself. Churchill divides
the Dervish wounded into three classes—(l) Those
who were dangerous, (2) those who, being terri-
bly wounded, were killed to put them out of their
misery ; and (3) those who were butchered in cold
blood. And he says—
I must personally record that there was a very general
impression that the fewer the prisoners the greater would
be the satisfaction of the commander. The sentiment
that the British soldier is incapable of brutality is one
which never fails to win the meed of popular applause ;
but there are, in fact, a considerable proportion of oruel
men in every army. The mistaken impression I have
alluded to encouraged this class. The unmeasured terms
in which the Dervishes had been described in the news-
papers, and the idea which had been laboriously circu-
lated of “avenging Gordon,” had inflamed their passions,
and had led them to believe that it was quite correct to
regard their enemy as vermin— unfit to live. The result
was that there were many wounded Dervishes killed. •
And this is ChurchiH’s picture of the man who
goes to aid in destroying the homes and liberties
of the Dutch in South Africa—
The General, who never spared himself, cared little for
others. He treated all men like machines—from the
private soldiers, whose salutes he disdained, to the
superior officers he rigidly controlled. The comrade who
had served with him and under him for many years in
peace and peril was flung aside incontinently as soon as
he ceased to be of use. The Sirdar only looked to the
soldiers who could march and fight. The wounded
Egyptian, and latterly the wounded British soldier, did
not excite his interest, and of all the departments of the
army the one neglected was that connected with the care
of the sick and injured. The lamentable episode of the
Mahdi’s tomb has already been noticed. The stern and
unpitying spirit of the commander was Communicated to
his troops, and the victories which marked the progress
of the River War were accompanied by acts of brutality
not always justified by the harsh customs of savage
warfare.
So far Churchill, whose softened testimony even
the British cannot impugn. S. A. Shibley, whose
testimony the British can impugn if they
like, but, whose story bears all outward
evidence of truth, is a native of Syria, educated at
the American College in Beyrout, who was
attached to the Intelligence Department of the
Guards in the Soudan campaign, and received two
medals for bravery and efficient service. He
points out that he has no motive either to harshen
or to mitigate the facts.
The rank and file of the English soldiery, the very
backbone of the expedition, I moved among constantly ;
with the native Egyptian troops, the arm and sinew of
the enterprise, and the loyal Soudanese, who were ever
pushed in the van and made the shield and targets of the
main body, and to whom, God witness, i’s due the glory
and honour of victory—l was ever en rapport, knew their
sufferings and pleasures, saw them living and dead.
Never stationed in one spot, my affairs kept me on
the run from post to post and through all ranks of the
soldiery, from the pampered sons of earls and dukes,
safely bivouacked in the rear, nibbling wafers and sip-
ping champagne over their oards in comfortable tents,
to the Baggara outposts in sleepless vigilance over a vast
untented field of sleeping humanity, fulfilling a hero’s
destiny on a diet of dharrah and brackish water, watch-
ing the fires of the enemy afar, ready to up and fight at
the roll of the drum and the shriek of the bugle, to lay
down their lives in the bleak wilderness of the upper
Nile and be forgotten, while those who said “ Go ! ” and
he goes, and “ Come !” and hq comes, took all the glory.
Shibley declares that to the Soudanese and
the chief credit of the Omdurman vic-
tory is due.
The magnificent fighting of the Soudanese and the
Egyptians in that terrible h,our of battle is something
that God must reward them for in the hereafter ;
precious little credit have they received at the hands of
the English, or the world in general, so thoroughly has
Great Britain taken all to herself the meed of praise and
the spoil of conquest. I repeat, that the success of the
charge of the heights where the Dervishes were so solidly
entrenched, and the whole success of that mighty first
blow to the Khalifa, were due to the Soudanese and
Egyptian troops, under their native officers ; for, not
only did they scale the mountain stronghold under a
whirlwind of hell fire, but they dashed into the very pits
of the Dervishes, and drove them scattering and stam-
peding from their fortress, while the rear battalions split
at the base of the mountain, and drove the enemy around
both sides of the hill, pressing onward toward Omdurman
and victory.
This is bad enough for the British ; but worse
follows. “As the English troops came up,” con-
tinues Shibley,
with the gay Twenty-first Lancers flourishing on the left,
the latter had no more than rounded the edge of the hill
when a party of Dervishes, who were lying in ambush
toward the Nile, sprang up amongst them, with wild yells
and clashing swords. The mounted men were taken by
complete surprise. After the Soudanese and Egyptians
had performed such miracles of courage —yes, and the
rank-and-file of the English, too—one would have thought
that this crack battalion would have plunged into the
thick of the ambushed Mahdists and fought them to the
death. But not so. There was an instant of amazed
hesitation, an hysterical mock show of resistance ; a few
brave men dashed into the disheveled ranks and fell
fighting; but the majority wheeled about in a body, and
back they came plunging and leaping toward the refuge
of the lines in the rear, the Dervishes following swiftly
on foot and actually clinging to the horses’ rumps, slash-
ing and lunging till kicked off by the flying hoofs and
bowled over headlong in the sand. I stood perfectly
stunned by the spectacle, staggered with disillusion of
vaunted British bravery ; for not less than ninety-four of
the lancers, by my own count, returned to safety, the
flanks of their horses dripping with blood, themselves
wounded, one and all, in the back !
I know that this event has been bruited over England
and the world as second only to the charge of Balaklava ;
but if so, then Balaklava is one of the most colossal fabri-
cations of all time. To that hour I had been an English
worshiper, regarding Britains as the most civilised of the
conquerors of the world—heroes to a man. But the shock
of disillusion here made a lasting impression upon me.
Trembling in every fibre, my eyes blinded with tears, I
sat down and watched a lancer riding by, a score of
wounds in his back gushing blood, his horse’s rumps
stuck with spears and slashed to ribbons. Ido not know
what mad sentiment escaped my lips at that moment;
but I was brought to my feet by the eommand of an
officer at my side i
“ Shut up I" he growled. “If you ever say anything
about this, I’ll run you through. ”
There was the whole situation in a word. There was not
a foreign correspondent within five hundred miles of the
spot, and the English correspondents were cooped far in the
rear, taking notes of such events and facts as the officers
reported and nothing more, tongue-tied and censored to
a degree inconsistent with modern methods when a battle
for civilisation is supposed to be a matter th%t concerns
all the world. For instance, the published statement
from the field, that the Dervishes fell to the number of
eleven thousand that eventful day, is grossly exaggerated,
for not more than four thousand was the actual number.
As for the battalions of the Grenadier Guards and the
other petted regiments of the service, it is true that they
smelt powder-smoke and did a deal of shrieking; but I
state here, to my certain knowledge, that they were
never once nearer the danger line than five hundred
yards, with a solid wall of Soudan dust and cannon-smoke
between them.
And in all this there is nothing improbable.
The typical Briton is as brave as any other man-
or, if you like, a little braver; but average courage
is partly due to lack of imagination, and partly to
readiness and preliminary preparation. Very few
men will turn tail when they are “strung up” to
fight, are ready and expect to fight a known and
ascertained enemy ; but the bravest troops will
fly from an ambush, or when their “nerve” is
spoilt by fatigue or disaster. It is the business of
officers and men to cover up these occasional flights,
and covered up they are, to flatter the popular
fiction that every soldier is a herb. But Kipling,
in “The Drums of the Fore and Aft,” describes
one of the routs which have been not infrequent
in India ; and there are other witnesses.
Shibley goes on to describe the insolent treat-
ment of Egyptian soldiers and officers by the
British, and the horrors which accompanied the
sack of Oindurman ; and decides that it is to Slatin
Pasha, the Austrian, that the British owe the
,Soudan. Taken with GhurchiU’s, his account of
Oindurman leaves a picture of British warfare as
disgraceful as anything of the kind on record.
The English prisoners suffocated in the Black
Hole of Calcutta suffered less than the Dervishes
left to die of wounds and thirst under the blazing
sun of Omdurman.
—... ■» ———■ V .. - /
William Watson, who damned Sultan Abdul
for attacking the Armenians, is consistent in his
sympathies with the small dog, and joins with
Herbert Spencer, Frederic Harrison, and other
English writers, in condemning the Grab-War
upon the Transvaal. Criticising war-poetry in
general, and Swinburne’s “England, strike
home ! ” sonnet in particular, he writes—
Let us remember, that the existence of a great theme,
not less certainly than of a great poet, is one of the indis-
pensable antecedent conditions of great poetry. The
assassination of a State, and the strangling of a people,
are not heroieal themes, and never while this world
endures shall they evoke one note of noble song. More-
over, in all combats between a giant and a stripling the
Muse must of necessity be at a certain moral disadvantage
in the sorriewhat ludicrous task of enheartening the giant.
It is the valour of David with his sling, and not the arro-
gant bulk of Goliath, that kindles the imagination of
poets, and captures for ever the sympathies of man.
Memoranda—
W. H. Fitchett’s new Jingo book, “How England
Saved Europe,” is spread oht in four volumes. There is
an English suggestion that, in choosing a dear form, the
publishers will miss the cheap readers who bought
“Deeds” and “Fights.” But just now a record of
British victories will be consoling at any price.
Britain, in fact, has let slip the dogs of war, and Kip-
ling and Austin the doggerel.
Just issued at 16s. a life of Charles Sturt, Australian
explorer, by the wife of his sou, engineer-colonel Napier
Sturt,
Robert Buchanan’s literary reminiscenoes are announced
forthcoming, and will contain all the good reading which
Justin McCarthy left out.
Ob. Florence Marryat, tenth child of Captain M., and
author of about seventy indifferent novels.
The poetry of the Celt is merely the intensified
poetry of strong, emotional, unliterary races. General
Joubert’s remark on the war— “The heart of my soul is
bloody with sorrow ’’—would come fitly from the mouth
of the bard of the sons of Usna.
“ Kipling has recently joined an Edinburgh Masonic
lodge, where he is known as Brother Joseph Rudyard
Kipling.”
One of the moles who burrow for Notes and Queries
finds an original of “Rome was not built in a day” in
some lines of Palingenius’ “ Zodiacus Vitse ” (c. 1627), bk.
xii, 460—
Non stella una cavat marmor, necque protinus uno est
Condita Roma die .
Poet—“ Why is it you never print any of the verse I
send you ? ” Editor— “ Why is it you never send me
any of the verse I print?”
Some unprinted lines by the late Francis
Adams
IN THE " BOORA."
Here where the black man sat, I sit, and where
His gunyah spread fine odours in the air,
The ashes of some swagman’s fire lie dead :
But X, like black man, in the water stare.
The muddy God Australian in his bed 1—
Not less the water-violet lifts her head
By her frail fellow in the grass with the may,
Whereby the brown shows yellow plashed with red :
Not less the maiden-hair spreads, hid away
Round grassy tree-roots, while the live-long day
The creaking frogs give back to overhead
The birds' short songs, and lizards glide and stray.
Francis Adams.
More interesting than stimulating, maybe.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running' a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have along thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How Long?
How long will the parsons lie,
Who, smiling and smirking, tell
Of a “ beautiful land on high ”
For the vile who oppress and cry :
“ O, Lord ! I believe ! ” —and yell
To the unbelievers who dwell
In goodness, fierce threats of hell ?
How long will the parsons lie ?
How long will the rulers lie,
Who, double-faced, aye delight
To trade on a cheap-drawn sigh
For the poor, but are ever nigh
To work out the rich man’s spite,
And crush, with tyrannic might,
All freedom and truth and right?
How long will the rulers lie?
How long will the rich men lie,
And claim as their own the land—
The land that the poor live by—
And all things beneath the sky;
The toil of the worker’s hand,
The lives of the hunger-banned ?
How long shall their false claim stand ?
How long will the rich men lie ?
• •
How long will the People lie %
In abject and crouching woe
At the feet of the men on high,
Who are only men, and can die ?
How long will they vainly cry ?
How long ere their rights they know ?
How long till they sweep the sky
With Freedom’s flag, and defy
The forces of Tyranny ?
How long ere they boldly go
To Slavery’s overthrow ?
How long will the reckoning grow ?
How long will the people lie ?
J. A.ANDREW[?].
The Rose and the Wish.
There’s a red rose tree in the Gardens ;
It’s all a-hum with bees ;
And the girls go trooping around it,
Eyes wide, in twos and threes.
But a man stands guard o’er the treasure
That tempts each dainty nose,
And, although they tip-toe to rifle,
It’s few may steal a rose.
There’s a wishing tree in the Gardens ;
It towers above all trees ;
And the girls go trooping around it
In laughing twos and threes.
With their eyes upcast to the brmehes
They blush and name their loves,
And their wishes mount to the tree-tops
And brood aloft like doves.
But alas for Fate in the foreground !
And woe to weakly flesh 1
And it’s only one in a hundred
That comes to get her wish.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN .
The Challenge.
I paused, as the woman was passing ;
The crowd flung me out of my place ;
But her roving eyes swung in their orbits,
And focussed their glance on my face.
A moment it lit, and then vanished
As swift as the swing of a bird,
And left me with eyes that were swimming,
And ears that but vacantly heard.
And all that my brain could remember,
As I shook myself up with a start,
Were insolent eyes that were staring
Through mine to the depth of my heart.
Gray eyes could not glance with that boldness,
Nor blue with that passionate light,
Nor brown with their deep amber softness—
But only orbs black as the night.
And they must be liquid and gleaming,
Like metal gray-hot from the fire,
And full of a tentative meaning
That quickens the flame of desire.
Shall I take up the challenge she threw me—
The lightly-cast taunt of her sex,
That womanhood dangles before us
While binding her chains on our necks?
Shall I bite, at the lure of the tempter,
Red lips, with their subtle demand —
Or crouch, like a dingo in hiding,
If Prudence but lift up her hand ?
Shall I call myself man or a nithing?
Shall I churn my red blood into milk?
Shall I flee at the shimmer of satin
Or faint at the rustle of silk ?
Shall I hang in the wind when a womaw
Gives signal to follow her ? No !
Should pleasure or pain be the profit
The gods may decide. I shall go.
W.A.
JULIAN MITCHELL.
At Richmond (Vic.), the other day, a boy
of thirteen was charged with habitual cruelty to
his father. Young Australia had been in the
habit of “ laying for ” the old man with a billet of
wood. He was sent to the reformatory. Some
of our young children are quite unfit to be trusted
with the control of adults.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 30, 1899.
The People or the Syndicates.
In view of the fact that the Commonwealth will
shortly be established, and that a Commonwealth
capital will shortly be wanted, the Lyne Govern-
ment has provided one of its friends with a light,
clean, airy billet inquiring after possible sites.
What kind of instructions were given to the Lyne
Government’s hired inquirer and official note of
interrogation isn’t definitely known, but if any
opinion can be formed from the scanty published
reports of his proceedings, he has got the wrong
instructions. Or if he hasn’t got the wrong in-
structions, he has perhaps misunderstood the right
ones, or perhaps he is the wrong man altogether.
The terms of the federal Constitution are that the
Federal capital is to be in territory now belonging
to New South Wales but to be ceded to the
Federal Government, said territory to be at least
100 miles from Sydney, and not less than
100 square miles in area. It may be as
much larger and as much further from
Sydney as are thought desirable. Any land on the
site selected which is now the property of the
N.S.W. Government is to become the property
of the Federal Government free of charge; as
regards private lands, the-Federal Government,
as sole ruler of this patch of territory,- will have
the same rights of resumption that every Govern-
ment enjoys ; it has merely to pass the necessary
laws and make the necessary arrangements for
assessing and paying compensation.
* * • •
The Lyne Government’s Commissioner appears
to possess all the elements of a great mistake from
first to last. To begin with, the Federal capital
is to be the joint property of five States—until the
other two cqme in, when it will be the joint pro-
perty of sevtffi ; it is to be paid for by five States,
and is to be placed where it will best suit the in-
terests of five States. When one State appoints
an agent, pointedly ignoring the other four States
and joint-proprietors, to select possible sites, a
suspicion will probably arise in the minds of
the other four that he is appointed to select
and recommend sites which will suit that
one State only, and that they were ignored
of cat jmrpose. Also, when ho was ap-
pointed by a Premier who was mostly against
Federation, and who was one of a little parochial
band that was willing to sell Australia into per-
petual disunion unless it got the Federal capital
located in, or close to, its own city of Sydney, an
idea may get abroad that the stipendiary investi-
gators’ chief business is to find promising sites as
close to Sydney as the 100-miles limitation will
permit, and to find eminently unpromising sites
everywhere else. Also, what is worst of all, the
commissioner appears to hold a brief for private
landlordism, and to assume, without any shadow
of authority, that the Federal Government won’t
decide to have national land-ownership inside the
Federal territory, and that it will make a present
to the private land-owner of all the vast
increase in values that will be created by
the expenditure of the Commonwealth’s money
on Federal territory. Furthermore, a ques-
tion has arisen as to whether the fact
that the Federal capital is to be at least
100 miles from Sydney means 100 miles as the
crow flies, or 100 miles by the nearest road or
railway—which latter may mean only 50 miles in
a straight line. Imperial law measures distance
as the crow flies; N.S.W. law measures it by the
nearest road; and certain Sydney politicians
insist on the N.S.W. measurement being adopted,
in the hope of thereby dragging the Federal city
closer up to Sydney’s back-door. And the Com-
missioner apparently holds a brief for this special
view of the case, which, if adopted, may lead to
fearful and wonderful complications hereafter.
For, if the Federal capital is planted on a site
that is now, say, 105 miles from Sydney by a
devious mountain railway, it is probably oniy a
matter of time till the straightening and improve-
ment of the line disqualifies the new and highly-
expensive city by reducing the distance from
Sydney to 95 or 90 miles.
* » * *
The following extract from a recent daily-paper
report of the Commissioner’s sayings and doings
at Bathurst probably sums up that gentleman’s
objectionable attitude as briefly as possible :
Mr. Oliver said he had been receiving a great sand-
papering from the Melbourne papers for having said that
the question of distanoe from Sydney was governed by
local law rather than Imperial statute. The Age and
Argus had sent him what they considered good advice.
They urged that it was a question for the Federal Parlia-
ment to decide. He had only expressed his opinion, but
was supported in it by' some of the leading spirits in the
Federation movement. He argued that it would not be
right to exclude any site which conformed to the rule of
being without the 100 mile - radius. Messrs. Barton,
Wise, O Connor and other authorities agreed that the
point was arguable, but that his contention was right.
As far as Bathurst was concerned, only one portion of
the proposed area would be affected, even if the distance
was taken as the crow flies, The Federal Parliament
would only resume what was requisite for buildings,
parks, reserves, dkc., and the N.S.W. Government would
hand over all land vested in them. Court-houses and
other public buildings were not vested in the Crown, as
were other lands, and where these were resumed the
Government was compensated.
• • * *
The Bulletin calls Mr. Oliver’s attitude ob-
jectionable for many good and sufficient reasons.
In the first place it is, of course, a fact that a great
deal depends on whether the 100 miles distance
of the Federal capital from Sydney is measured
as the crow flies or (as the Commissioner and the
purely Sydney crowd demand) by the nearest
means of access. In the latter case a number of
places may crawl in as eligible—some of them with
a good many miles to spare—which would other-
wise be shut out. But the 100 miles ought to be
measured as the crow flies. In that case the
Federal city which is eligible now will always
remain eligible. If a place is selected which is
just over the 100-miles limit by the nearest exist-
ing crooked road or railway, then it will be necess-
ary to enact that anybody attempting to open up
a shorter road, or to invent a new kind of railway,
aerial or otherwise, which can take a straight line
over the steepest grades, shall get five years’ im-
prisonment as a public enemy who is trying to
disqualify the Federal capital and put Australia
to the fearful expense of getting a new one. More-
over the Commissioner shouldn’t be fooling around
any places just on the 100-mile limit, in the
already populous parts of the country. The
capital,. instead of being as near Sydney as
the limit will allow, should be as far away
as it can get without going close up to Bris-
bane or Melbourne or Adelaide, which would be
every whit as objectionable. Seeing that the
building of a Federal capital means creating a
large new centre of population, every effort
should be made to find an eligible site where
there is no population now, or next to none—
where land-values are lowest, and where the
country most wants opening up. The fact that
Bathurst, Goulburn, Wagga, Armidale t Albury,
Dubbo, Orange, &c., are good - sized towns
in thickly-settled districts, where almost every
inch of land is already privately-owned, is just the
reason why every one of them should be rejected
firmly and definitely.
• * * *
And if the Lyne Government’s Commissioner
really stated, as alleged in the newspaper report,
that the Federal Government would only resume
the land required for buildings, parks, reserves,
that it would not insist on being its own
ground landlord, and on securing for the nation
the enormous wealth in the shape of increased
land-values which will be created by the expendi-
ture of the nation’s cash on the new capital, but
will let all that stupendous profit go to private
land-grabbers, banks and syndicates—if he really
said that then he invented the thing that is not
true. Also, he evidently holds a brief for the pri-
vate land-stealer and the private syndicate, and
against the nation at large. It is not settled that
the Federal Government will be the unspeakable
fool and criminal that the Lyne Government’s
agent cheerfully assumes it will be. If the Federal
Government has brains enough to grasp a great
opportunity it will insist on these three things
(1) That the Federal territory (which is to be not less
than 100 square miles in area -equal to a square 10 miles
each way) shall be at least 5000 square miles (say a square
70 miles each way).
(2) That every inch of the soil in the said territory
shall be for ever and inalienably the property of the Com-
monwealth. Whatever form of leasing may be adopted
the Commonwealth must be the sole ground landlord’
and all leases must be subject to periodical re-valuation.
(3) That the capital must be in a cool, well-watered
spot high up on the N.S.W. table-land and as far removed
as circumstances will permit from all listing centres of
population.
* •
These are points of such immr /ise importance
that the Australian community can’t fix its eye
on this matter any too soon. The new city should
be high up on the table-land because it w'ill grow
into a great city some day, with a great surround-
mg population as well, and when there is a chance
to get a million, or two, or three, of extra popula-
tion, it is essential that they be, not enervated
dwellers in the steaming coist lands, but hardy
highlanders with the splendid physique that the
dwellers in the cold highlands develope—good
athletes, good soldiers, with a reserve of good
blood for the future repair of the debilitated Aus-
tralian constitution. It is essential that the Com-
monwealth should be sole ground-landlord because,
by establishing in a spot, now empty or nearly so
Parliament Houses, Governor’s residence, public
offices, courts of justice, gaols, military head-
quarters, &0., &c\, it will gradually draw together
a great population, and thus turn a site, now
worth, perhaps, half-a-million at most, into one
worth 40 or 50 millions. And the question
that the nation has to decide is just whether it
will insist on the right to buy that site bodily at
its value as it was immediately before it was fixed
upon as the location of a Federal capital, and
make these 40 or 50 millions, created by the
nation’s expenditure, the nation’s property—or
whether it will make them a present to the land-
boom fiends of Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide and
Brisbane. In other words, whether the nation
will hang on to a prospective income (not so very
far away either) of a million or two annually,
or give it to the least deserving class in the
community. As to the reason why the Federal
territory should be 5000 square miles instead of 100,
that speaks for itself. If the Federal territory is
only ten miles by ten, even if the Commonwealth
is sole ground landlord inside the territory, much
of the unearned increment will be annexed by the
private land-owner over the border. It wants to
be so large that the Commonwealth will appro-
priate all the increased land-values for itself, and
so that nothing, or practically nothing, will slop
over into the hands of the private owner outside.
* * * *
Of course, it would require money—much
money—to buy up the freehold of 5000 square
miles of land, to lay out a city thereon, and to
build a Parliament House, Government offices,
barracks, State bank, law courts, residence for the
official figurehead of State, the harmless necessary
lock-up, gaol, &c. Well, the money can be got
without taxation, without borrowing, without
paying one penny of interest, without robbery,
without injury to trade, and without damag-
ing anybody’s vested injury. It can be
done without adopting any wild, reckless, novel
or untried theories, but simply by following the
sound and well-tested policy of a British com-
munity which was blessed in this matter with more
sense than ourselves. In Canada it has been the
law for many years that every bank shall keep 40
per cent, of its cash reserves in State legal tender
notes. To put it another way, the State has a
perpetual loan, without interest, of several mil-
lions which would otherwise have been lying idle
in.the banks’ vaults and earning absolutely no-
thing. A similar law would give the Common-
wealth £10,000,000 or more in cash without any
interest to pay, and with no obligation as to re-
payment at any fixed period. And with that
fund the Federal territory of 5000 square miles
could be acquired and the new Federal city
created in other words, a magnificent asset,
which would yield the Commonwealth a great,
perpetual, and ever-expanding income, could be
created out of nothing. It is merely a question
as . to whether Australia is or is not too tired to
seize the opportunity, It is an opportunity that
will only come once in Australia’s history.
• * * m
Of course all that section of the influential and
respectable press whose proprietors intend to go
land-jobbing, and booming, and syndicating and
trading in wild cats in and around the new
Federal city will denounce the idea of the Com-
monwealth being its own ground landlord as
visionary. Also these disinterested syndicators and
wild-catters will say off-hand that it is impossible,
not to be seriously considered, the dream of an ill-
balanced Labor Party, something contrary to
sound banking theories, an outbreak of well-
meaning socialism, and a scheme whose absurdity
is self-evident and which needs no refutation. In
short the stodgy, tall-hat party will do everything
except prove that there is anything absurd or im-
possible or even difficult about the matter. So far
as the community is concerned the case can go
into two sentences: The creation with public
MONEY OP A NEW CAPITAL WILL ALSO CREATE A
VAST AMOUNT OP WEALTH IN THE SHAPE OP
INCREASED LAND VALUES. EITHER THE NATION
(WHICH CREATED THEM) OR THE PRIVATE LAND
SPECULATOR (WHO DIDN’T CREATE THEM) IS TO HAVE
THAT WEALTH. NOW, WHO IS TO HAVE IT?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Some Very Plain English.
“Falcon” of Newcastle (N.S.W.) Herald
sends the following :
Editor, Bulletin. —Under the heading “The Bulletin’s
•hay Sermon " you affirm among other things that you are
not a “ good enough liar,” etc. Those who have lived
behind the scenes are not prepared to accept your affir-
mations. The} 7 are more inclined to denounce you as a
good enough perjurer to violate your newspaper oath in
pretty well every issue. The accusation may be best
made in the form of a question having special reference
to your attitude on the Boer War. Is it not a fact that
the publisher of The Bulletin is under an oath, made
before a judge of the Supreme Court of this Colony not
to allow to appear in its columns anything the character
of which is seditious or against the good government of
her Majesty the Queen, and that sureties were entered
into for its due observance ? I challenge you first to pub-
lish my question intact in a column where men who do
not know you may read it, and second to give it a truth-
ful answer.
.The Bulletin has the greatest possible plea-
sure in publishing “ Falcon’s ” question intact
in a column where it may be read both by men
who know this paper, and men who don’t
know it, and all other sorts and conditions of
men as well. And it proceeds to give him a
truthful answer with great pleasure ; in fact,
the few minutes thus spent constitute one of
the happiest periods in its life. The publisher
of The Bulletin is under some such oath as
above described—he doesn’t remember the
exact words, but the meaning is all right. And
for nearly 20 years this paper has striven hard
to keep down sedition (Webster’s dictionary
defines sedition as synonymous with “insur-
rection, tumult, uproar,' riot, rebellion, re-
volt,” also “excitement of discontent against
the Government” and “resistance to lawful
authority ”) by helping, so far as in it lay,
to remove the causes which lead to tumult,
and to make the Government one that no one
would be discontented with, and to make
lawful authority so walk an honourable chalk
line that the public wouldn’t dream of wanting
to resist it. Also, it has never knowingly or
intentionally said a word against the good
goverment of Her Majesty the Queen, though
it may have been justly severe at times on the
bad government—not of the Queen, who
doesn’t govern, but of those who do so in her
name. It never took an oath to uphold the
bad government of anybody, only the good
government; and being somewhat particular
about its oaths it lias toiled hard all the days
of its life to uphold every sort and variety of
good government it has met with (there isn’t
very much of it) and to arouse popular objec-
tions against all had government. Would
“ Falcon ” like to ask any more questions’
Wolseley’s Reputation.
It is high time that Field-Marshal Wolseley
began to consider his reputation buried in
South Africa along with all the other bloated
British reputations. Exactly 20 years ago—in
December, 1879 —when Wolseley was com-
manding the cocksure troops in the Transvaal,
he stated (at a banquet to Bartle Frere) that
no British Ministry would dare to surrender
the Transvaal to the Boers. Also, he pooh-
poohed the very palpable signs of discontent
among the Boers, whose grievances at that
time were based partly on real fact, and partly
on suspicion—false, but perfectly natural sus-
picion. Twelve months later the Boers
opened their rebellion—or War of Indepen-
dence— with an unexpected attack on Colonel
Anstruther’s force of 250 men, marching
from Middieburg to Pretoria. Ansteuther was
told by messenger that a Boer Republic had
been proclaimed, and any advance of the
British detachment beyond Bronkhorst Spruit
would be treated as a declaration of war. The
message gave the astonished commander two
minutes in which to decide whether he would
halt or advance. Anstruther could only say
that he was instructed to proceed to Pretoria,
and immediately the war began ; 120 Britishers
were “laid out” by Boer bullets—the others
surrendered. So much for Wolseley’s know-
ledge of Boer feeling towards the British.
A little later came Majuba, and Glad-
stone relinquished the Transvaal to Boer
control. So much for Wolseley’s knowledge
of what the British Government would dare to
do. During the past 19 years Wolseley, the
small-headed hero of petty warfare, has learned
nothing, seemingly, of the country wherein he
prattled nonsense to Bartle Frere’s banquet-
ters. As Commander-in-Chief of the British
Army, he utterly failed to realise the magni-
tude of the task he took in hand last October.
His original plans for the invasion of Boer
Africa were, by his own recent admissions,
formed on a scale of arrogant ignorance. He
failed to foresee that the Boers would invade
the British colonies and gain large reinforce-
ments from the Afrikanders there. He was
so staggered by the news of the British re-
treat from Dundee that he lied to the House of
Commons and called the retreat a “ change of
front.” When is the cable going to announce
the decease and interment of Garnet Wolse-
ley’s reputation ?
Australian Labor Parties—the
Sweaters’ Friends!
With few individual exceptions, the Australian
Labor Parties in Parliament are now found
supporting or acquiescing in the despatch of
Australian mercenaries to the Transvaal—to
fight under the syndicate-banner in the Great
Grab War, and aid in sweating the wages of
Johannesburg workers down to Kimberley
level, for the benefit of the Rhodesian gang of
capitalists. Labour representatives, one of
whose chief aims in Australia is to keep up
wages to a decent level, doing their best to
bring down the wages of their co-workers in
Africa that the dividends of the Rand
companies may be further increased ! This
is the result which Rhodes and Co.
have striven for; but they' could hardly have
expected that the Australian Labor Parties
would help them. As every South African
know's, under Boer rule Johannesburg was one
of the best places in the w'orld for the white
worker ; as under British syndicate rule Kim-
berley is one of the worst. Right up to the
outbreak of the war, under the so-called Boer
tyranny, white miners and artisans earned at
Johannesburg from £1 to £2 per day ; while
at Kimberley wages have been sweated to from
£!2 to £3 10s. per week. And, in spite of these
wages, the Rand mining dividends for 1898
were £4,847,505 — more than the whole income of
the Transvaal Govt. From 1892 to 1898,
when, according to the Rhodesian yarns,
the Johannesburg mining industry was op-
pressed to the last degree, the dividends
paid rose from Bs. to 13s. 2d. per ton of ore
crushed. This is the statement, not of one of
Rhodes’s opponents, but of his friend, John
Hays Hammond, one of the signatories to the
famous sham “ women and children ” letter
—concocted months before the Jameson Raid,
and published as extempore by London Times
on the day the Raid commenced. Mr. Ham
mond is not satisfied to take from the Rand
mines 4§ millions a year. He points out how,
under “good government’’—like Kimberley—
the mines can be made to yield a further
dividend of 6s. per ton, or a total in-
creased dividend, according to Hammond’s
reckoning of probable output, of £4,826,535.
Mr. Hammond assumes that, under British
rule, labor will be plentiful and cheap.
Y\ hen British civilisation is forced upon
the Boers, British sweating is to acoom-
pany it. While Australians are taxed to pay
the cost of the Transvaal Contingents, the Rand
syndicates are to gain by the war nearly five
millions a year additional in dividends. And
the Australian Labor Parties will have the
proud satisfaction of knowing that their mili-
tary enthusiasm has signally aided to reduce
the wages of their comrades overseas.

More Widow.
The N.S.W. Government being short of money,
and having pledged itself not to increase
customs taxation, and not caring to go out of
its groove and raise coin by means of the tote,
or by a bicycle-tax, or by any other unusual
device, has fallen back on the handy expedient
of increased probate duties. The Victorian
schedule of duties has been adopted whole, and
in the main, and so far as the taxes on large
estates are concerned, there isn’t much in the
Victorian schedule that can fairly be objected
to. As regards the taxation on small estates,
the arrangement is objectionable all round.
Estates of less value than £lOOO go free as
heretofore, but between £lOOO and £5OOO the
taxation is doubled, and now stands at
2 per cent. Where the person who
inherits the goods and chattels of the
deceased is a male adult relative, or
almost any kind of relative except his widow,
there is no particular reason why it shouldn’t
stand at 2 ner cent, or even more. But in the


case of the widow a tax of 2 per cent, on an
estate of less than ,£5OOO is very like highway
robbery. The widow isn’t inheriting someone
else’s money as any other relative does ; she is
practically inheriting her own money. She
and her husband, by all equity, were joint
owners of the property, and in fairness it was
all along as much her property as his. The
only financial difference caused by his death is
that she loses whatever income he earned by
his personal exertions, so that she makes, in
the average case, a serious loss. If he earned
.£5OO a year, and in one form or another she
got £l5O of it, then his death means
the loss of about £3OOO of what is
practically her personal property. If she
is left with £2OOO accumulated capital, then
she has lost three-fifths of her property by the
old man’s death. The law which, when a
woman loses her nearest relative and three-
fifths of her property, regards her as a fair
subject for special taxation on what goods and
chattels she has left, is a brutal law. More-
over, it is based on a wholly erroneous theory.
Where the old man’s pile goes to a nephew or
other outsider, that individual is really coming
into unexpected wealth, and if the State takes
part of it and leaves him the rest he is still a
gainer. The widow is merely retaining part
of w'hat (on the principle that man and wife
are one) was always her own, and to tax her as
a person who is inheriting wealth and suddenly
coming into property is an absurdity. And
when a woman with probably no knowledge of
business, and no capacity for turning her little
pile into a larger one, is left with, say, £l5OO, it
is only a safe income of about £6O a year at best.
Is a bereaved, elderly female with less than
255. a week the sort of bloated capitalist that
direct taxation was intended to fall upon ? Of
course she is rich when compared with the
bereaved elderly female who has nothing a
week and goes out washing ; just as the latter
is wildly affluent when compared with the
female who has no money and no legs, so that
she can’t even wash—but that isn’t the ques-
tion. It is positive, not comparative, wealth
that has to be considered.

The Plaintive Cry of the Shopkeepers.
Melb. Age seems to be losing grasp of that
acumen which has so long enabled it to run
with the hare while it hunts with the hounds ;
also, its discriminating capacity for trimming
seems to have been slightly overworked of
late, for a paper mostly gets the letters it wants
and is judged by those it prints. Recent
letters relate mostly to the Factories’ Act
Amending Bill and to the alleged demand for
an inquiry into the working of the present law,
and, while they make up in iteration what they
lack in logic, they are all built on the same
lines, and merely repeat the same old weari-
some stupidities. A couple may be taken as
samples. The first is from the president
of the Vic. Shopkeepers’ Association, who
puts his case in a nutshell when he re-
marks : “ The truth is, this proposed legis-
lation to compel a man to close his
business and remain idle is simply monstrous.”
Further, he enquires how the poor shop-keeper
is to succeed with only five days’ trade and six
days’ expenses ; though how he succeeds now
with six days’ trade and seven days’ expenses
(for rent, taxes and food and clothes go right on
Sunday and week-day, and wages are so-much
a week —not so-much every six days) he never
dreams of explaining. Probably he never
noticed the awful position he has been in all his
life through the law not allowing him to open
on Sunday. In fact, things are even worse than
that, for he doesn’t keep open all night, so he
has in every week about eighty hours’trade and
168 hours’ expenses. This is terrible ! Another
correspondent, a suburban shop-keeper, objects
fiercely to his being compelled to close one half-
day a week “because other people want to go
pleasuring.” He winds up by enquiring “as
long as I pay my rent and 20s. in the pound,
what right has any one to close me up and
make me remain idle and probably go insol-
vent ? ” The ideal is noble, even pathetic ; and
it is pleasing to observe that there is at least
one man in Melbourne who desires to pay 20s.
in the £ as long as he can. But if this gentle-
man and the other letter-writer would take a
few hours off on one of those half-holidays and
do a little plain thinking—provided they have
the needful plant and implements—they would
see that a compulsory half-holiday a week is
not only not a loss but is an actual gain to
them, provided all shops in competition with
them are also closed. For the volume of trade
in the particular suburb or city or province is
the same whether the shops close for half-a-day.
a week or not, and on the other hand
when the shop is shut the gas-meter isn’t
working, and the dust isn’t getting among
the goods, and the customers aren’t wearing
the floor out, and the assistant isn’t wearing
the varnish off the infrequent seat through too
much sitting on it, also the woman who always
helps herself to a handful of biscuits has to
stay at home, and the small boy who sneaks
peanuts from the bag at the door is tem-
porarily disengaged. The short and simple
case is this : The average person has to
spend all the money he has in order to live,
and as he will still continue to live under early-
closing and one half-holiday a week he will
still spend all he has. And he wouldn’t spend
any more if the shops kept open seven days a
week and 24 hours a day—for he hasn’t got it
to spend. But when a man has spent long
years considering the beauties of a half-penny
a pound profit here, and a half-ounce short
weight there, and all the other small mean
things that go to make that great entity known
as trade, it is difficult to get him to view any
new idea in a clear light.
Kiplingesque War.
Long courses of Ritdyard Kipling have created
in the English mind a .conception of war and
the part played therein by Tommy Atkins,
utterly at variance with facts. Poems and tales,
based wholly upon English experiences in
fighting savages, have taught the slaughter-
loving section of the English public to expect a
British regiment to “ disperse” any enemy that
stands up to it within the hours provided by
the Early-Closing Act, and come home comfort
ably to tea. The modern Major-General must
live up to this ideal or perish, so far as his
status as a provider of sensational copy is con-
cerned, and if he doesn’t stand well there he had
better be out of service. All the Generals,
irom the beginning, have been playing to. the
gallery. The idea that there was any opposition
in front worth reckoning has not, before this
last week or two, struck any of them.
Their business was not merely to win—
that was a task too ridiculously easy to
worry over—but to win a la Kipling to
take the Africander position before break-
fast —to march serenely like Kiplingesque
demi-gods through the entrenchments of the
enemy. Methuen’s and Buller’s attacks were
made in front, in daylight, seemingly without
reconnaissance, apparently without pre-
caution against ambush or surprise, and with
the fixed idea that the enemy stand in the same
intellectual relationship to themselves as a six-
tooth wether to a Supreme Court Judge. The
business of the enemy was to act after his kind
—i.e., after the kind of Kipling’s enemy, the
enemy depicted in Kipling’s military tales —
and disperse before the superior genius, reso-
lution, and equipment of the British. As to
strategy, why should a British general stra-
tegise? White attempted that at Nicholson’s
Nek, and fell in. If he had only been content
to stick to the tactics which press and public
(instructed by Kipling) demand, the successes
at Elandslaagte and Glencoe might have been
continued indefinitely. The methods of the
British leaders must obviously be short, swift,
and business-like. Kipling has explicitly laid
down the plan and specifications to which
British generalship is expected to conform.
A battle between Briton and Boer must satisfy
the expectations of the nation, trained for ten
years in the Kipling school, or it had better
not take place. “ Up then comes the regi-
ment, and pokes the ’eathen out” was the ideal
set up by the Jingo bard, and the regiments
were accordingly set to the task of realising it.

Labor-Members and Ministers.
Labor-leader Batchelor’s inclusion in the
newest S. Aus. Ministry may mean a lot. It
is the first time an Australian Labor-party
has allowed one of its members to join a Minis-
try. Hitherto Labor-parties have refused to
sanction anything of the sort on the ground
that if a Labor-member were a Minister “ the
caucus ” would most likely call in vain upon
that member to throw his colleagues and him-
self out upon the cold, hard Opposition benches;
it was forgotten that if a Labor man or two
were in a Ministry it would be less likely to
need throwing out. Labor-parties were, how-
ever, formed on the idea that there was no good
outside themselves—that both the old parties
were incorrigibly bad, and that nothing could
be got out of them that was not squeezed or
dragged. Experience has since thrown doubt
on that plan, which has yielded a fine crop of
promises, and little else. Besides, Laborites
themselves have been getting very sick of it,
because it has cooped them up and kept
them private members. The old argument was
that they should wait for the Labor Ministry.
But a Labor-party’s Ministry seems out of the
question; anyhow, the Labor-party has no-
where gone ahead fast enough to bring a
majority in sight. Also, by the time its prin-
ciples are supported by a majority, the chances
are that the most Radical constituencies will
want a more advanced programme, and will
call for a new party ; in fact, they are calling
for one already. So far, then, Laborites have
only been able to get into Ministries by seced-
ing ; and secessions have naturally outnum-
bered the Ministerial appointments. It is a
striking fact that of the big batch of N.S.W.
Labor members returned in ’92 only three are
Labor-members now. Those who have not
seceded have been displaced by Liberal or
Radical members; for many Liberal con-
stituencies have not been willing to keep
a Conservative crowd in office on the
off-chance of the Labor-party being able to
squeeze out concessions from the crowd. The
S. A. Labor Party proposes to now try the
plan of making the Liberal party more Liberal
by joining forces with it. There is sense in
that. You may win the Melb. Cup with a cart
mare guaranteed to go 8 miles an hour, and you
may also win it with a draught horse
guaranteed to go 6 miles an hour. The old
idea of the Labor party was to buy a whip and
flog the nags indiscriminately. The new idea
is to mix the old cart mare’s blood with some
of a speedier sort ; and the Bulletin reckons
the party has a better chance to win with the
progeny than with the old draught horse no
matter how hard he may be walloped.

Gaol as a Sanatorium.
Victoria’s new Ministry, which was to show
such splendid administrative ability, has lost
no time in following at least one bad example
set by its predecessors. In his Bairnsdale
speech Premier M'Lean promised that, pend-
ing the establishment of Old Age Pensions, the
poor and the sick would at least be temporarily
relieved. It will be remembered that towards
the end of last session Turner, in the House,
promised that in future the old, the poor and
the sick should be assisted somehow, by magis-
trates’ order, and should at least not be
gaoled; since then not only have the sick
and the poor been repeatedly sent to prison,
but time after time prisoners discharged
from Pentridge, after serving their term,
have been sent back for three or six months,
as the unspeakable J.P. thought fit , for the sole,
crime of ill-health. Hitherto six months has
been the longest term awarded, but recently
the Coburg Bench started in to break the
record and establish a precedent. On Oct. 28
a young woman named Malena Howell was
discharged from Pentridge stockade. Being un-
well and feeble she was seized- by a policeman
as she went out, and the disgraceful Coburg
honorariums actually sent her back to the
cold cells and the hominy for afurther 12 months.
She was charged with having no! lawful means
of support, but, as the wages paid her during
her sojourn in gaol were not extravagant, and
as the practice of thrift while under lock and
key is hardly calculated to produce a compet-
ence, such a charge wus a farcical absurdity.
Her real crime was being ill; that is all. The
brutality of this sentence appears to have stag-
gered even ttie departmental officials, as the
Under-Treasurer called for a report on the
matter. Said report from the police and the
Stockade medical officer indicated that the un-
fortunate woman was not a fit subject for any
charitable institution, as she had been placed in
one previously and had escaped ; therefore
she had better remain in prison. The fact that
she had thus escaped would seem to suggest
that the conduct of the institution, rather than
that of the woman, should be inquired into;
but, apart from that, is it already a crime to
refuse charity in Victoria? The idea of leaving
a sick woman in gaol for 12 months merely
because she once quitted a charitable institu-
tion in au informal sort of way, and refused to
be a burden on the community any longer than
she could help, suggests that the refusal of
public aid is a very awful sin indeed.

Quite a squad of M.L. politicians who lost
their seats in the partial Liberal slump of 1896,
have been returned at the late General Election.
Among others, appears on the scene again W.
W. Collins, freethought lecturer, said to have
been rejected in Christchurch through the
raking up of a very plain ancient public utter-
ance of his anent Her Gracious. Also,
diminutive Hee Hem Smith, alias “Ironsand,”
the lamplighter, is up again from Taranaki.
The Radical Scotch constituency of Dunedin,
after lapsing from grace in 1896, has returned to
the Liberal fold and elected three Ministerialists.
In 1896, Dunedin took to her inmost bosom
Soobie Mackenzie, the Tory funny man ; and
Scobie was under the grand illusion in 1899
that that bosom was still his for the asking.
“ Dismal Bill ” Rolleston, the heavy father
of the M.L. House of Reps., has fallen before a
politician of less calibre and reputation, but on
the popular side of politics. Rolleston was
second-in-command of the Tory party, and his
fall is a notable event. The left-wing band of
guerillas, which has been scotched by the dis-
missal of Tommy Taylor, Invercargill Kelly,
and that awesome wobbler, young Mont-
gomery, never will be missed,except by specials
hard up for copy, who could safely rely on one
“scene” a week emanating from the corner.
The wing crystallised in the session of 1896
under the leadership of G. W. Russell, as the
Radical left of the Liberal party, and was then
a force to be reckoned with. The general elec-
tion which ensued, however, cut out several of
the free-lances, including “ Ricketty ” Russell
himself. What remained of the party, under
the leadership of political-mosquito Pirani,
ran off its original track and rapidly evolved
inLo an Opposition skirmishing corps, diluted
with Cold Water. As such the Wing became
a ghastly nuisance, a fact which the M.L. free
and independent have evidently realised. The
Corner is now reduced to Pjrani and ex-Labor-
member John Hutchison, who got in on the
Tory ticket. “ Ricketty ” Russell, by the
way, long ago renounced the Wing and all its
ways, and has just successfully contested the
Riccarton seat as a straight Ministerialist.

“ Malle ” on coupons :
Your correspondent" Cockney” (25/11/’99) endeavors to
bolster up one of the worst of commercial vultures—
couponism. Instead of the big shops trying to get this
curse blocked by law, they would gladly have an army of
small boys to whoop it up, for nothing could suit then-
book better. Let’s vivisect it. The trader pays the
coupon fiend 12s. 6d. per thousand for the coupons, and
gives one away with every sixpennyworth of goods sold.
When the shopper has laboriously collected, say, 500
coupons he, “or principally she,” trots them round to
the coupon establishment and obtains an article, probably
some piece of tawdry electroware that she does not want,
and which she would never dream of buying for straight-
out cash. The shopkeeper gives a five per cent, discount,
but out of this comes all the coupon harpy’s printing,
advertising, canvassing, rent, and living expenses, so
that the public only gets about one-fifth of what the
retailer pays. The small men are constantly crying that
they cannot compete with the big shops, and many of
them disappear through this yearly, How on earth then
can they give away five per cent.—not of their profits,
mark, but of their gross turnover—and have a hope in
such competition ? A shopkeeper who would reduce his
prices five per cent., the full advantage of which would
be reaped by the public, or who gave his own coupons
redeemable for cash or goods on production of a specified
number, thus securing a mortgage over puchaser’s
custom, might succeed. At present the non-producing,
blood-sucking coupon promoter is the only person bene-
fited. The retailer is bled of five per cent, of his gross
takings, and tries to get it back by squeezing his em-
ployes, fining his miserably underpaid shopgirls, selling
inferior goods, &c., and all the time sees himself drift
nearer to the insatiable maw of the “universal providers.” .
No doubt the system is popular with a section of the
public, which is mostly an ass, and fancies it’s getting
something for nothing, but the coupon promoter is only
a commercial vampire, and the sooner the law squashes
him—as it has done in America—the better.

“Gumleaf,” writes:—
The disparity of sentences in the eriminal courts of
Australia is a growing scandal, It is not solely a matter
of different Judges having different ideas of what
constitutes adequate punishment. That is pnly one of
the causes of the scandal. I have had personal experience,
as a newspaper man, of the criminal courts of four of
these provinces, and I have seen the same Judge on
different occasions dealing with the same offence, under
exactly similar circumstances, and passing sentences so
diverse that he couldn’t account for the difference him-
self. There cannot be two opinions therefore, that much
of the trouble arises through the varying temper and
health of the Judge at the time of passing sentence.
An illustration of this kind was furnished by Justice
a’Beckett in the Vic. Criminal Court the other day.
He had been showing signs of bad health and worse
temper throughout the sittings, and this culminated in
his making a savage attack upon a prisoner,, found guilty
of perjury of a comparatively harmless kind—to defeat
one of the silliest provisions of the Licensing Act—and
sentencing him to five years’ imprisonment. I would
suggest that every Judge who presides in criminal juris-
diction should be medically examined at intervals,
with a view of ascertaining whether he is in a fit
mental and bodily state of health to exercise
judicial powers in a reasonable manner. It should
also be necessary for every man appointed to a Judge-
ship to undergo an annual medical examination in the
matter of his sanity. I am quite positive that there are
men on the Australian Bench to-day who do not come up
to the ordinary standard of sanity. They are not
lunatics ; far from that, but they are certainly fanatics*
No religious or teetotal zealot should be tolerated on the
Bench, for it is a clear indication that his mind is subject
to fits of hysteria. I have seen men of this stamp
sentencing prisoners while their faces were aflush With
anger, and a violent passion-quiver shook their voices.
There is one Judge now on the Bench who fairly spits his
passionate words at prisoners through his thin lips, while
his eyes glitter with a maniacal glare. It would be in-
teresting to find out how many criminals these '-•* v iust
and unequal sentencers manufacture yearly.

The M‘Lean Ministry has already begui/ to
go to pieces. It got a bad set back, as a coun-
try Government, by the defeat of M*Cay at
Castlemaine by a substantial majority. Then
the internal strife which was foreseen from the
first by anyone with eyes has, it appears, been
raging for several days past. A third and
surer sign of the Ministry’s decomposition is
the fact that Fink, who joined the Government
as an honorary Minister with palpable pleasure
and pride, and immediately had his name
printed in large gold letters on one of tlie
Treasury doors, has deserted it. His ostensible
reason for doing so is that he disapproves pf
Shiels’s speech at Bairnsdale and his dual con-
trol in the Government. This excuse is far too
.thin, when it is remembered that in the letters
which passed between M‘Lkan and Shiels,
and in which Fink’s name was mentioned,
the dual control was provided for. The truth
is that Fink is a political, turncoat of the worst
description, and believes in early desert-
ing a born - dead Ministry. His seat at
Jolimont is most insecure, and it is only by
barking with the biggest dog that he has a
chance of getting into the next Parliament.
The Conservatives led by Gillies, however,
are sure to make a big fight to keep the pre-
sent Ministry afloat, and the reason is plain to
see. If they accomplish this object they have
a better chance of getting the province so
divided into Federal electorates as to provide
safe pocket constituencies for some of the more
prominent members of their party. Gillies
himself, Murray Smith, Irvine, Johm M‘ln-
tyre, and others are all after seats in the first
Federal Parliament.

So far as N.S.W. is copcerned, there is a
mighty difference between the second Trans-
vaal contingent and the first. Despite the
“unparalleled enthusiasm” which the local
anti-Australian papers rave about, only about
1000 men volunteered to take 4s. 6d. a day to
go and fight Boers ; and this small number
included a lot of weedy specimens whom even
the relaxed-to-suit-circumstances medical ex-
amination could not pass. If the medical,
shooting, and riding standards had been fully
maintained, not 300 men would have
passed muster; and 450 were wanted.
Yet every possible pressure was brought
to bear on the militia; and the police
force was temporarily crippled so that
the number promised should be made up. As
a matter of fact, the War Office is being cheated
over “Australian enthusiasm” just as it was
cheated over the Lancers’ enthusiasm. The
men who go, go chiefly for the fun and the
change. As they are paid 4s. 6d. a day, they
are not volunteers but mercenaries ; and just
as many eould be got on the same terms to join
in any foreign fight that Parliament chose td
back up. “ Loyalty ”is natural enough in the'
English settled here ; but “Australian loyalty
to England” is all flam, though “ Australian
disloyalty” is carefully suppressed by the
newspapers. Australians are loyal to Australia
—to the land they live in; and their loyalty
stops right there.
Justice A’Beckett, hitherto regarded as an
absolutely sensible Judge, rushed off oh an un-
warrantable side-path in Vic. Criminal Court,
the other day. A man was charged with a grosg
crime—indecently assaulting a little girl BJ.
years of age. The Crown case was none too,
strong, and the defence suggested that the
motive for laying the charge was blackmail on;
the part of a relative of the girl, who was said
to be an immoral woman. Altogether there
was considerable doubt about the case. The;
accused did himself no good by the manner in
which he talked all round the compass, and :
when the jury returned with a verdict of not
guilty he made an exhibition of himself .by cry-,
ing and hysterically thanking the jury. The;
Judge promptly told him that it was in-
decent to thank juries for doing their duty,'
which was sensible enough, but hfe went
further. He evidently considered the man;
guilty, and allowiug his feelings to get
the better of him, he overstepped the,
bounds of justice and propriety. Speaking,
with considerable warmth, he said he inter-
preted the verdict to mean that the jury be-
lieved he committed the crime, although there;
was not sufficient evidence to prove it. To
this the lickspittle foreman of course remarked,
“ Quite right, your honor.” Then, turning to,
the accused, a’Beckett said, P Leave the court
at once —you are disgraced but not convicted.”
Surely in the eyes of folk with a sense of com-
mon honesty the accused was not the only per-
son who stood disgraced in connection with the.
incident ! If Vic. Parliament approves of this
sort of thing on the part of judges, it should at
once reform the law by giving juries the alter-
native of finding a verdict of Not Proven.

Apparently the Lyne Government is still
half inclined to build the principal Syd-‘
ney railway station at Devonshire-street, where
it will be about as far away from the business
centre as it is now, with an apologetic little
“ extension ” into the city. Fancy the second
city in Australasia, and the fourth in the southern
hemisphere, being served by an “extension.” If
all the trains are to come into the city, then the
great terminus wants to be there to give them
room. If only a few are to come in, then the rest
of the passengers will have the same old grievance
as at present, and the row about the need for a
big city station will start over again in a few
