The improved edition of Sydney Royal
re-opened on Christinas Eve under Williamson’s
management with “The Only Way’’-which is
Freeman Wills version of Dickens’ “ Tale of Two
L/ities. It has always been a recognised fact that
Dickens doesn t dramatise well, but it can at
least be said for Freeman Wills’ production that
it. is quite as successful as could be expected.
Dickens had a leisurely method of developing his
p ots, which is unsuited to the hurry of the stage,
where the whole tale has to be told in three hours;
em Pl°y ec J a vast array of characters who
fitted so accurately into his story that it suffers
when some of them have to be left out to give
breathing room _to the others. Jerry Cruncher,
the resurrectionist, and his wife (who flopped in
prayer) and son are omitted as a matter of course.
Ihe Vengeance, with her knitted registers, is a
mere shadow who howls a brief but convincing
w m Act 111. ; Miss Julia Merton’s Vengeance
is a Vigorous character while she lasts, but she
lasts a very short time. John Barsad is only a
casual mention, and his sister is dropped altogether.
And there isn t time for the prisoner of the Bas-
tille to relapse into periodical idiocy and start
shoemaking so he is of necessity a rather
undeveloped martyr. But the essentials of the
story are preserved. It goes rather slowly at
hrst, and the act which is mainly devoted to Syd-
ney Carton being drunk hangs fire in a good
many places. Thomas Kingston takes the part
of Carton, and he presents hardly a sufficient
resemblance to Harry Hill, who fills the green
coat of Darnay, to account for things. AlsoT the
back view of Darnay in his green coat and tight
green pants and long hair isn’t artistic, and when
Cecil Ward, as a slimy and deceitful man in a
blue coat and tight pants, lays a wicked trap for
him the public sympathies are rather with the
bad blue man than with the good green one. The
strength of the drama lies in the last two acts,
which go strongly enough to redeem previous
deficiencies. The revolutionary tribunal is a loud
and energetic court of justice, where everybody in
general constitutes the jury, and where the jury
rushes at the prisoner every now and then with
a U j e ," s ? 11( * threatens to brain him on the spot.
i n 11 , W^iere the frivolous and
beautifully-upholstered noblesse of France trot off
cheerfully to the guillotine, is a picturesque piece
ot work though there is a certain monotony about
the yell of the invisible populace outside. The
populace seems to have no preferences, and the
ory of execration it • discharges at prisoner No.
41 is exactly the same as the one it discharges at
every number up to 47.
,* * .
There is comparatively little room for
scenic effects except in Act 11., where Goatcher
has surpassed himself, and in the final tableau,
where Carton’s dark figure looms up on the
guillotine against a reddish background that sug-
gests the burning of Moscow. The rest of the
glory goes in costumes, which are done with an
elaborate and beautiful detail. A part of the
company is reluctantly held over till next week.
But The Bulletin desires to mention hurriedly
that Thomas Kingston, though weighed down at
first by the slowness of Act 1., is very satisfactory
in some later scenes, and plays his sombre character
hke a real artist. Also, Miss Rose Musgrove’s
Mimi is a nice good girl ; and Miss Linda Ray-
mond is a pretty and pleasing though slightly
monotonous Lucie Manette. David Glassford,
Havvtrey and Cecil Ward leave a good impression
on the writer’s vast intellect; Hawtrey’s little
sketch of Stryver, the hard, pompous, bounceable
lawyer, is really good, while Cecil Ward, as a dark
bitter vengeful oily man in blue clothes, is about
the strongest feature in the cast. Ward has the
bottled-up vengeance of 18 years in his eye, and
his concentrated and apologetic malevolence is an
interesting study. A passing glimpse of Oily
Deenng as the bloated prosecutor creates a desire
to see more of him ; also many glimpses of the
vague.and sketchy Harry Hill make the writer
feel that he would be quite satisfied if he saw a
good deal less of him.
** * *
The “Little Red Riding Hood” panto-
mime was due at Her Majesty’s last Tuesday, but
the exigencies of a paper which goes to press on
that evening make it impossible to describe the
beauty and pomp and glory thereof until next-
issue. The literary part of the work is by Mr.
Bernard Espinasse, an Australian dramatist of
good repute in the past and with promise of much
freater repute in the future, and his name should
e a guarantee that the book at least will be all
right.
*# * *
Sydney Lyceum started a premature
Christmas on Saturday night with a great and
irresponsible production, entitled “The Power
and the Glory,” by Charles Darrell. It hinges
mainly on the supposed profitable business of the
burglar ; but the cracksman’s trade is apparently
not all that it is cracked up to be. But apart
from its aggressive morality, the piece is capitally
adapted to the needs of the Christmas season. It
is low comedy, farcical comedy, drawing-room
comedy, melodrama, patriotic drama, plain
insanity, variety show, music-hall entertainment,
and largely pantomime. It is, moreover, highly
moral. When Ferdinand Crayle, the “ King of
the Mobsmen,” attempts to hurl the virtuous
heroine off the top of the Monument to bottomless
P erc *iti° n > there to dwell in unrecognisable smash,
the heavens open, and Ferdinand sinks in a
heap of horror at the vision of a blind child sailing
aloft on a cloud. This is the cheerful side of the
play; the gloom comes in whenever a low-comedy
proprietress of a coffee-stall, a lowly baker, and a
stage-struck purveyor of lambs’-frys appear upon
the scene to make music for those whom Svengali
was wont to call ze pig-dog.
* * * *
“ The Power and the Glory ” affords little
scope for acting. Miss Ida Gresham as the wife
of a sentimental and aristocratic burglar enlists
sympathy; and Miss Beatrice Holloway, the same
burglar s blind daughter, deserves some praise.
Ihe sentimental burglar’s wife is a poor and de-
serted, but flawless, woman of humble connec-
tions;but the blind daughter is a childish im-
possibility and a perpetual nerve-irritant until she
is taken to the angels in a thunderstorm over St.
Jrauls Cathedral, on a “property” cloud with
hook and wire attachment. The sentimental
cracksman is portrayed by Mr. W. J. Mont-
gomery m a strong and painstaking manner, but
tne honors fall easily to Lawrence Dunbar, as
Ferdinand Crayle, “King of the Mobs-
men. _ The third burglar is an honest, hard-
working*, horny-handed Bill-Sikes, with a pro-
fessional jaw, and muscles hardened by well-
deserved tread-mill exercise. The hero of the
piece, a large and important British person in a
Jack iars uniform, is undertaken by Mr. Charles
Holloway, in whose personality the United King-
dom and its Dependencies are nobly supervised,
and fought for, and bragged about generally,
ihis representative of might has a low-comedy
mother, who keeps an open-air restaurant on
wheels ; a sister who gathers the roses of wicked-
ness as a tight-wire-walking demi-mondaine; and
another sister who is a spotless flower-girl,
pursued by the attentions of a gushing Johnny of
high degree, a weary comic baker, and a stage-
struck butcher whose mother was a gipsy countess
and whose father was a troubadour Moravian.
As the sailor’s “Mar,” Miss Roland Watts-
Jrhillips is great, but sacrificed to the sordid
demands of the coffee-stall; Miss Roxy Barton
gives a striking impersonation of the dashing
elder daughter, and Miss Alice Deorwyn is the
seller of faded flowers. The scenery is glorious,
but “ the Summit of the Monument,” with sleep-
ing London spread beneath, is the gem of the
collection, and on the opening night the audience
fetched artist Clint out about six times to tell him
so at great length.
** * *
“A Tale of the Transvaal,” staged at
Sydney Criterion on Saturday, is partly a revival
of Rider Haggard’s old story of “ Jess ” and partly
a new “Jess,” brought up to date regardless of
expense and anachronism. The Boer war of ’Bl
and the ditto of 09 are mixed together, in defiance
ac * :s an d dates and circumstances. The
characters belong to the war of ’Bl, but the Aus-
tralian contingent, which rushes in and deals out
large and embittered stoush to the hardened
Protestants of the Transvaal, belongs distinctly
to ’99. And when Mr. Lewis Scott, who is
responsible for the trouble, represents the British
soldiers as falling back in disorder until Australian
reinforcements turn the scale—the Britisher is
evidently no good by himself, but the Australian
reinforcements simply stamp the Boer flat—he is
playing to the gallery in lamentable claptrap.
Ihe Bulletin begins to foresee a time when an
Australian history of the Transvaal will be written,
showing how the war of ’Bl panned out badly
because the Australians weren’t there, and how
they saved the sinking empire in 1900, just as Bill
Adams saved it at Waterloo. It will be some-
thing like a certain Spanish account of the Penin-
sular war, wherein Cuesta and Castanos drove the
French over the border, though hampered a good
deal by a foolish Irish person named Wellington,
Set * n roac * something that he
c f led his troops just when Castanos was about to
charge and wipe the French off the face of the
globe m fact, Castanos’ most brilliant charges
were mostly spoiled by his anxiety not to tramp
on Wellington, which well-meaning but unprac-
tical individual was always in between the two
armies and letting off yells at the wrong moment
• * * *
Kruger, who was little known in ’Bl, is
also dragged largely into the drama by way of
bringing it up to date, and the author has gratified
public sentiment by making him a mean, fat, un-
warlike comedy-hypocrite of the Nonconfor-
mist variety. Taking it all round, it is a
m j Xec * and rather awful production, partly
redeemed by some fine scenic effects and war
tablea,u. Harry Diver, not being sufficiently
capacious in the lower chest for a stage Boer,
represents Englishman John Neil ; and Miss
Dunckley and Miss Hope Mayne look nice and
fresh and pretty as the two English girls, Jess
and Bess. Certain large fat unwashed Boers
and Boeresses, who will doubtless be officially
bathed by the British Government when the
present regrettable armed coolness is over, await
consideration at a more favorable moment.
** * *
At Sydney Palace G. W. Hunter, the
comedian with the short coat and the baggv pants
raconteurs nightly to a crowded house. The gap
left by Tom Costello is filled by Professor Davy’s
life-sized marionettes, a gorgeous ballet, little
t army Powers, and the Newsky Family of alleged
Russian _ vocalists and dancers. Unfortunately,
when this paper got round, the crowd had slopped
over into the street, and the great organ of public
opinion solaced itself with mixed drinks and the
fact that it would give full particulars next week.
*# * *
At Sydney Opera House (the King-street
ancient playhouse), a diorama of the Boer war is
raging, to the accompaniment of much martial
music. The sanguinary display is preceded by an
inoffensive - looking individual, named Drissco,
who, among other things, beats variations of
Home, Sweet Home ” out of a collection of
empty whisky bottles. When the last tinkle of
the ancient refrain has died away the diorama
begins. The best picture is that of the Gay
Gordons carrying Elands Laagte by storm. It
shows a crowd of unkempt-looking Boers working
rifles and quick-firers for dear life, while up the ridge
sweeps a wave of kilted Scots. One big High-
lander, mortally wounded, seems to spring right
out of the canvas; another has sprung in and
bayoneted a Boer; while on the right, away down
the line of tartan, looming through the smoke, is
the figure of the officer waving his men on. While
the pictures are being shown several people sing
moreor-less national songs. Afterwards, one
Salma, dressed as a lady, kicks the back-hair of
his own blonde wig. The last item is jugglery, by
Miss Vidi Providi, a little lady in yellow satin,
who juggles not at all badly.
** * *
Sydney Royal Standard re-opened after
all, contrary to this paper’s hopes and expecta-
tions, with the appalling “Kelly Gang.” The
‘ Kelly Gang ” is bad enough at ordinary seasons,
but in hot weather the sight of Ned Kelly frying
in his armour is too painful to be further men-
tioned.
** * *
“ Little Christopher Columbus ” started at
Melb. Royal last Saturday week, the wind in its
favor. The show is a large good-looking venture
launched on the holiday market at popular prices ;
also, it is a novelty in Melb., and it brings two old
favorites back to notice at a time when the
Princess has gone to sleep, therefore Little
Christopher should pile up riches on earth for the
Royal management, and gather reputation from
century to oentury. This is the sort of opera that
must either boom or perish. The jokes are mostly
puns, the quaint music is not built to catch the
common or garden ear, and the story amounts to
something resembling a Gaiety burlesque strength-
ened with an infusion of Widdy O’Brien. Inasmuch
as little Christopher depends on the performers
for its humor, the performers need full houses to
cheer them on their way. Fortunately they are
getting a liberal supply of the needful. Bridget
O Brien has married a millionaire named Block
during her five years’ absence from Aus-
tralia, and the result of their union is a
bright new daughter (Miss Denny) who
loves a cabin-boy. Miss Gracie Whiteford,
rounder and more effective every way than the
former Gracie, plays the Cabin Boy in several
changes of costume, and is a fetching little hero all
the time ; but she may be earnestly implored to
substitute a new ballad for the antique coon-song
which she sings in the second act. Detective
O Hooligan, played by Fred Sinclair, is about the
most frequent item in the tragedy, and first im-
pressions of this attenuated stranger are highly
favorable. Sinclair, who appears to be a
very young man, or a rather elderly youth,
reproduces the methods of lank Teddy Lonnen,
the original O’Hooligan in London. He is
weaker than Lonnen in singing and business
details, but he sometimes is funny without any
effort. In fact, Fred Sinclair follows close upon
the heels of Bert Gilbert, who also derived his
mannerisms from the laborious Lonnen. There
are two or three other acceptable new arrivals in
the . Sheridan co., and the. ballet, the chorus, and
the fixings in general have been arranged with a
reasonable regard for splendor,
** * *
At the Bijou the first part of the Rickards
show now happens amid a nice new “sylvan”
scene with a fountain thrown in. The ballads in
a first part usually make some mention of a foun-
tain a silver fountain, as a rule—that ripples
down the moonlit stream in the sweet golden
sunset, but this is the first time that Rickards has
conferred the favor of an actual dripping fountain
on the Melbourne stage, as far as The Bulletin
can remember. Good old Wallace King warbles
by the fountain in his good old winning way; also
basso Hahn is there ; furthermore Little Alma
Gray reappears after a long absence. The Xmas
bill at Rickards’comes fully up to requirements,
and even if it didn’t the house would be crowded
at present, all the same. Tom Costello was to re-
appear on Boxing Night, weather permitting.
Tom Logan, from America, took the floor last
Saturday, and is at present under earnest
consideration.
** * *
For the third week in succession here
cometh the announcement that “Les Cloches”
will clang at the Alexandra for a holiday season,
dating from Boxing Night. In a time of dearth
of ordinary theatrical news, it is permissible to
hammer away at a rather small fact until you have
flattened it out to look like a very large one.
Next week the true character of the Vic. Operatic
Society’s performance will be reverently laid bare.
A show that begins on a Tuesday night gets nine
days start of this Jingo newspaper.

M‘Adoo’s Jubilee Singers, supported by a
“special representation of the Ober-Ammergau
Passion Play,” fell due at Melb. Town Hall on
Boxing Night, and were no doubt uttering their
quaint soothing harmonies to a mighty big
attendance when The Bulletin went to press.
A Waxworks and other matters have started or
are about to start business at St. George’s Hall,
and the conjecture that something will happen at
the Exhibition Building (where the Aquarium is)
on the first night of the old century’s last year is
confidently hazarded herewith.
At Poverty Point.
“ Melb.” : Deadheadery had a glorious show over Mar-
shall-Hall's Conservatorium Concert at the Alexandra
the other night. Everybody came on the nod, and there
was far more slavering humility than in the Beauchamp
case. The tickets given away were, respectively, for
dress-circle, stalls, pit, and gallery, according to Respec-
tability or the layers of the Crust in the Social Pie.
Marshall-Hall, in his determination to boom, gave away
the house about twice over. The droves of deadheads
along Exhibition-street were a sight to be remembered.
Marshall-Hall s girls rendered opera selections in cos-
tume. The deadheads were decidedly flat and fishy, as
they always are. Floral tributes were ostentatiously
fetched in at the stage-door, and handed up at the wrong
psychological moment. Greatest mistake in the world to
play to the supercilious and contemptuous deadhead.
“ S.S. ’: The charity performance at Melb. Princess on
Dec. 16 served to illustrate the terrible butnorlessness of
a swagger “ push ” in Melbourne, and the stupid intoler-
ance of the “ great dailies.” Will Whitburn got off some
harmless little wheeze about the Scotchmen at Majuba
being asked a shilling a pint for beer, and succumbing to
the charge. This outraged the feelings of the solemnly
jingoistic audience, and it treated the performer much as
a i^ e ven utterance to some flaming blasphemy.
Also next day the idiot press took him severely to task
for his “egregious outrage on good taste in making war
incidents a subject for poor jesting.” This after two
years of jibing at the Boer! But that was another
matter—the Britisher considers himself beyond a joke.
“S.S.”: This last season of the Broughs in Melbourne
convinces me that Robert Brough is not in his real
element in farcical comedy. Brough got much praise for
his work in plays like “The Magistrate,” but he was
almost invariably Poskett in farces of that stamp—the
same Poskett every time. lie is at his best in character
work and superior comedy, and nobody wants to see him
again doing rubbish like “The Brixton Burglary,”where-
in he revived our old friend the magistrate, and ranhim
through a fresh series of trials and tribulations. How
much better his work in “One Summer Day,” “The
Liars,” “Trclawney,” “The Gay Lord,” “Sowing the
W ind —and yet Brough himself was amazed at the
failure of “The Brixton Burglary,” and thought highly
of that new box of old tricks.

Harry Rickards, after long consideration, has decided
to extend his operations to Adelaide. He has secured a
ong lease of the pretty little Bijou Theatre. A gallery
is to be built and other alterations made, and the theatre
opens as a music-hall next Easter.

STRANDED.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN .]
He started off from Sydney with the Scramble-out
Brigade ;
The management was genial, but it hardly ever paid.
They flitted over gullies, and they flitted out of pubs ;
They skidded down the mountains, and they scampered
through the scrubs.
But the road beyond was heavy, over countless miles of
sand—
The road from Bullock Blazes to the Never Never Land.
The man above referred to was a young ambitious chap
Who’d never sounded misery, but lolled in pleasure’s lap.
His future glinted brightly, and his fortune seemed as
made
As he took his first engagement with the Scramble-out
Brigade.
Oh, joy of rushing on to say: “ No, damn me if you do 1”
As the villain tells a woman, “Then, by God, I’ll force
you to 1 ”
It thrilled him at the Gil-ghi Flat, he glowed at Groggy
Creek,
But the scene began to pall on him when done six times a
week.
His artificial fury was a noise, and nothing more,
Which couldn't be convincing even down at Bungendore.
On reaching Jumping Sandhills, where the Mallee hens
are hatched,
They paid him with a promise and they told him he was
“scratched.”
A hundred miles from anywhere the Jumping Sandhills
stand,
And on the road to Nowhere, in the Never Never Land.
He wasn’t only stranded in that distant, barren hell,
Through years of patient agony he ‘ silted up’ as well.
He started trapping rabbits, as a means of livelihood.
And teaching “Little Jim" to kids and cutting billet-
wood.
His hair was grey and scanty when he started back to town
In the blazing days of summer when the wool was going
down,
And lighting out to ‘pad the hoof’ across the waste of
sand,
He cursed the Jumping Sandhills in the Never Never
Land.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Lyne (N.S.W.) Government proposes to
spend £2,016,341 of loan moneys during che present finan-
cial year, so the loan fraud and swindle isn’t growing any
more honest as time goes on. Some of the items are just
as disgraceful as anything ever perpetrated in the
worst days of George Reid. Here are a few samples of the
more or less “ permanent and reproductive” works which
Bill Lyne proposes charging to posterity
Munitions of war .. .. .. .. ..£100,000
Other military expenses .. .. .. ,. 40,550
Thinning forests.. .. .. 10,000
Altering unpaid-for steam trams to electric trams 80,000
do. do. do. .. 65,749
Snagging rivers .. .. „ 13,000
New steamer to replace old one (unpaid for) ... 15,000
Law expenses, &c. (M'Sharry case) ... .. .. 52,500
For Board of Exports .. .. „. .. 100,000
Museums, &e ... .. .. 20,000
Roads, bridges, gaols, &c., &c. .. .. .. 145,000
Repurchase of land, previously sold and proceeds
treated-as revenue 55,500
Every one of these, and many more similar items, are a
gross swindle upon posterity, and another example of
that pitiable cowardice which seems to make it impossible
for the Australian people to honestly face their responsi-
bilities. In particular the £100,000 for ammunition is a
thing which any respectable Ananias might be ashamed
to call “ permanent and reproductive.” It isn’t perman-
ent for it goes off with a bang as soon as the trigger is
touched, and it isn’t reproductive in any sense. Yet
there was hardly a single member honest enough or brave
enough to object to this ghastly item, or to any of the
others like it.
.* » • 9
For the first 5J months of the present financial
year (1st July to 15th December) the Victorian railways
yielded £118,610 more than for the corresponding period
of 1898. There are great hopes, therefore, that this year
will see the Vic. railway deficit lower than it has been for
11 years. In 1886, 1887, and 1889 Vic. railways showed
a handsome profit over working expenses and interest on
capital,' but since then the losses have been on the fol-
lowing cheerful scale:—
1890 .. .. £190,083 I 1895 .. .. £300,445
1891 .. .. 293,032 | 1896 .. .. 493,949
1892 .. .. 379,998 j 1897 .. .. 295,924
1S93 .. .. 283,639 1898 .. ... 374,446
1894 .. .. 292,256 I 1899 .. .. 294,802
Up to 1895 there was very little to choose between the
railway deficits of N.S.W. and Victoria (in fact N.S.W.
showed the worse record as a rule), but since then a
great improvement has set in so far as N.S.W. is con-
cerned, while the over-railwayed province down south
started in 1898 to put up the worst record in its history.
* * * *
The National Bank and the Bank of Victoria
both pay the last instalment of their reconstructed
deposits next March, and thus bring to a close the most
disastrous transaction in their history. Owing to that
truly lamentable re-construction they have, for six years,
been paying 4J per cent, interest on a mass of deposits
which they would otherwise have got for about 3J. Also,
the part of their deposits which was converted into pre-
ference shares has cost them 5 per cent, per annum in
dividends, whereas if there had been no re construction it
would also li we cost only about 31. On a rough estimate
each bank has paid fully £200,1)00 more in interest than it
wou’d have done if there had been no re-construction.
And neither bank had any real need to suspend payment
in ’93—at least, a very small measure of State assist-
ance would have pulled them both through. If the
Patterson Government had had the plain horse-sense
of Dibbs in N.S.W., and rushed a measure through
making bank-notes legal tender, or had authorised an
issue of State legal-tender notes and lent a few millions
of them to the banks, making their repayment a first
charge on the assets—if it bad done anything like this
instead of losing its head and tearing up and down in the
dark, the National and Bank of Victoria would never have
gone under. The ineptitude of the Patterson crowd has
already cost these two institutions fully £200,000 each in
interest ; and probably 1. 1,000,000 in depreciation of
assets, for a vast amount of the depreciation was caused
by the suspensions and the consequent dislocation of
business. Further it will cost them an indefinite amount
in the future by way of dividends on preference capital.
What the Government’s incapacity cost other institutions
and the public generally it is difficult to say. Probably
i. 20,000,000 wouldn’t cover the needless loss, wreckage
and depreciation, all because James Beef Patterson and
Godfrey Carter lost their heads for about a month at a big
crisis.

The National Mutual Life Association (Mel-
bourne) finished, on 30th September, 1899, its third year
since the amalgamation with the Mutual of Victoria. The
following is a condensed record of these three years :—
1897. 1898. 1899.
Premiums .. £303,098 £347,417 £362,787
Interest .. 116,763 132,248 136,966

cent, on total
income .. £17 18 0 £17 15 5 £18 4 5
Interest earned
on funds .. £4 17 2 £4 18 2 £4 15 10
Apart from the unfortunate increase in the ex-
pense rate and the unavoidable fall in interest, the re-
port is a good one all round. The National Mutual
shows a steadily-increasing premium and interest
revenue, a decrease in claims, a larger addition to the
funds every year, and a good rate of interest earned on
funds. Its accumulated assurance fund is now £2,951,502,
so another year will evidently see it well past the three-
million mark.
The statement of assets is a very full and com-
plete document, and there is nothing in it to which
serious objection can be taken, always excepting that
dubious item: “Deferred instalments of annual pre-
miums, 1 50,840.” This is arrived at by assuming that
premiums ought to be paid yearly in advance, and if a
policy-holder pays half-yearly or quarterly, and if his
policy dates from, say, the beginning of June, so that
when the society’s year closes on 30th September he lias
only paid half or quarter of next year’s premium, the
remainder of the year’s premium is putin M a debtdue
to the society and consequently as an asset. uctinff
is an asset, for the society makes a practice the Dohcv
any unpaid balance of the year’s premium from the policy
money in case of death. But it isn’t a tanK'We asset «hi,h
the society has actually in hand, and it sn an, asset
which brings in interest. If it were eft °ut altogether
and written off the investment fluctuation fun ,
make the rate of interest earned on the total funds ap-
preciably better. An item of £50,840 which earns noth ng
reduces the average interest considerably. lhe y
Mutual also used to reckon ‘ Deferred instalments of
annual premiums” as an asset. But last year, t y
decided to abandon the practice and to write off the item
by degrees, and the National Mutual would be well-
advised to do the same.
The only other office in Australasia which
almost exactly corresponds to the National Mutual in
size and age is the State Life Department of Maoriland.
The balance-sheet of the latter is due about three months
hence, and apparently both assurance funds will reach
the ±'3,0110,01)0 limit about the same time. the
absorption of the Mutual of Victoria, however,
has given the National Mutual 'a much larger business
than its rival, and its funds are consequently increasing
more rapidly, so unless there is a big boom in the affairs
of the State Department (which does business in one pro-
vince only), the National Mutual (which draws its busi-
ness from seven provinces, also from England and Cape
Colony) appears bound to get ahead. There are two
points especially worth noting: in the comparison between
these two institutions. The National Mutual’s expenses
absorb over IS per cent, of its income; the Maoriland
State Office only uses a little over 14 per cent, in working
expenses. And at the end of last.year the State Depart-
ment, with a business representing ±279,000 a year in
premiums, had a larger assurance-fund than the National
Mutual with a business represented by £347,000 in pre-
miums. The National Mutual is a good, sound, reliable
office, but in some important respects the Maoriland
State institution appears able to keep ahead of all Aus-
tralian rivals of its size and age.

The Fourth Victoria Permanent Building
Society (Melbourne), whose financial year closed on 31st
October, 1899, shows further signs of recovery. The re-
sults of recent years have been as follows :
1892 .. Profit £9,916 1896 .. Profit £3520
1893 . „ 1,021 1897 .. „ 2912
1894 .. Loss 33,375 1893 .. „ 3353
1895 .. Profit 2,711 1899 .. „ 4214
In 1894 there was a profit on the year’s actual trading
transactions of £2560, but it was in that year that the
lamentable fact dawned on the institution that it had
made a loss of nearly ±36,000 on properties in possession,
and it was found necessary to write off the reserve-fund,
profit balance, and £28,114 capital. Since then the society
has paid four dividends of 2 per cent, per annum each
(the one declared for 1899 absorbs £2022), thereby
accounting for £7932 ; created a new reserve fund
of £4315 ; and applied £4463 to the further writing
down of properties in possession. As the Fourth
Victoria realises over £4 12s. per cent, per annum gross
on the present alleged value of its assets,_ and the per-
centage is increasing, it would seem as if most of the
losses were now provided for, and the institution was get-
ting near the end of its troubles.

The Haymarket Permanent Land, Building
and Investment Co. (Sydney) begins to get its head above
water again in a small way. The last three years’ profits
have been :
1897 ... £452 I 1899 ... £l4Ol
1898 ... 477 |
The co. is so much inflated over this small gleam of pros-
perity that it pays a dividend of 1J per cent, per annum
(±lO9l) —a thing that hasn’t happened to it for a long
time. The remaining £3lO, added to previous balance of
£5963, leaves £6273 to carry forward. The Haymarket
Co. has a reserve of £33,148, contingency fund of £l5OO,
and accumulated profit balance of £6273 —total reserves
£40,921 ; so, on paper at all events, it occupies a very
strong position. But its assets only yield, even with the
present improved earnings, about 3 per cent, per annum
gross. Deposits have been reduced in the past two years
from £61,872 to ± 54,827, and other liabilities are being
steadily wiped off, so this big land-jobbing institution
appears to have better prospects before it, comparatively
speaking. But the dividend of lj per cent, per annum is
a very comparative affair.

really went £5534 to the bad, and the accumulated loss

and it would be interesting to know if the money is to
come out of the directors’ or manager’s remuneration, or
out of the shareholders’dividends, or out of the profits
accruing to the policy-holders ; and in the latter case have
the policy-holders been consulted and what do they say
about it ?

The Australian Mont do Loan and Deposit
Co. (Melbourne) is an institution which shows that even
the pawnhroking business isn’t necessarily too profitable.
Last half-year’s earnings (to 31st October, 1899), after
writing off £5O for wear and tear of office props., were
£893, and an 8 per cent, dividend on the paid-up capital
of ±'21,605 absorbed £864. The concern started business
in 1881, buying up the already-established connection of
one Hyams, and it has just now succeeded at long last in
writing off out of profits the goodwill asset thus created.
It has no reserve fund, and the accumulated profit
balance is only £l6OB. But the most surprising circum-
stance is that the Australian Mont de Pi6t 6 has amort-
gage of £lOOO on its South Melbourne establishment. The
spectacle of an incorporated pawnshop with its own
premises in pawn is something heartrending.
A rather curious little institution, the Town
and County Permanent Investment and Building Society
(Melbourne), publishes its balance-sheet for the year
ended 30th September, 1899. The book-value of the-
total assets is £4631, and the year’s revenue was £259.
The expenses of running the concern were £269, so it
went £lO to the bad on mere trading-, and quite apart
from £998 lost by sales of property below book-values.
This made the year’s loss £lOOB-more than one-fifth of
the society gone in one fell pop. The mistake seems to
be in keeping the Town and County Permanent Invest-
ment and Building Society in an office instead of in a tea-
cup. If some other institution could be persuaded to-
take it over, and run it in the office-boy’s spare time at a.
cost of not over t'so a year, it looks as if it could pay 3:
per cent, dividends to shareholders, and have £5O a year
over to write off depreciation with. And for a tea-cup
institution that wouldn’t be so very bad.
* * » •
The Vic. tobacco-growing industry this year
scored its direst failure up to date. Fifty hogsheads of
leaf, specially chosen by Government expert (Bondurant),
were shipped some months ago, to test the same old Lon-
don market which had previously snorted at Victorian
’baccy, the result being the same old 3d. a pound for the
pick of the shipment, and no market at all for the
balance. Bondurant is throwing up his billet this month,
for a more promising job in the West Indies, and there
doesn’t seem to be a keen political demand for another
expert. Even the prediction that this blue-mouldy
industry would end in smoke is falsified. The contents
of the 50 hogsheads will be mostly boiled down into soup
for the destruction of insects.
* ♦ * *
Somebody writes to the Age re Victorian tobacco
growing—“ For two years we have had no tobacco crop to
be cured according to the Bondurant or any other method.
The price of hops is so discouraging that growers are giv-
ing that up, and if the local tobacco market is cleared they
will grow tobacco, which by the way, is this year entirely
free from blue mould, and the plants look strong and
healthy. This will be the first clean crop grown from
seed bed to harvest since the Govt. Expert arrived,
and, if he is permitted to leave the province now
when his work is only beginning, the Vic. growers
will be thrown back once more upon their old
methods of cultivation and curing, and the work
done up to this date entirely lost.” From the pessimists’
point of view, the Age correspondent condemns his own
cause. Expert Bondurant came to Victoria three-and-a-
half years ago, and in the meantime two crops of tobacco-
have gone wrong, notwithstanding that he has per-
sistently preached the principles of successful growing
and conducted an experimental farm where the results
were no better than in other places. As for the present
crop it has been lucky thus far in escaping hot winds,
but vvhen the hot winds and other ordinary summer
calamities happen to it, it will probably go wrong as
usual; and even if it doesn’t go wrong there is surely
little left for Mr. Bondurant to teach the growers. The
grower who has failed to learn how to pick, clean, and
cure tobacco leaves on Bondurant’s system after three-
and-a-half years of verbal and practical instruction should
retire from the business and turn his mind to turnips.

The average mining fields, and especially the
fields way-back, know from experience what theuitlander
is after; and so they summed up the S. African situation

White Cliffs (Sleath’s stronghold !) —“ Sympathy here is

• • • •
Apropos White Cliffs opal-mining and the re-
cent wholesale dismissal of tributors, and the lately-
published statement that “ leakage” is at the bottom of
the trouble : “ leakage ” is not quite at the bottom, but
it is down that wav. The trouble really began with the
history of the field—and that history is worth telling,
first because it is absolutely unique, and then because
White Cliffs itself is one of the most interesting fields and
one of the least known in Australia. Eight or nine years
ago opal was found on a droughty western station, miles
from anywhere, by some Wilcannia people. They knew
nothing about mining, and all they knew about opal was
that it was worth a bit. Whether it made into lodes at
depth or bubbled up in springs they didn't know ; but
they pegged out 40-acre blocks on the off-chance. The
Mines Department knew just about as little of opal (it
had never been worked in the province) and granted the
leases! Of course, it was soon discovered that the
country ought to have been treated like an alluvial gold-
field ; but it was too late then—the pick of the country
was leased. The leaseholders were poor men, and so put
on tributors as the speediest way of making a rise. They
did not know what the stone was worth, and so were at
the mercy of the one or two buyers who were
in the business, and had no regular outlet for
surplus stones; and for their part the tributors,
knowing even less of the value of opal, were
at the mercy of the leaseholders. Now, after six
or seven years, the older diggers are as good judges of the
market-value of the gem as the buyers ; and when they
compare what they now know was the real market-
value of the stone they raised in the early davs with
the prices which they actually got for it, they are the
maddest men between here an'd Albany. Scores of them
say quite openly, “No ; So-and-So (or perhaps the co.)
‘had’me for three or four hundred ‘quid’ (opal was
plentiful then), and I’ve got to make it up somehow"—
though, as a matter of fact, somebody “had” the Aus-
tralian dealer, who also possibly “ had ” the leaseholder,
so that the digger certainly was not the only sufferer.
And, further, there is a much better market for the stone
to-day than there was six or eight years ago. It is a fact,
anyhow, that there is a great deal of “cronk stuff”—
stolen—about the field ; and though leading buyers will
not have anything to do with the business directly they
buy it indirectly, since dozens of diggers are themselves
also dealers. And there’are other buyers who have
practical men, tired of hard graft, in their regular pay,
with instructions to hang round pub. parlors and “do
in ” all the “ cronk stuff ” offering.
The opal is got in almost any kind of country—
in ironstone, clay, and sandstone, and even in quartz—
and at depths of from a few inches to about 60ft. A man
sinks a pot-hole ; if he finds gem-stone, or “ potch ” (un-
colored opal) at, say, 20ft., he drives that hole in any
direction, following the opal or “ potch ” along. When
he has worked out his tribute-block (say 100 ft. square) at
that level, he may sink again, and at 40ft. may find more.
Then the groping begins again. Sometimes men work
for months without seeing a sign of gem. The lease-
holders (most of the best leases are held by the English
co.) don’t direct operations in any way ; a* man simply
looks around once a week or so. Gem may have been got
but the co. has no proof of it. No timbering is done ;
most of the ground is a sort of soapstone. Some day
there will be serious “ caves.” Already there have been
one or two. When a man gets tired of one hole or block,
he quits it; but it remains his so long as he doesn't put
in a pick elsewhere. When he does that, hole and all (as
on an alluvial field) belong to the first comer that has a
fancy that way. When the opal is got it is taken to the
office. Sometimes the “office ’’ buys it; but if the price
offered doesn’t suit, the tributer may take it elsewhere
bringing to the office a certificate of the buyer that he has
given so much. The system of working—and, in fact all
the other arrangements—are crude ; and that up to i5OO
men could have been employed there without more
trouble than actually has arisen is almost a marvel.

The S W. districts of W.A., adjoining Jno.
Forrest s beloved Bunbury, are about to have their boom.
Good seams of coal have been opened at Collie, there are
inexhaustible forests of karri and jarrah, and tin has now
been proved to extend more or less from Greenbushes to
the Donnybrook field, which last looks like being a big
gold-producer.
•.•. • • *
In consequence of Zeb. Lane’s transactions in
Murchison shows and the probable booming of the dis-
trict prior to the flotations which he contemplates in
Europe, the fashion now is to secure Murchison options.
Numerous claims which had been more or less abandoned
have been taken in hand ; and in some cases the early
birds have snapped up winding plants and batteries for a
mere song. Work on tribute mostly follows.

London financial papers are often much
funnier for being five weeks old when they arrive in Aus-
tralia. About that time the “ Bear ” attack on the Lake
View Consols commenced. “ Moses Moss,” of Truth,
who always fights for “Bulls,” referring to the “ Bear”
squeeze said : “ The shares have gone from £2o£ to £23,
and I have every reason to anticipate a further important
advance, and there are many who say they are going to
£3O. Here is a co. paying diys. at the rate of £4 per
share per annum, or 400 percent., which at the present
price works out at 17 per cent, with every prospect of
this distribution being maintained.” He also published
a letter to show that a well-informed man has run the
rule over the Lake View bonanza and finds it to contain
15,886 tons of marvellously rich rock, while less than
3000 tons had been taken away. The writer wonders
what “ M.M.” is saying now that the shares, instead of
rising to £3O have fallen to £l5. Will he kick himself or
engage some able-bodied person to do it for him ? “ Moses
Moss” must write himself down as an ass and not a suc-
cessful tipster. He says of the notorious Peak Hill :
“ These have been quietened down to £6 155., but they
will probably take a very sudden jump shortly. They
have taken it—downwards—unfortunately for “M.M.”
They are now standing at £5 10s., a loss of another 255.
per share for his followers if he has any.

The early-day tip was that the Iron Duke was
going to be one of the big things of Kalgoorlie; and they
took over a high-grade Broken Hill man to develop it.
So far, little but shaft-sinking has been done, and that
leisurely if elaborately; but now, on the discovery of a
big low-grade sulphide body, the new Associated Northern
Blocks Co. is shaking things up, and the next few months
ought to show what the three or four already-discovered
formations are stuffed with.

Diehl, metallurgist in charge of the Brownhill
works, Kalgoorlie, is attempting to treat sulphides by
amalgamation, concentration, and cyanidation of battery
residues without preliminary roasting, and his experi-
ments are keenly watched.

When two or three Australians foregather, their
first impulse is to have a drink, and the second to ask
Govt, for a grant. They do it instinctively. The out-
skirts of the great dredging boom having just reached
Charters Towers, and every available gutter for 100 miles
around being pegged out, and, of course, duly exempted,
a meeting of interested speculators was called. It was
moved (1) that those present form themselves into an
association and subscribe 2s. 6d. per member (presumably
for drinks) ; (2) That Govt, be asked to purchase and in-
stal a dredge on some area to test the whole spec.
Carried unanimously. In the meantime, and while Govt
is being harassed for cash, the unwary traveller smashes
his shins on the pegs that fairly bristle all over the coun-
try. And any man who hasn’t any shares in a tin or gold-
dredging venture is a very mediaeval person indeed.
Apparently the hitherto self-reliant mining industry has
succumbed at last to the tired feeling that make one look
in Australia for the fabled land of the lotus-eater.

The Great Boulder Co., Kalgoorlie, have just
E laced an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.,
ltd.. South Melbourne, to build a pair of compound con-
densing: engines of 700 h.p. to drive their extensive mill-
ing: plant. These engines will be of the latest type, with
Proell valve gear; the flywheel is 20ft. in diameter,
grooved for 16 ropes, and weighs upwards of 22 tons ; and
the total weight of engine will be 85 tons. The engines
are to be completed by the end of the year and will be
the finest ami largest plant on the field. Three engines
under construction at the Otis Works for the Kalgoorlie
Goldfields have an aggregate of 1800 horse power.*

The United Brothers gold mine, situated at
Glen Wills, about 25 miles from Omeo, Gippsland, has an
improved appearance, and even better things are looked
for as deeper ground is reached. The mine possesses an
up-to-date mill. The stone is conveyed automatically to
hopper, thence to Gate’s Crusher and on to 20 head of
stamps of the A.T. B. pattern. A small but complete
plant of Luhrig Vanners and Classifiers treats the battery
tailings and continues to win a large percentage of valu-
able concentrates, which are a satisfactory source of
revenue to the co. This Luhrig plant was supplied and
erected under contract by Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere,
606 Little Collins-street, Melbourne.*

The contract entered into last year by Mephan
Ferguson for the supply of his patent rivetless or locking,
bar pipes to the South Australian Government having
been completed, Mr. Ferguson is now removing portion
of the Adelaide machinery to Western Australia, in order
to increase the output of pipes for his Coolgardie Water
Supply contract. IJe is at present delivering about 400
tons of pipes per week, but expects to more than double
this quantity when the additional plant is started. Mr.
Ferguson has just left for London by the Orient steamer
Oruba to arrange with the manufacturers for larger de-
liveries of plates and bars.*

Ifc is a common jibe ajvinst the hysterical
French that, whenever a military disaster strikes
them, they raise the wail, “We are betrayed.”
Notably when they had lost 60,000 men in the
early battles of the Franco-German war this
dolorous cry was loud all over the land. Appar-
ently the Australian is built on much the same
pattern. When General Gatacre lost less than
1000 men at Stormberg the lament arose in large
ink on almost every newspaper-bill in this
country: “ General Gatacre trapped,” “Gatacre
Betrayed,” &c. Two days later it appeared that
there was no certainty about any betrayal.

• * * *
New South Wales has already got back in
fines and liquor-duties a part of the money spent
on. its Contingent. The news of the latest
British reverse came to hand on Saturday, and on
Monday morning about 30 more drunks and
assaults than usual came up at the police-court.
The story was much the same in each case. The
patriot who was full of whisky said Buller was
a blamed fool for not making a strategic move-
ment by Cairo and Khartoum and taking Burger
in the rear. Then the patriot who was full of beer
drew a map of North America on the pave-
ment, and showed that if Buller hadn’t been
a very great fool he would have gone round by
Timbuctoo, with his base resting on South
America, and his left flank protected by the guns
of Gibraltar, and struck Burger, on the right
flank, just below the ear. And at this point
a third patriot generally chipped in and pointed
out that Peter Jackson would have hit Burger
straight on the point. Then the three patriots
fell on each other on top of the map, and fought a
demoniac shindy till the police came along.

Interesting cablegram :
The terrible effects of tbe lyddite shells were demon-
strated during the recent engagement at Magersfontein
between Lord Methuen ’s Kimberley relief column and the
Boers under General Cronje. The shock of the exploding
shells accounted for many of the enemy, who were found
standing up dgad in the trenches during the night.
Possibly the Dutchman has such large feet that if
he dies lying down he immediately falls up into a
standing position. If his feet are larger than his
body he would naturally fall on to the longest
part of him.
The English papers were not slow devising
a means to turn an honest penny out of the war.
The latest scheme is the issue of regular bulletins
to subscribers. “For 6d. per day the subscriber
receives one message of 12 words (including
address); for Is. 6d. a day a service of three
messages, if news of sufficient interest is received;
■or he may deposit a sum of money and leave it to
our discretion to send important news as it arises.”
Bulletins are dispatched at 12 noon, 5 p.m. and
7 p.m. The first man to avail himself of the
innovation was a shopkeeper, who wished to stick
fresh war news in the window to attract people,
who might thereby be induced to notice the fact
that he was selling herrings much cheaper than
any other shopkeeper in the street.
All told, there are now just eight men
resident at G-undaroo, N.S.W. ; _ and all
but one are J's.P.

Jingoism is humorless indeed ! In the
South Australian Assembly last week somebody
implored the Govt, to discover the “ villain ” who
had fooled the local Stock Exchange with a tele-
gram announcing “Relief of Ladysmith—6ooo
Boers captured.” In consequence of this tele-
gram the jobbers went forth on the burst, and
they will not feel happy until the iniquitous
joker has paid for the drinks with his life, or 10
years penal servitude at the least. It is an awful
thing to play larks upon the Himperial sentiment
of a Stock Exchange.

PARENTAL PRIDE.
Irate Neighbor: “See here, that boy of yours
has broken five panes of glass at my place,
killed my cat, set my fowl-house afire,
blackened my son’s eye”
Proud Father : “So Johnny has done all this,
has he, and him only six years of age—six
years, sir !”
They have a quaint old mad woman at
Carlton, Vic. She is small and perfectly wizened
and dressed quaintly in frocks of many colors and
numerous bits and pieces. Wherever she goes she
is accompanied by a dog, or at least she accom-
panies the dog, and her confidence in
that small wall-eyed tyke is the
peculiar characteristic of her mad-
ness. The dog leads, she follows,
and if she starts out to go across
the way her ever getting there
depends entirely upon the pup.
She trots after the dog in all his
devious windings, and often fetches
up, exhausted, in unheard-of places,
and has twice been caught wading in
the sea in devout emulation of
the responsible dog. The
sight of a little old woman
jigging exuberantly up
and down, playing fol-
low-my-leader to an ec-
centric, bob-tailed terrier
has ceased to be a novelty
in her own neighborhood,
but in other suburbs she
draws great crowds, and is
frequently seen with a
frenzied, unwavering optic
glued on the dog, and fol-
lowed rapturously in her
wild career by three day-
schools and about five hundred
unemployed. '

“The British have been repulsed at every
point except Arundel,” cabled Agent-General
Reeves to his Govt. And the same day—to make
the tally complete—came news that the British
had been repulsed at Arundel. The “ embattled
farmers ” have beaten, one after the other, every
British general brought against them.
* * * *
A Balmain (Sydney) father was sevSrely
rebuked in court the other day because he chained
up his small son as a punishment for playing the
wag. Strange how opinions change. In a very
moral book (the “Fairchild Family ”), written by
a lady too, a very kindly father of two or three
generations ago is represented punishing his off-
spring by taking him at nightfall to see the cor-
rupting body of a murderer hanging on a gibbet.
A certain amount of starvation and locking-up
were included in the programme, but these were
too trifling to be dilated upon.

Birth advfc. from the Wellington (M.L.)
Evening Post :
Browne.— On sth December, at Wellington, the wife
of M. J. Browne, elected a daughter. No more.
Query : Is the little elected Browne no more, or
have the old Brownes taken this opportunity of
informing their friends and the public of their
determination' to abstain hereafter from helping
to supply the greatest want of their young country
—population ?
The King’s School, Parramatta,
which assumes new dimensions under
the design of Architect Blacket, was
founded by the authority and at the ex-
pense of the British Govt. It was
christened, not King’s School, after Gov.
King, but The King’s School, after the
then King of England The first head-
master arrived in 1831, and for a time the
attendance was small. But if the boys
were few the chapel of the building held
many worshippers, as the imprisoned
convicts were sent there for Sunday ser-
vice. This is the third great architectural
change the historic old school has under-
gone. The headmasters were almost all
distinguished men, even outside the
bounds of their special profession.
Among them was the Rev. W. B.
Clarke, the Australian geologist.
G. E. Ardill’s anxiety to get
permanently on N.S.W. Estimates for his
Home of Hope should not be taken as a
sufficient reason for putting him there.
_ When the late Royal Commission on
Charities asked him what preparation
> he had made for fires in his establish-
ment, Ard ill replied : “ Personally, per-
haps, I take a stand that most folks do
not —I think that by trusting in God in
most of these matters we secure deliverance.”
Let him trust in the same way for a salary.
In the theatre gallery ’Arry’s best girl
solaces her thirst with microby lemonade and is at
peace. Down below, female aristocracy shudder-
ingly repudiates chocolates done up with blue
ribbon, and gets nothing better. Curious that
while man rushes out in a body to the bar at every
interval, woman manages to survive the three
hours of martyrdom without wetting a lip. All
the same, it is a wonder no enterprising manager
has thought of proffering ice-creams round among
the Nicest on hot nights. Seems to me he would
earn gratitude, likewise half-crowns.
THE VULTURE KING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When gaily in the morning
The flags of battle flew,
And trumpet-notes gave warning
Of bloody work to do,
Above that host unquailing,
With scarce a beat of wing,
A baleful bird was sailing—
’Twas the Vulture King.
He heard the bugles calling ;
He heard the soldiers cheer;
He saw the front ranks falling,
Linesman and cannoneer—
Around him sailed the others
In grim and gruesome ring—
“ Good feasting here, my brothers 1 ”
Said the Vulture King.
Some fought to make a story
Immortal —for a day—
And load themselves with glory ;
The rest, they fought for pay.
The red blood ran like’water
Down creeks in days of Spring—
“ For us is all this slaughter ! ”
Quoth the Vulture King.
Blood-mad like Berserk Norsemen
On death the squadrons dashed ;
The long swords of the horsemen
Like summer lightnings flashed ;
The fight in many an eddy
Did backward, forward swing.
“ Now let your beaks be ready ! ”
Said the Vulture King.
Like leaves that on the wind drift
In winter, phantoms pale
Flew past; they flew like spindrift
Before a shrieking gale.
The smoke above the battle
Rose thickly ring on ring—-
“ Men taste as well as cattle ! ”
Said the Vulture King.
The Boer fought not coldly
For Freedom, as he thought;
The Britisher as boldly
For Rhodes and Empire fought.
Both sides found red sepulture—
Their friends hosannas sing—
“ This War is for the Vulture”
Said the Vulture King.
When ended is the quarrel,
The butchery complete,
O, some shall wear the laurel,
And some the worms shall eat:
But, be who may the winner,
It is a dead-sure thing—
A table’s spread for dinner
For the Vulture King.

The Queen will not this Xmas leave
Windsor Castle, says the cable. It’s an ill war
that blows no good to anyone. Premier Salisbury
and other old and decrepit personages will be
spared the trouble of journeying all the wajr to
Scotland every time their Gracious Sovereign
wants ten minutes with them.
THE REWARD OF BRAVERY.
“ But I thought you was engaged to Jim."
“ So I was, but he went off with the contingent.”
Adelaide Chronicle reports in all appar-
ent seriousness that a patriarchal stone-breaker,
aged 90, was charged before Chief-Justice Way
with passing bogus coin, and was sentenced to a
year in gaol, the Judge saying that prisoner was
“ old enough to know better,” and that he hoped
the crumbling ruin would follow some honest em-
ployment when he came out. Next case was that
of a man aged 67 who had tried to cut short his
bright young life by suicide. He got a sentence
of six months and a severe warning to “act better
in the future.” Little stumpy Samuel’s fatherly
manner towards these badly-brought-up old men
is touching. But surely there should be some age
at which suicide is allowable. When a man has
given Australia 67 years’ trial and found it all
dust, he ought to be allowed to act on his matured
judgment and leave. Not being rich enough to
leave by mailboat, the only other vehicle to leave
by was death.

An interesting example of law as opposed
to justice came before Judge Edwards in M.L. a
little while ago, in a will case. Testator left his
property in shares to his widow and children, and
a plea was put forward that a share be allowed to
another child whose claim to citizenship is yet in
the future. The plea, however, was disallowed,
so in a short while the curious sight will be seen of
four children enjoying their father’s wealth with
all its educational advantages, and the fifth, for no
particular reason, an absolute pauper. Considering
this posthumous child cannot by any possibility
have annoyed its father in any way —for instance,
it cannot have refused to marry the girl its father
wanted it to, or taken to the turf or any of the
courses which are popularly supposed to have a bad
effect on paternal grey hairs, there seems no reason
why Baby Collins should be so severely punished
for happening on the scene rather later than his (?)
brothel's and sisters. Evidently, M.L. and this
country badly want a law which shall correct
such mistakes, and which shall also step in and
undo the effect of greed, malice, or religions
mania. The idea that a man has a right to dis-
pose of his own property just as he likes seems
deeply imbedded in the public mind. So, by the
way, was once that other idea that he could thrash
his own wife whenever he felt disposed.
• * * *
The election of Mauger, the water apostle,
for Footscray, has already brought grief to one
member of the Bung brigade. After the poll, a man
strolled into a Brunswick hotel, and said affably,
“ I see this ’ere bloke Magger got in for Footscray
all light.” “ Magger nothin’,'” said O’Bung,
“ It’s Morger ye mane.” The discussion proceeded
till at last the customer said disgustedly, “Oh, it’s
no good argin’ with an iggerant Irishman.” Then
O’Bung was over the counter, and the other man
lost three teeth and gained a black eye in five
seconds. Next day O’Bung paid a fiver for his
fun, but he’s likely to be an authority on the
English language in future—in his own bar, at
any rate.
* * * *
Vandemonia is now the reverse of what it
was 60 years ago. Tenderness of heart and con-
sideration for prisoners on the part of the
authorities have taken the place of tenderness of
the back and inconsiderate curses on the part of
the convicts. A few days ago a Sandy Bay milk-
man was tried in Hobart for bigamy. His sister
wrote from Sydney to say he was mad, and his
wife (the first one) sent to the prisoner’s lawyer
to ask him to tell the judge that the prisoner
wasn’t a bad sort, and as soon as he came out of
gaol she’d take him to her arms again. She
wanted him back at the earliest convenient time.
“ He’s been in gaol three months,” said lawyer
Ball, “ and I’d suggest your Honor should give
him three months and a little more, so that he
may spend his Christmas with his wife and five
children.” “I’ll make it four months from his
committal,” proposed the judge. “Oh, then,
he’ll spend Christmas in gaol !” said Ball. “All
right,” responded the judge, “I’ll make it ten
days.” Then the lawyer had another application
to make : “I hope they won’t shave him or cut
his hair, your Honor?” Of course it wouldn’t
look nice to go home for Christmas with stubble
face and gaol crop. But the judge only grinned,
and the gaoler bundled the bigamist down below.

Letter from a Lancer published in northern
N.S.W. paper:—
We have had our first day in South Africa at about SO
miles from Capetown at a town named above, if you can
only pronounce it. What an experience we have had,
and only landed about 24 hours. This morning, about 9
o’clock, we saw about 30 blackfellows, South African
natives, getting flogged. My word, they got it properly,
they were tied across a log stark naked, and the strongest
and ablest men were chosen to use the whip. Each got
from a dozen to two dozen apiece. All the Lancers saw
the performance. I have often heard of poor devils
getting flogged, but little did I think we would ever
see anything like what we saw this morning. How they
did sing out. When the flogging was finished a tinful of
brine was obtained, and about a pint was poured over
each one’s back. Whilst the flogging was going on they
had about 300 other fellows watching the performance,
and they were told if they caused any disturbance in the
night they would be dealt with in a similar manner. The
boss over them asked them if they understood and each
one said “Oh ! yes boss, we know.” A disturbance had
arisen the previous night and one of their mates was
badly handled—nearly killed in fact. I think he must
die, and if he dies three of these fellows are to be shot.
The cruelty of the Boers towards the local nigger
is one of the strong points in the case against
Kruger’s crowd, yet it would seem as if the life of
the Cape Colony nigger isn’t unadulterated beer
and skittles either.
* * • *
Apropos the recent disappearance of a
highly-trusted Victorian solicitor and its lament-
able results, “ Yamba ” writes to The Bulletin :
The man’s victims include dozens of people who are
now reduced from comfort to beggary. The horror with
which I view these crimes dates from three years ago,
when I became acquainted with the persona? of the fol-
lowing obscure tragedy: A man, his wife, and four
children and a grandmother were living in comfort on an
income of about £2 10 per week, the. result of 40 years, of
hard work and careful saving. The agent for the in-
vested money—the father had retired from business—
was a boornster who is now in gaol, and one morning the
family discovered that its whole little fortune had been
s vept away. The shock was coo much for the father,
aged 60, and in a fortnight he was taken to Kew Asylum,
where he is now. The wife’s health then gave way, and
she had to go to hospital. The four children were left
with the grandmother, aged 81, who, keeping her poverty
to herself, sold article after article from the house to buy
food. A philanthropic friend of mine who visited the
sick wife was asked by her to look up the children, and
when she did she found the house stripped of everything
but a few bags. Even the beds were gone, and the old
dame and the four children were huddled together,
clothed in rags, in a corner. We got them blankets at
once, and when the wife grew better we bought her a
sewing-machine ; and by working 16 hours a day she
keeps the family in food and clothes. And now, when I
hear of cases like that trusted solicitor’s, I feel that for
once I would like to be either Judge or jury.
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent:—
A strange thing happened in connection with the
remains of an old woman who died at Balmain, Sydney,
the other day. The body was coffined, and, at night,
candles were iit about the corpse. Some 30 neighbors,
mostly women, sat in the room and gossiped. At mid-
night a candle suddenly went out, and a bird flew in
through the open window and settled on the quenched
candle. There it sat the whole night long with its eyes
fixed upon the corpse. Nobody disturbed it and it took
no notice of the visitors to the chamber of death. It said
nothing, not even “ Nevermore ! ” Daybreak saw it still
sitting on the candle and it was perched there when the
undertaker arrived at 2.30 p.m. When the coffin, how-
ever, was closed and carried out of the room, the bird
shot through the window and was lost to view. The
people of Balmain are, just now, more concerned over
that mysterious bird than they are over news from South
Africa or anything else.
A picturesque story, but lacking in detail. What
kind of bird was it that roosted on the candle
which “suddenly went out,” and “sat the whole
night with its eyes on the corpse ? ” The Bulle-
tin would much like to cross-examine say half-a-
dozen of that party of 30 neighbors—mostly tunned
Murphy, no doubt.
* * • •
The Supreme Court Judges in Tasmania
don’t go on circuit like Judges in other places,
and a queer instance of what happens under the
Tassy system occurred last week in Hobart. A
man was sent from Queenstown, on the West
Coast, to stand his trial for an attempt to steal
two bottles of Usher’s whisky in a village pub.
With him were shipped the police inspector of
the place and 12 witnesses for the prosecution or
defence. “Her Majesty’s Solicitor-General,” the
Hon. Alfred Dobson, Q.C., &c., &c., came down
in a wig and silk gown to proseeute the hardened
monster of iniquity, and the Hon. W. W. Perkins,
M.L.C., &c., &c., appeared in wig and gown- to
grapple with the indictment and move the jury to
tears—or whisky and water. The senior Puisne
Judge of the Supreme Court of Tasmania sat on
the bench in state, and the sheriff, in evening
dress (though it was before lunch) sat beneath the
Judge—suggestive of a speedy execution. Then
the jury found the prisoner not guilty, and as the
attempt to steal two bottles of grog was the only
case on the calendar, the great legal machine
resolved itself into its constituent elements once
more.
DESOLATION—IN EIGHT LINES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beneath the pepper-trees,
I kissed her brow :
Ambrosial was the breeze
Beneath the pepper-trees;
But night came on—and please,
I’m sorry now
Beneath the pepper-trees
I kissed her brow.
MILKY WHITE.
FOR BEDUTY'S SAKE
THERE’S never a castle-tower so stbep
Nor the breath of her guns so warm
But, if Beauty lay bound in herdun'geon-keep,
The cowards of earth would storm ;
Not a barrier built in the bounds of Time,
Not a charge in the lists of Life,
But a man would dare, but a man would climb,
Were Beauty to crown the strife !
There’s never a ’chaser too»wild to steer—
-1 One touch of her hand to earn ;
There’s never a fence for ajnan to fear
If Beauty shall watch at the turn ;
And heavy the quest will a man take up,
However so light the need,
If Beauty will fill him his stirrup-cup
And kiss him his last God speed !
By height and hollow a man may ride,
By river and ridge and plain,
But Beauty will beckon and Love will guide
Till he win to her feet again.
Beauty’s the toast! Be she dark or fair,
Wherever her witch-spell lies
We shall fight for a lock of her golden hail
Or a glance of her dear brown eyes.
