A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, December 26, 1899.
Mr Dear Myee,—
One Xmas is very like another, you won’t deny,
but the ghastly details of the war lend this a
miserable distinction. Nobody expects to do
much merry-making, and the summer hotels are
looking forward to anything but a good season.
Who has the heart to go after fun in the face
of Lord Methuen’s description of the Battle of
Modder River, fought December, 1899 ; “ one
of the bloodiest battles of the century ! ”
The Bohemians in this city who banded them-
selves together into a club that agrees to have
suppers at certain times and musical evenings
after, so that there can be no mistake about its
freedom from all social conventions, gave a sit-up
evening last Tuesday with His Ex. as the honored
guest. The supper came off at the Victoria Hotel.
Everybody pleased himself about dressing. Some
of the men came straight from office with the ink
fresh on the middle finger, which represents only
a certain amount of freedom after all.
From a woman’s point of view, a club where
everyone had to turn up dressed to the best pos-
sible advantage, but where the shackles of a
musical programme were cast off—where there'was
no chairman to call on Mr. Throatee for a song,
and Mr. Dorbo for a lightning sketch, and where
music didn’t always stick out of overcoat pockets
in the lobby, and the evening didn’t repeat all the
saddest features of a local concert with the addi-
tional anguish of having to be a friend to the
perforiners thrown in—would feel far more like
freedom and release and the genuine long-haired
atmosphere, and all that sort of thing. It is
always a mystery to me why men will persist in
drawing down on their heads the musical evening
aforesaid, particularly as the Bohemian Club
(which changed its name, I must hasten to
remark, somewhere near its beginning, and
called itself the Supper Club) in Sydney
is a fraternity that never gets wholly ob-
livious to what goes on after supper.
His Ex. and Wilfred Smith, who is now P.S.
and aide-de-camp, and chaperon and middle-man
all rolled into one, arrived at seven o’clock, and
were welcomed by poets, artists, journalists,
musicians, and a few unclassified kinds. I don’t
know why they put His Ex. next to Attorney-
General Wise, who is only an ordinary being
after all, such as His Ex. knows heaps of, when
there were so many queerer kinds about that
would have been positive novelty to an English
earl. However, that’s neither here nor there.
The great feature of the party was that His Ex.’s
speech was a grown-up effort, unadulterated with
Kipling, and pointed with a story about a mixed
bathing-place. Can it be possible that the dew of
his Excellency’s youth is drying up under the
burning Australian climate.
Speaking of clubs, a story is going about lately
regarding a brotherhood that is altogether made
up of poets. The club hasn’t a meeting-house.
It falls-to on the curbstone, or against a lamp-post,
at any time, in any spot. His Ex. met the presi-
dent of the club lately, unofficially, and said some
nice things about the latter’s “ poitree,” and in-
vited him to lunch at Gov’ment House. Where-,
upon the president quickly told all about the club
that met against the lamp-post, and His Ex. just
as quickly said that he would like to meet all these
gentlemen at the lunch aforesaid. So His Ex.,
never tired of entertaining bodies, has now taken
also to entertaining souls. A lamp-post club is all
soul.
A war story that has been circulated in Melbourne
has been freely fastened on to Miss Sylvia Darley.
A Melbourne paper lately wrote of the story of a
mysterious marriage between an officer who left
by the Aberdeen and the daughter of a Sydney
Lieut.-Gov. It told how the marriage came off at
Manly, just before the troops started, and how the
bride was on the Aberdeen under her maiden
name to see the newly-made husband off. As
there’s only one Liout. -Governor in Sydney, it was
either Miss Kitty or Miss Sylvia Darley. The
Darley family was besieged'with inquiries, veiled
and otherwise. The officer was freely spoken of
as Captain Dangar," I suppose for want of more
reliable information. It turps out now that the
whole romance is only romance—not a word of
substance in it. j
I went down to see the Moana off last Wednes-
day and found a big crowd going. There was the
very young bride, who has just married JSSOOO
a-year in a grey suit with a nice face, nie Miss
Pery, of The Towers, and her bridegroom, Mr.
Hineheliff. The young couple are off to. one of
the wealthiest ranches in California. Charlie
Kenningham was farewelled regretfully by heaps
of friends. He goes to play big parts in New
York. Miss Viola Gillette Alan-a-dale, in
“Robin Hood ” —cut-up the outlines of that big,
beautiful figure of hers in a huge blue-and-white
check frock, and sported the latest thing in your
second-best hat—a straw, trimmed with scarves,
the fringed ends falling over one side of the brim,
in the old-man puggaree style. Mrs. Fred Aron-
son, the well-known Sydney pen-woman, was on
board, bound for the hot baths at Rotorua. She
has. been a victim to rheumatism lately, and her
large circle of friends hope that the baths will do
her a world of good.
Speaking of pen-women, Miss Darehy, of the
Daily Telegraph, is leaving us,in January, to go
lecturing on metaphysics, and the like bottomless
subjects, in America.
Do you notice what hilarity marks the local
artist of to-day ? It looks as if Art was beginning to
be a more paying game than in the old days when an
artist wasn’t the genuine article unless he could
starve and go without Pears’ soap. The nice'
young men who paint Sydney’s pictures are quite
remarkably trim ; not a hollow cheek nor a hectic
flush among them. They cut their hair more
than once a year, have their manners carefully
pared and prepared, and look unanimously eager
and healthy, especially when a picture-dealer is
inspecting their canvasses. No. There are too
many ambitious Australian maidens yearning to
learn to paint roses climbing over fiddles, or
sliced oranges keeping company with a dead bird,
a woman’s shoe, and a Sevres vase, for any artist
who will teach to indulge in a hollow cheek.
“ The Only Way ” opened at the Theatre Royal
on Saturday to a d.c. that was nearly all from
Israel, or else theatrical. The Jewish element,
you will be glad to hear, is going through a trans-
formation scene in its theatre frocks. Even the
largest, fattest Jewess wears black now, and
kaleidoscopic raiment that used to mark the
Chosen all over the house is rapidly disappearing.
Edith Trilby Crane was a figure you couldn’tover-
look in pink satin, pluckily suspended from her
large white shoulders by thin pink threads. Mr.
and Mrs. J. C. Williamson watched from one of
the boxes, the latter looking particularly well-
gowned in jetted black, made liigh to the throat.
And there was seen the curious spectacle of
“ Goody ” sitting out a play from start to finish.
Linda Raymond takes a leap above her head in
the part of Lucie. She makes up fair and plump
and pink and gold and white, and subdues herself
a good deal, but still makes eyes in 'a too-moni-
festly manufactured way. One arch glance or so
carries a delicate meaning, but a whole battery of
made eyes that look as if they know too much are
out of place. Miss Raymond frocks herself in
flowing clothes. A sky-blue satin frock, worn in
the garden of Dr. Manette’s house, falls in long
lines from the bust, with a blue chiffon fichu round
the shoulders. Her second frock is black, worn
with a big black hat. And then, when she falls
asleep and Sydney Carton steals one kiss off the
extreme edge of her cheek, she wears pure, soft-
clinging white, as you know very well she must.
Rose Musgrove has an uncomfortable-looking
little part in elbow-sleeves that seem too tight
and frocks that have a squeezed look generally.
Rose wears a thin white face and a look of patient,
earnest fragility, and you like her better when she
sits down with her elbows on her knees than when
she walks carefully across the stage in that un-
comfortable little bursting brown dress.
The fat of the play, with the exception of that
large-waisted part. The Vengeance—taken so
cleverly by Miss Julia Merton, in revolutionary
dishabille —all falls to Mr. Thomas Kingston.
Yon won’t be surprised to hear that I like King-
ston much better sober than in the otherwise state
in which he plays his first act. It reminded me
of Wilson Barrett’s drunk-scene in “Silver King”
—now sober, now sober, now able to say “Colonel
Richardson,” then Suddenlyand mysteriously gone
in the legs and tongue in the most hopeless
way. Kingston looks the part to the life, and, after
the first half of the play, gets into your sympathies
till you find yourself looking for your handker-
chief when the final beautiful tableau' shbws
Sydney Carton mounting the scaffold to lay down
his life for Harry Hill. The marvellous likeness
between Harry Hill and Kingston doesn’t strike
the d.c. as forcibly as it is meant to. In fact, if
both men “did ” their hair the resend lave— such
as it is—would vanish into thin air, f r dieir chief
similarity is in the way in which their long, dark
locks are equally uncombed.
The third act of “The Only Way” shows
The Revolutionary Tribunal, a stirring crowd of
citizenesses; and blood-seekers. It is such
a relief to have the attention chained, to
have the thoughts gripped till they cease to
wander. Half through your average play the
mind gives itself up only by fits and starts, and
very dreary are the long dull spells when the stage
is only a stage, and all the actors are merely
mummers. This Tribunal scene goes splendidly,
the trial of Charles Darnay, his defence by Sydney
Carton, his acquittal, and tlie woman with the
long starved arms ceasing to cry “ Warm Berlud ”
for a minute, then his sudden arrest again, re-
indictment, sentence of death, almost before you
can breathe, and the thin woman howling “ Warm
Blood ” again—all this is thrown at the audience
so vigorously that the house was carried away on
Saturday night, and gave the end of the play a
hearty welcome.,
Fashion is in an eccentric mood just now. All
the things that “no lady ” would ever do are done
now with impunity, flaunted in your face by the
upper ‘ten, by d’ay as well as by night. Linen
collars are bye-gones, and with their passing the
bare neck showing in street costumes has become
not only permissible, but admirable. A little
while ago the low neck characterised chorus-girls,
actresses who coiddn’t act, and maidens, who
smiled from behind bars. Now, everybody wears
it. Women tie a bit of ribbon velvet for
choice—round their necks, and clasp a string of
pearls beneath the ribbon. And pearls—these are
put on before breakfast now, and worn all day,
and any part of the day. The vulgarity of jewels
in the morning is exploding rapidly. The smartest
women are in town nowadays in the morning in
holland frocks, with jewelled chains around their
chests, and pearls around their throats, and
jewelled chatelaines at tlieir waists, and jewelled
pins all over the lace sailor-tie, and jewelled hat-
pins in their sailor hats ; also they put on many
rings, carry a fan in one hand, and a purse
that looks like a scent-bottle in the other,
and go gloveless anywhere and everywhere. This
is extremely swagger. But if you wish to go a
step further, you can treat yourself to a pair of
white cotton gloves at elevenpence halfpenny a
pair, and carry a shilling grass-bag of very common
quality in your left hand.
But oh ! the contumely that has fallen on cer-
tain well-beloved articles of woman’s dress that
held a place of honor for years! The belting
of last year, and other years before, is suddenly
dead. Nobody looks at “Petersham” nowadays.
A fold of ribbon on the skirt—cerise, turquoise,
black, or matching the skirt—knotted at one side,
is the only belt permitted. Also, quite dead is
the colored shirt-tie for blouses, although the shops
keep on showing it. The twist of cream or
string-colored lace has driven ties out of time. A
slower death, but a surer one nevertheless, is that
of the silk petticoat. White underskirts have
come to the front this year, billowy with lace and
frills, and overskirts, and the silk petticoat (was
there ever an idol in the wardrobe so widely and
passionately cherished as the silk petticoat?) is
creeping outwards into oblivion.
An ingenious tip for women travelling by boat,
where the soft sea-breezes have a way of smacking
one’s hair hat out on the forehead, is to travel with
a toupee sewn inside the hat. If the hat is well
pinned-on the toupee can’t swerve in the stiffest
breeze. This is a regular boon in a bathing place
where a woman unrobes, has her swim, and when
she is ready to depart, pops on her hair and her
hat, and there she is. Nothing lank and damp
and water-washed about her front fringe, While
if she had merely.reinoved her toupee, and put it
on a chair, the chances are ten to one it would
have blown away before she returned. You can’t
beat hair for blowing.
While on the subject, a toupee is a thing you
can talk to anyone about nowadays. I don’t
know why, but it has almost grown into an item
of conversation: “Is my toupee straight, Hr.
Dash ? ” “Just an eighth of an inch to the right,
Miss Blank,” wouldn’t make Mrs. Grundy agitate
an eyelid. For my part, I believe that the woman
who sits in a shop-window in Pitt-street and
dresses woman’s false hair all day long to crowds
of curious men is doing hastily away with much of
the solemn and unbroken mystery that used to
belong to any sort of hair you paid for.
New music continues to pour in, and it is easy
to see that the war is going to lay its great red
hand over all who follow the Muse. I have before
me (from Paling and Co.) Alfred Hill’s new song,
“When the Empire Calls,” words by Hugh Mac-
Donald, M.L.A. The best thing about the song,
which is a long way after Mr. Hill’s “ My Fairest
Child,’’is a photo, of Nelson Illingworth’s clever
statuette of one of the Australian Horse. The
words are strung together with “the crimson tie
of kinship,” and the music hasn’t the magic, irre-
sistible voice of the Empire calling. “ The Chal-
lenge Waltz ” I suspect to be martial also,
although Alfred E. Perkins dedicates it to Mayoress
Harris, who has often revolved to much less
melodious valse music. “Fidelis et Paratus”is
frankly a regimental march of the 4th N.S.W.
Infantry, by W. G. Bentley, a tuneful well-
marked piece that makes you all soldiers.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Vic. National Gallery exhibition of students’
work in competition for the travelling scholarship
shows in its seven features a large quantity of good
painting, an amount of drawing of mixed quality,
and an infinitesimal trace of imagination. The
technique of the worst of the paintings is ten
degrees higher than the best is in composition and
dramatic power. Apparently the moment the
Vic. students attempt to think out a picture the
strong draftsmanship they show in their studies is
instantly affected. For months past artistic Melh.
lias looked upon the scholarship as a foregone
conclusion for Meldrum, the little Scotchman,
and in the face of the opinion of artistic Melb.,
the judges evidently thought it “up to them”
to give Meldrum the prize. To the lay-
man, and the unprejudiced man who knows
good art when he sees it, Meldrum’s
picture, over-crowded, ill-drawn, and dirty-colored,
would appear to be about fourth on the list; Like
nearly everybody else, his idea of ‘Welcome
News ’ consists of one person reading a letter to
an interested crowd of uninteresting people, and
it is streets behind the picture of Norman
M‘George, in which two unlovely female artists
have rushed into the studio of a male ditto with a
daily paper. Miss Violet Teague’s bright picture
with a heliotrope key-note is the most striking
of all, and Hennessy’s attempt to catch the
popular eye by putting a Transvaal map at the
back of his picture provides a bit of bathos to a
good picture of an aged couple with the inevitable
letter. Ramsay has a domestic picture of the
Meldrum sort, and Miss Hake, Somers and Wilkie
all provide commonplace surroundings to the
eternal missive.
“The exhibition of studies is put away in
another part of the building, apparently in order
that the sassiety crowd who come to see the
scholarship pictures may riot be shocked at the
naked and 'unashamedness of Miss Trilby. The
gallery itself might well be-ashamed of its models,
for, of the two girls whose forms are shown at the
exhibition, one is skinny, the other is bloated, and
both are distinctly plain. The same eccentricity
of judging the characterised scholarship pictures
is manifest in the studies also.
“Florence Fuller, the Australian artist whose
portrait of Cecil Rhodes is reproduced in Revikw
of Reviews, has painted no less than three pic-
tures of her famous sitter Miss Fuller is a sister
of those tuneful warblers, Christie and Amy
Fuller, and she has an elder sister who formerly
contributed graceful verse to local journals. These
gifted girls worked themselves to the front at a
time when benefits were less common than they are
now.
“ Lord Brassey runs an elegant little picture-
gallery at Govt. House. It is not much of a pic-
ture-gallery when visited by daylight —it chiefly
consists of the conventional rich man’s pictures
guaranteed to have cost heaps of money—but
visited by night it fetches the loyal to their knees.
His Ex. touches a button, and the electric-light
does the rest. Conspicuous in the collection is a
large, red, Turneresque painting looking like a.
turbulent sunset ; all round the frame of this
picture are set a score of little lamps, and when
the button is manipulated, the painting is bathed
in a sea of glory, and is seen to be a portrait of
Lord Thomas Brassey himself. This is Brassey’s
idea of the fierce white light that beats upon a
Throne—once removed.
“ Students at Melb. National Gallery are sore
afflicted of late by the gambols of juvenile
‘ talent ’ in their midst. Half-mast-skirted school-
girls come giggling up to the easels arm-in-arm
and discuss Art as though it were chiefly a means
of escape from helping mother to make the beds
and peel the domestic ’tater. And the work they
put in for the most part reveals only .their special
aptitude for the household duties they have fore-
sworn.
“ Attorney-General Isaacs’ 3500-guinea fee from
the Melbourne Tramway Co. to conduct its
municipal-rating appeal before the Privy Council,
is p'rribably the biggest sum ever paid to counsel
in an Australian case. Mr. Isaacs’ordinary fee for
attendance in the Vic. Law Courts is 30 guineas
a day. As he expects to be away about three
months the 3500 guineas figures out at about that
rate. Mrs. Isaacs accompanies him, and should
go far to show official people at ’Ome that the
wives of Australian public riien are riot all female
hoodlums. For the wife of the ex-A.-G. is as
brilliant as her husband —an excellent linguist, a
fine judge of art, and a witty talker. Isaacs
refused a knighthood two years ago.
“A recent Melbourne demise and probate is
that of Mrs. Nicholas, of Toorak, widow of the
rich western squatter, Johnson, who when left
with a heap of money put some of it to
good use by buying for her buxom self a
nice, well-curled young riian riamed Nicholas,
on whom she settled some cash and from
whonoshe soon separated? I have seen a news-
paper paragraph that he died in. the East. Any-
how, the will doesn’t credit the name of
The Johnsons, her children, have got
all, as they ought to. The story, doubtless false,
used to be that Nicholas —he was once a bank-
clerk—courted one of the daughters, but the
mother took him. A daughter married City
Organist Lee. Another was the famous boy-girl,
so called only because she sensibly got her hair
cut, as hundreds do now.
“‘Charles Donaldson,’ in private life Dr.
Charlie Law, whose Shakesperean . and Dickens
recitals are praised by London critics, is a tall,
bright-faced, light-hearted Victorian. This pro-
mising elocutionist is chiefly self-taught. When,
as a boy, he set his heart on an actor’s career, his
parents, whose only son he is, persuaded him to
postpone any steps in the direction of the stage
till he should have qualified as a doctor. His
ensuing medical course, owing, perhaps, to divided
devotion, did not slip hy as smoothly as a motor-
car, and the student quitted Melbourne medical
school for a Scottish one before he arrived at the
dignity of a fully-fledged physician.
“Actors, and especially actresses, experts in
simulation and dissimulation, are as little able to
disguise their emotions as the undisciplined ruck.
I have been a dutiful theatre-goer for 15 years, and
have yet to discover the actress who can refrain
from looking painfully and abjectly demoralised
when the showy bouquet handed up from the
orchestra is carried to her professional sister. The
lady sheds what is no doubt intended for a con-
gratulatory smile, but what a rigid, lost-soul of a
grin it is ! —as joyous as the facial spasm of a
gouty alderman when his big toe rings him up
suddenly.
Y ours affectionately,

PERSONAL ITEMS.
War-artist Melton Prior, now in South
Africa for the Illustrated London News, has
already represented that journal in 21 campaigns,
beginning with that of Ashantee in ’73. The
Ashantee campaign is memorable as having given
rise to PuNOH’s.last real joke :
Coomassie was to ashes burned,
And Coffee forced to flee,
And only ash and Coffee left
Of what was Ashantee.
“P.”: Strode, a name “not unknown in
Melb.” (8., Deo. 2). liather! Why, Thomas
Strode was the printer and publisher of the Pr.
Phillip Gazette, Victoria’s first legalised paper,
first issued on Oct. 27, ’3B. A plucky fellow he
was. He had only small type, and out all his
“ poster ” letters out of pine; also, for some
months he set up the whole issue himself, twice a
week. He had been overseer on the S.M. Herald.
Arden (aged 19) was the first editor.
Dr. Wollaston, secretary for Vic. Customs,
•is grandson of the first parson who ever settled in
Westralia, where Wollaston hitnself was born, in
the Bame place and year as Premier Forrest. He
is deaf as a post but sharp as a needle. His father
was a surgeon, but became a parson, and
for years had charge of Holy Trinity Church,
E. Melb. Old Archdeacon Wollaston, the Customs
man’s second brother, brought out a church in his
portmanteau or otherwise, and erected it at Picton,
Westralia, aud it’s there still.
The members of N.S.W. Marine Board
haven’t always been fossils. President Hixson,
when he belonged to H.M.S. Herald in the early
’6o’s, received the Humane Society’s medal for
jumping overboard after an A.B. in Shark Bay
(W.A.), and on State occasions wears a miniature
of the medal, of which he is very proud. Also,
one other member of the Board, when third mate
of a Sydney-London clipper, made a run away
match with a lady passenger. He doesn’t 100 b ’
much like running away with anything now !
A.n indignant Melbourne Britisher :
People here remark that you are not “ game ” to print
a portrait of your friend Kruger in all his bestial ugliness.
No pro-Boer newspaper would be so rash as to publish
Kruger’s portrait. His vile old countenance is at once a
condemnation of himself, a cruel satire on the credulity
of his disciples, arid an insult to the name of Religion.
Train up a hog ter wallow in the Scriptures, and when he
is old he will be a flattering likeness of Oom Paul.
And yet there are half-a-dozen harmless monkey-
shaved Melbourne preachers who look just like
Oom Paul !
IN PUSH SOCIETY.
MAUDIE: “ What does 'a la carte ’ mean,
Andy? ”
ANDY: “ Yous girls knows nuthin’. That’s
Italgun for yer brings yer own feed in per
own cart.”
Hornsby, who has ousted Squatter
Buchanan from the Wairarapa Tory stronghold,
is a well-known M.L. journalist. Hornsby gave
Buchanan a severe shaking three years ago.
Shortly afterwards he lost his billet as editor of
Wellington Times. This happening cost
Buchanan his pocket constituency, for Hornsby
settled in the enemy’s district, bought a local rag
and steadily boomed his own candidature and
Liberal doctrines. Wairarapa was a Tory fortress
of very long standing, and an extra large
Ichabod was written on the wall when the party
heard of this loss.
’Victorian ex-Chief Secretary Peacock’s
recent appearances on public platforms indicate
that the mantle of Berry has fallen upon him. He
is fast becoming the idol of the Liberal masses of
Victoria, and wherever he goes gets a great
reception from the crowd. Unlike Turner he is
a born fighter and not afraid to hit his. opponents
when opportunity offers. He has no original
thought and his heart is a bigger factor than his
brain. The blood in his veins is warm and that is
the true secret of what is generally called platform
magnetism. Like Berry, too, he was, at one
period of his life, in the grocery business.
“ D." writes :
Referring to a recent Bulletin paragraph, South Aus-
tralian Attorney-General Gordon is not the son of a Bible
Chrisiian minister. His father was for many years the
minister of the Presbyterian Church in Gawler. Gordon
began life in the office of Duffield, a well-known wheat
buyer and miller in Gawler. He was subsequently
articled to a solicitor and in due course was enrolled in
“The Devil’s Brigade.” For many years he had a good
practice at Strathalbyn which brought him in from 11500
to <£2ooo a year. About ten or twelve years ago he sold
his practice, intending to go to Alice Springs to take
charge of a cattle station in which he was interested, hut
instead he gravitated to Adelaide, ran for the Legis ative
Council, was elected arid became a partner with that
well-known, eccentric and most able lawyer, E. Paris
Nesbit. Gordon is a very fluent and ornate speaker with
a persuasive manner, the effect of which is marred by his
apparent lack of sincerity and conviction. It is difficult
to take Gordon seriously. He has the misfortune of
seeming to be an agreeable man who believes in nothing
Vic. ex-Sheriff Ellis talks of writing his
reminiscences. He should entitle the bonk
“Recollections of the South Corridor,” which is
where the gallows stand in Melb. Gaol. A small
grey man, always dressed in grey and of a grey
nature; a pensioner of some years’ standing, and
apparently good for another twenty ; he may be
seen any fine afternoon on Collins-street block, or
walking home to St. Kilda. His recollections
would be interesting, though grisly-grey, em-
bracing, as they would, a long series of incidents
connected with the hangman and the white cap
and the “dull, sickening thud.” One of the first
“episodes” he assisted at, as under-sheriff, was
the turning-off of Mrs. Scott, the first woman
hanged in Victoria, her paramour, George, and
an accessory, a Malay. The lady was possessed
of good looks and considerab’e personal charm,
»nd the under-sheriff had known her previously
and had even danced with her; so was most
desirous of keeping in the back ground on the
morning of her abrupt demise. Among the
spectators that day was an unknown man, an ex-
sea captain, who, as the condemned woman
emerged from her cell, started forward with the
cry “Good God!” but "was restrained by
by-standers. Questioned afterwards he put it
down to involuntary and irresponsible nervous
excitement, but he was supposed to have been an
old flame of the lady’s.
The only hereditary-titled official in the
Westralian Government service is Sir Alexander
Cockburn Campbell, Bart., lately appointed clerk
to the magistrates at Wyndham, the farthest-
north Westralian town. The young man,
whose father was formerly president of
W.A. Leg. Council, onoe held a Parlia-
mentary assistant-clerkship, and has since
had experience of station life in the tropical
Kimberley district.

The recently - deceased John Hancock,
M.L.A. for Fitzroy (Vic.), was a compositor, and
worked in London at a house where Dickens’s
novels were printed. John often “set-up”
Dickens’s copy, as he himself told the present
writer. John was fond of telling that his mother
was Irish, and that she used to say, “ Whatever
good there’s in you, John, you’ve got from good
Irish blood ’’—and he trotted out this at Michael
Davitt’s reception in Melbourne, a few years ago.
Trenwith was the next speaker, and he, too,
wanted to establish an Irish connection. “I
can’t say,” remarked Trenwith, “ that my mother
was an Irishwoman or my father an Irishman,
but I served my time as a shoemaker to an Irish-
man who had ; done time’ for politics.” Which
brought down the house, and set Trenwith at
ease.

In obituarising the late Colonel Mac-
donald a Sydney daily made wild work with its
history and geography. “In 1839 he served in
the Scinde campaign ; was in command of the
Poonah Horse in Upper Scinde in 1847, and was
engaged against the. Beluohee and Candaharee
tribes infesting the frontier.” Ist. There was no
“Scinde campaign” in 1839, or for two years
after. (Butler’s “Life of Sir Charles Nanier.”)
2nd. The Poonah Horse were not in Upper
Scinde in 1847. (“Records of Scinde Horse.”)
3rd. No “ Kandahari tribes ” ever “infested the
frontier.” Kandahar is a long 250 miles from
the then British frontier, and the Kandaharis
would have had to fight their way for at least 200
miles of that distance through tribes that hated
them like poison.
The war in South Africa has rent the
Schreiner family like a rag. Before, they were
divided on questions of religion and politics, but
the war has made the breach complete. The
eldest brother is a teacher in England and is abso-
lutely unknown in South Africa. Theodore
Schreiner is an evangelist preacher, an enthusias-
tic temperance lecturer, and in politics an ardent
Imperialist of the Rhodes and red-paint party.
The eldest sister, Mrs. Lewis, is built on the same
lines. W. P. Schreiner, Premier of Cape Colony,
is a la wyer, leader of the Dutch Party, and a pro-
nounced agnostic. Olive Schreiner is like W. P.
in politics, and in religion is said to be the same,
only more so. The.family are the offspring of a"
German father and an English mother, and there
is no Dutch blood in them.
During the recent political juggle in Vic.,
a reporter from one of the dailies was despatched
to interview an M.L.A., who was rumored as
possible Treasurer. The Minister expectant is a
suburban shopkeeper in private life. “ What is
wanted,” he remarked, with a sweep of his hand,
“ is a man with a knowledge of finance —not a mere
visionary, but one who is accustomed to deal with
large figures, and who has a thorough grip of the
money questions of the world.” Just at that
moment, the conversation was interrupted by the
entrance of a little girl, in a pinafore. “ Well,”
said the prospective Treasurer, “what is it?”
“ Please, sir,” said she, “ can you give me change
of a shilling?” The man accustomed to deal in
large figures pulled open his till impatiently, then
banged it back ; “ No,” he cried, “ we haven’t got
it !”
H. S. W. Lawson, the newly-elected
member for Castlemaine (Vic.), has had the novel
experience of getting evon with his old school-
master, M‘Cay. The late member and very tem-
porary Minister used to teach young Lawson at
the Castlemaine college not many years ago, and
the pupil has now wiped off the score of all the
keepings-in and canings that M'Cay inflicted on
him. Lawson has just turned 23, and is conse-
quently the youngest member of the Vic. As-
sembly. In private life he is a law-student, and
he first came before the Castlemaine public as a
cricketer and footballer. He gave up both games,
however, a few months back, because he could no
longer endure the langwidge habitually used by
his fellow-players, and devoted himself to his
studies and tiie Sunday-school where he is a
teacher, until M‘Cay was unhappily chosen as a
member of the still-born “M’Shiels” Govt, fn
app arance, Lawson is a big, heavily-built young
fellow with a face that might be that of “ Bread-
or-Blood ” Vosper, minus all the hair and 20 of
the years.
In Christchurch (M.L.) the National Ass
party, after appealing to various Fat men to allow
themselves to be nominated for Parliament, had
to fall back on an .unknown, building contractor,
a devoted adherent of the Fatman beliefs. A
hall was taken, the meeting advertised, and on
the appointed night a goodly number rolled up
to hear the candidate expound the woes of the
capitalist and the iniquity of the working man.
In the chair was an obese brewer, and on the
platform numerous other persons with bald heads
and big bank-balances. But lo ! when the candi-
date stepped to the front and essayed to speak,
not a sound could he utter. He reached out and
desperately drained a glass of water — then a
second and a third—but still no.effect. Then a
man rushed out from the back and refilled the
empty jug ; and still the unhappy candidate
drank on, till at last speech came ; but there was
no “go” in it, and he drifted waterlogged to a
dreary conclusion. The Ass party intends to have
a tank of water on the stage the next time it tries
a new candidate.
There lias been much speculation in Vic-
torian political circles as to the reason of Watt’s
inclusion in the M'Lean Ministry. When the
censure motion was before the House he was
absent; he has a habit of being absent when fine
points are being decided. He gave no indication
how he would have voted had' he been present,
and it is common knowledge that his guide in the
House is Deakin. That gentleman colors almost
all his ideas and actions, and as Deakin paired in
favor of the Turner Government, and has since
gone into Opposition with them, people are saying
that Watt was given the Postmaster-Generalship,
which only lasts till Federation is accomplished,
in order to insure his support to the new Govern-
ment. Had a good Liberal been put up
against Watt he would have had a close shave.
Watt’s attitude on the proposed second South
African contingent from'Victoria is as sinuous as
all nature’s creations that crawl upon the belly.
He was against the first contingent because Eng-
land didn’t need Australia’s help to crush a 33rd
rate power. N.ow he is in favor of a second con-
tingent of 200 men at a time when England is
able to mobilise 50,000 extra troops for South
Africa at a few days’ notice. The reasoning is
poor but sufficiently strong to convince an ordin-
ary politician, always providing he gets a port-
folio with it.

Aboriginalities.
A large and imposing Yic. Western district
J.P., who regards himself with reverent solemnity,
got a peculiar moral jilt one recent morning. He
had risen to discharge homilies at a disorderly
conduct—a melancholy wreck with a nose like a
torn hat and an eye like a ripe gooseberry, who
was maundering behind the spikes, apparently
dead to the world. “We are determined to rid
this district of disreputable loafers of your stamp,”
said the J.P., weightily. “You are a danger to
defenceless people and a disgrace to your sex.
You have no regard for ” But just here
disorderly Conduct had a lucid interval ; the
ohieken-headed spider on the edge of the dock
ceased to interest him, and he fixed an alert eye
on the justice. “ Wha’—you here again ?” he
said, sternly. “ I (hie) fine you for’y shil’n ’r
sheven days, an’ (hie) don’-li-me-see-you-’ere-
again ! ” The J.P. gasped and collapsed, and the
policeman cried aloud in his agony, but Disorderly
Conduct was again absorbed in the blue centipede
and the carmine wood grub with the glass eye.
AUSTRALIAN ROYALTY.
Election candidates often drift into tight
places. One well-conditioned Maoriland legisla-
tor found himself caught in rough weather, after
receiving a thorough drenching, at a small farm-
house. The good-natured occupants at once
invited him to undress and get inside a spare pair
of moleskins and blue shirt of the house-father’s,
to which arrangement the man of talk eagerly
acquiesced. But, alas ! the moleskins positively
refused to meet—by inches —across the lower
chest of the capacious orator. When the M.H.R.
discovered into what straits he had fallen, nothing
remained for it but to draw the bedroom door
slightly ajar and inform the housewife point-blank
of the unhappy circumstance. “Bless me, hthat
all?” she remarked, taking off her apron and
handing it in; “ put that on ! ” A few moments
later the hon. member came forth with the stride
of a Caesar, and began, amid the family giggle, to
get outside a steaming dinner of pig’s counten-
ance and cabbage.
According to Treasurer Philp, Queens-
land is now possessed of 711 bores, an increase of
67 on last year’s total, while those flowing natur-
ally number 439, an increase of 60.' The daily
flow amounts to 268,000,000 gallons, enough to
cover 562 square miles to a depth of one foot an-
nually. Total cost, £1,044,822 ; so that for every
£1 of cash there is a daily supply of 256 gallons in
bad country, or 93,440ga15. per annum. Deepest
bore m Queensland is at Both well-4860ft. : the
shallowest at Manfred Downs, where there is a
permanent flow at 10ft. Average cost, 255. a foot
Average depth, 1175'6 feet.

Pelican : Re snakes jumping. Q.
nigger once warned writer never to try and kill a
dear-adder from the immediate rear. “That
fellow come back all same boomerang.” No other
kind of snake, however, he said, had the same
power. Also, re speed of snakes. Have killed
hundreds of various varieties, and have only twice
been gone for once by a tiger snake, other
time by a brown. Had no difficulty in either case
in getting away, though the country was bad for
pedestnanism.
* * * *
A common superstition (?) among miners
is that falls of earth or rock are more frequent
between the hours of 12 p.m. and 4 a.m. (the first
half of night shift) than during the whole of the
remaining 20 hours. Akin to this are the assertions
of many bushmen to the effect that timber falls to
a greater extent at night than during the day.
Also furniture and old houses only creak their
loudest between midnight and 4 a.m.

Dear Bulletin, —A fact that has puzzled
me for the past 10 years, during which I have
taught hundreds of sth. and 4th class boys.
Directly the melon season comes round, the boys’
drawings of circles get more and more melon-like
in their contour. I have at present about 50 boys
doing ’ the 3rd book of Euclid. At the be-
ginning of this quarter they drew circles on the
blackboard as round as “Giotto’s O,” but now
only about half-a-dozen draw reasonably good
circles ; and be it noted, none of these eat melons.
With the others, even the cane can do nothing, so
I want to know if any of your reading can throw
a «y light on the subject ? Same experience has
been mine in the Big Scrub—on the Hawkesbury
in Parramatta, and now in a Sydney suburb.—
Billy B.
* * • .
Gr.M. W.” : On Garibaldi Hill, near
Rutherglen (Vic.) lately came upon a bike-lady
who had pulled-up at sight of a monstrous snake
lying across a by-road, and was alarmedly waiting
for somebody to come along and kill it. ’Took the
buggy-whip, and advanced upon the foe, which
was about 9 feet long, remarkably slender and
apparently semi-torpid. Turned out to be a pro-
cession of large hairy caterpillars (78 in all) moving
tail-to-beak from one vineyard to another. In-
vited the bike-lady to come and see, but she
gathered up her skirts with an injured air, and
pedalled off without thanking me.
“ Fortuna ” : I remember, years
ago, when the aboriginals of Dungog
and Paterson (N.S.W.) were preparing
for a “ bora,” ambassadors were sent
out to inform neighboring tribes. These
spent days, sometimes a week, at each
camp, having a short discussion daily
with its males but rigidly excluding
gins from such. The ceremony called
“bnmbut” (“u” as in .“full”) was
performed with great secrecy, anti
seems to bear some analogy to our
baptism, as the youths initiated re-
ceived fresh names, never afterwards
using their former ones. Gins were
not allowed any knowledge of the pro-
ceedings under penalty of death. The
natives in those days wore only a belt
of ’possum-skin with fur suspended in
strips, and knotted in this fur they
often carried pieces of clear quartz
called by them “ murals ” and used to
test friendship, their acceptance by
another betokening amity. The women
were never allowed to see these.

“Double U.” : While a friend was re-
cently picking caterpillars off his grape-vines, he
asked me if I had ever seen an ant drunk, and,
selecting a fat caterpillar, he placed it near a few
ants (common black variety, about half an inch
long), which fastened on to it instantly. The
caterpillar squirmed and wriggled, until presently
ant No. 1 dropped off drunk, and staggered round
aimlessly for about half-a-minute. Then it seemed
to recover, and made a dash for more caterpillar,
just as another ant rolled off drunk. And so it
went on with all of them, so that there was all
the time at least one on the “flure.” But as the
caterpillar’s exertions slackened, the ants hung on
longer, and when they fell off recovered quicker,
until the merciful pressure of a No. 9 boot put an
end to the spree.
A TRUE BILL.
(On a sheep-station
in the far Nor’- West
where the nigger
flourisheth aloud and tends
his scattered flock by the placid cobble-stone river
Ashburton.)
Hard Case (to Squatter): “Can you give us a
job, boss?”
Squatter: “Maybe. Can you count sheep?”
H.C.: “No.”
SQ: “ Can you shear?”
H.C.: “ No.”
SQ.: “ Kill sheep?”
H.C.: “No.”
SQ: “ Drove? ”
H.C.: “No.”
S Q: “ Well, what the deuce can you do?”
H.C.: (showing his arm): “I can fight like hell.
SQ: “ Right. You can go as overseer at the back
of the run, and just see those boys don’t lose
any sheep. There’s your camp for to-night !”
“Ell.”: Have never seen any reference
to the young magpie’s enormous gorging-pnwer.
I had one which was always hungry, and squawk-
ing for more. Gave it on one occasion a chopped
up piece of meat, nearly three-times its own size,
which it swallowed in an incredibly short time,
and then didn’t appear disinclined for another go.
It consumed actually more meat than my large
dog.
* * * *
This story hails from near Swan Hill
(Victoria) and relates to a man on whose behalf
the cake for immovable calm is claimed by
enthusiastic neighbors. Bradbery is inhumanly
phlegmatic, and it is told that lie was patiently
fishing off a stump in the Murray on a re-
cent afternoon. He . had been fishing for
hours,' and had had neither bite nor sup, but
nothing ruffled his deadly calm. Two weeks or so
previously his wife Hannah had gone missing, but
that fact did not we'igh on his mind either; ltd
was simply a human vacancy so far as emotions
were concerned. At last, just after sunset, Brad-
bery hooked something, and cautiously drew to
the surface a length of skirt. Taking hold of this
he towed a body ashore. After looking at the
face in a. disinterested way for a few moments, he
said, “ Blowed if it ain’t Hannah 1 ” Then he
went on fishing.
* * * *
“ Werrimee ” : Re the frequently-made
statement that, nowadays, stations have stopped
giving rations. No outback station refuses
to sell rations ; very few refuse to give when
coin is not forthcoming.' It pays to be good-
natured. Stop 1 the rations where Stations
are far between, and only “ financial ” tra-
vellers will venture out, and these, being in a
manner independent, can refuse work until offered
suitable wages. 1 This would tend in time to in-
crease the rate of wages. So the rations are still
doled out to keep up competition. Again, free
meat is a certainty on any sheep-station, because
it pays the squatter better to give a “ banjo ”
than to risk having a sheep killed on the run for
the sake of a “ piece.” No regular traveller will
go minus meat through a sheep-run. Deorepid
old men may at times be refused on the
assumption that they are too slow to catch a juin-
buck. But let the traveller have a likely-looking
dog or a rifle, and he can ask for a sirloin.
Dear Bulletin. —Can any reader enlighten
me as to how scrub-tick poisoning in dogs
should be treated. In Cook district our
dogs with few exceptions sooner or later
fall a victim to the deadly little para-
site. Usual bush treatment
is to locate tick and carefully
detach it, but by then
the dog is generally
paralysed. He is then
dosed with carbonate
j>f soda and bled from
ears or tail. I have
recently seen two dogs
die under this treat-
ment, so I should be
glad to hear of some-
thing more promising.—

Not long ago a photo-enlarger called on an
Illawarra man for an order. The latter told him
be had “a small picture of his head and body,”
and that he’d be glad to have legs added. “ You
see,” said he, “I wouldn’t look well in a big
picture without ’em.” Picture-man said he’d
“see what he could do about it,” and got the
order. In due time he returned with the enlarged
photo.—without legs. The customer paid 255.,
and remarked T “ You didn’t put the legs on it! ”
“ No,” said the other, “ we couldn’t do it.”
“Well,” said the old fellow, “I’ll show you how
/can do it.” Then he put the picture on the
floor, jumped on it, and smashed it to smithereens.
“ 1 can put legs on the blamed thing !” said he,
“ and I’ll put a head on you quick if you’re
not outside.” And outside it was, promptly!

Maoriland, who took part in 40 engagements
and was six times wounded.
“ Werrimee” : Pindara Hut, onConnulpi'e
Downs (N.S. W.), is noted for its snakes (a young
man lies buried there who died in 20min. from
the bite of a salt-bush snake that had got into his
bed). A boundary-rider hit upon an ingenious
idea to warn him of the approach of a snake. In
each corner of the hut and across the doorway he
pegged a sheet of newspaper, crossing which a
snake makes noise sufficient to wake him. When
camping out on the sand, snake-tracks are often
found all around in the morning. They come
within a yard or so, crawl round a bit, and then
go straight away.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A tenement in Goulburn-street, where dwelt a fat old
Ohow
Who ran a fan-tan gambling hell (his name it was Ah
Gow)—
One Burglar Bill designed to loot; he swore he’d have
the beans,
Concealed within that tenement, by hook-or-crook-ed
means.
He forced a door and entered in, all by his little self,
And rummaged till he wondered where was planted all
the pelf;
Then found a score of one-pound notes, and turned to
look for more,
But saw a light show clear and bright through crack
beneath a door.
Now, Burglar Bill was startled some, but curious as well,
His eye he to the keyhole laid, and saw' it was the “ hell,”
With larrikins and Chinamen around a table square,
On fan-tau all intent—and he could hear the larries
swear.
Fan-tan it was a weakness old of this burglarious gent,
He wished himself inside the door against which now he
leant;
And, striving hard to see the game, he quite excited
grew,
He pressed too hard against the door and open wide it
flew.
He boldly walked within and put a pound upon the
“ four,”
And down he went upon the tray—he saw that pound no
more.
He played until he lost the notes of which he’d robbed
the Chow,
And then a blasted thief was what he called serene Ah
Gow.
Q.

“ K.8.J.”
re deadliness
of Australian
snakes (“Shaker,” 8., 21/10/’99) :
Recent. Melbourne experiments would seem to have
proved the inefiicacy of both the chloride and strychnine
treatmen's, which were vainly tried on bitten animals
death resulting in every case. The difficulty is that
snake-venom is a compound poison, and the medical ex-
perimenters assert that in many cases of supposed
“ cure” the bite would not in any case have proved fatal ;
the poison having been more or less absorbed by the
clothing of the person struck. The poison of the cobra,
however, being injected through a tube in the fang in-
stead of merely along a groove therein, always enters the
wound. In these experiments the “ snake-charmer,”
Davis, appeared to suffer no effect from a bite beyond a
slight swelling and soreness of the part. He would,
without hesitation, put his naked hand into the cage and
pull out the snake required, holding it by the neck. To
extract the venom the edge of a watch-glass was pushed
under the erected fangs, and the furious reptile would
e.xude the poison for use in the experimental injectures.

nie ” was insufficient. Whereupon John

H. J. Mac., in stating’that
a carpet-snake never attains a greater length
than 12 or 13ft. is confirmed by Krefft and
Gunther. But for general information will
the M'Cooey point out the generic distinc-
tions between the carpet-snake (morelia
variegata) and the monster “rock snake”
(basis chiidreni)? Also the difference be-
tween the carpet and diamond snake. This
would interest hundreds.

“A.C.”: Out beyond Lake Darlot
met a party of 20 bucks in war get-up. Fine-
ooking fellows they were, averaging 6ft., and
were well-armed and under-clothed. Indeed,
most of their apparel consisted of live lizards,
slung head-downwards. A chaplet of the
wrigglers encircled their heads, and they
stopped me —I am a girl, and was riding a
bicycle—to ask “ which way Preen (Breen)
walk.” He is a mounted constable, and a
terror to evil-doing nigs. The party were
bent on mischief to another tribe that had
offended them.

“ J.S.T.” : Be “ X.’s " par. (B.
21/10/ 99). I have also seen a dog, when
jumping a tussock, “ struck ” by a snake,
and the canine was dead inside half-an-hour.
Once also, when riding over an Upper Mur-
ray flat, a lead-colored reptile sprang at iny
horse, just missed, and was killed with a
stirrup-iron. I have often seen a black snake
lie quite still until a blow was made at him,
when he would draw back his head swiftly
and “ strike ” like lightning forward and
downward at the stick. It’s not safe, though,
to experiment thus, except with a long waddy.

From a notification of rules har-
pooned on t') the door of a Vic. country hall,
where fortnightly dances are given
Any gent, introducing any lady of doubtful char-
acter to these entertainments shall be fined sixpence,
and not allowed to dance until such fine be paid. Any
lady objecting to dance with any gent, against whose
character she can bring no charge, will, on complaint
being made to the M.C., have her invite immediately
cancelled.

“““ Snell ” : Jim-jams affect many men in
many ways, and the impression that every dipso,
suffering the recoil is visited by the same kind of
fantod is a popular error. True, the mild maniac
who lies still, and patiently picks mauve spiders
off the edges of his bunk, is frequently met with,
and there are certain kinds of crimson snakes
afflicting a numerous class ; but scores of liberal
drinkers have their pet particular and private
fantod, a sort of registered blue devil, all rights
reserved. One gentleman, whom I knew very
well, always signalised the beginning of the end
by persistently and patiently lighting matches.
He sat at his hut door, striking the vestas, and
watching the flame in a pained and troubled way,
and would get through a oouple of dozen boxes
before working off his attack of beer-fever. An-
other interesting drinker when possessed of devils
spent his time anxiously digging little holes with
a shovel, and very carefully burying nothing. A
third —a publican—when afflicted, would squat
out in the middle of a big cleared paddock, and
derive great satisfaction from monotonously
pounding a tin dish with a stick.

Australia for the Australians ! Latest,
official return of Thursday Island marine and
land populations
Japanese, 790; Filipinos, 251 ; Malays, 247; South
Sea Islanders, 208 ; Torres Strait Islanders, 174; Austra-
lian aborigines, 150; Europeans, 86 ;,natives of Rotumah,
67; Javanese, 52 ; Cingalese, 19 ; East Indians, 19 ; North
American negroes, 14; natives of Sanioa, 8 ; natives of
New Zealand, 7 ; natives of Africa, 7 ; South Americans,
7; West Indian negroes, 6; AsiaticsVArabs, &c., 5;
natives of New Guinea, 2; natives of Mauritius, 2;
natives of Hongkong, 1; natives, of China, 1. Total,
2132. This was the marine population of the
people living on Thursday Island itself. There were 574
Europeans, 440 Japanese, 103 Filipinos, 60 aborigines,
71 Chinese, 58 Malays, 44 South Sea Islanders, 54 Cin-
galese, 6 Javanese, and 105 of other mixed races.
The Thursday Island Outlanders should declare
war on Ooru Douglas for refusing them the fran-
chise. They have collared everything else.
HE KNEW.
Ist. Retired Missionary: “ You have been to the
South Seas. Were you ever at a cannibal-feast
and was it disgusting?”
2nd. R.M. (gloomily): “Fes — I was the feast.”
Said the gun-johnny (one of those dear
fellows who lose their heads—in the proper sense—
at the right moment, and for whom the family
have to go into mourning): “ I saw something
move amongst the bushes. I crept nearer. I saw
a tail distinctly. I fired. The rabbit scampered
away. ‘ I nearly shot a rabbit,’ I said, when I got
back to the house. ‘Did you ! ’ said my hostess,
fiercely, and the rest laughed. Then the truth
dawned upon me. But there is one consolation
for the hostess. Her cat is now the only ‘ manx ’
in the district! ”

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

was at The Australia.”

“ Maorilander ” to The Bulletin :
The recent death of Lt.-Col. McDonnell, of Maori war
fame, reminds me that some time ago a writer in an
English paper professing a good knowledge of such
matters, stated that he had neverseen the “ New Zealand
Cross ” (conferred upon McDonnell amongst others) and
even suggested that no such thing existed. The decora-
tion, however, was instituted by the M.L. Govt, as a
reward for deeds of bravery by the local forces, mem-
bers of which were not eligible for the “ bit of bronze"—
like which the N.Z. Cross carries a pension of £lO a year.
1 have seen at least five, three of them being owned by
Wanganui men (McDonnell, Lingard and Austin).
Nevertheless the standard authority on “War Medals and
Decorations ” (Hastings Irwin) contains no mention of
this one, though the records are carried down to the
Dargai V. O.’s of ’9B. And yet Premier Seddon has been
getting “ beans” for giving this “ For Valor” Cross to an
Aucklander, and the incident has found its way into
numerous English papers. In this same book I find
Ensign McKenna, V.C., 65th ltegt., tabulated as
“deceased,” whereas he is still very much alive in Maori-
land though .it is 35 years since he earned his Cross.
Many Bulletin readers over here would be glad to see
some correction made of these errors.
P. R. BEAUCHAMP,
Winner of the Austral Wheel Race, 1899.
Rev. W. Wollaston, of E. Melbourne, tells
some remarkable stories of the early days of Wes-
tralia, when he was a medico at Albany. The
convicts once broke into the Commissariat office
and stole £l5OO in gold, and for some time nc
trace of coin or thieves could be found. Dr. W.,
however, had a convict servant, a tailor by trade,
and Mrs. W., going one day to this man’s work-
box for a needle, heard something rattle inside,
and on examination found that the box had a
false bottom, under which was a lot of gold coin.
The tailor had to own up that he knew all about
the robbery from the Commissariat office, and
pointed out the place on the beach where the rest
of the stolen coin was buried. He and his accom-
plices got ten years, which, as they were already
“lifers,” amounted only to the loss of their
tickets-of-leave. One day Dr. W. was out in the
bush, and heard a convict bullock-driver exhaust
his vocabulary on his team that had got boxed up
in the scrub. At length the driver went up to
the leader and knelt down—perhaps, thought Dr.
W., to pray. But, no—it was to bite a hunk out
of the bullock’s nose. This choice morsel cost
him six months’ gaol. A few days after the man’s
conviction Dr. W. found the family dog—a collie
for which .£3O had been refused —hanged in the
back yard.

LATEST WAR NEWS


A curious sea-rescue was that of a seaman-
of a schooner on a recent voyage from Newcastle-
to Sydney. While aloft, helping to shorten saiJ r
the man fell overboard, whereupon the captain,
as the vessel .swept past, flung the peak halliards;
overboard with such good aim that the bight
las.sooed the swimmer and enabled him to' be
hauled on board.

In 1872 one Wilkins drifted into the
Transvaal. lie was an Englishman of the
English. Also, he was a dour, taciturn indivi-
dual, who hardly spoke a word from one week’s
etid to the other. He became friends with a
worthy young Boer named Hans Botha, and they
became partners. When Theophilus Shepstone
annexed the Transvaal, Wilkins astonished his
partner by entering the house with a wild yell,
seizing the Dutchman's Heavy paw, and con-
gratulating him on becoming an Englishman.
When the Republic was declared at Heidelberg
(wherein Wilkins and Botha were, located),
Wilkins stalked into the house, took his pipe
from his mouth, cocked his head on one side, and
said to Botha, “ Briton or Boer, Botha ? ” Botha
removed his long pipe and replied simply,
“Boer!” Wilkins put his pipe back into his
mouth and proceeded to halve everything in the
place, Botha spasmodically helping him. When
everything was halved, Wilkins took his share of
the stock, and, loading up his goods, trekked into
Pretoria, the Boers never dreaming of interfering
with Botha’s mate. He was a most deadly shot;
and when he got into Pretoria he was at once
made commander of a company of volunteers.
One morning he informed the haughty Owen
Lanyon that the Boers were in a bad position at
Black Kopje, and could be attacked and beaten
at little cost, and that a considerabk quantity pf
stock could be brought in at the same : irne. Next
morning the troops moved out. WiJ . ins and his)
company were to engage the Boers with the rifle,
and make the way clear for a storming party to
rush the position. The attack began, and Wilkins
cursed with delight because he had discovered by*
some means that his old friend Botha was in
command of the Boers. When they got into
position Wilkins hoisted himself up to see if there
was anything to be shot at, and got a bullet
through the fleshy part of the left arm. Botha
had shot him. Botha hoisted himself up to see if
there were any more, and got a bullet in exactly
the same place. Then there was silence, and
Botha put his hat on a stick and held the rim just
above the rock. Every volunteer fired at it, and
as the reports died away, up bobbed Botha and
got another shot somewhere or other. And so it
went on till the storming party got their oppor-
tunity and took the position with a rush. When
the wounded were brought into hospital, Botha
and Wilkins found themselves in adjacent beds. A
faint smile of welcome flitted over each face, and
Wilkins said, “ How many, Botha? ” “Seven ! ”
said Botha—he was wounded in seven places.
“ Done you.” said the silent Wilkins in triumph,
tapping himself with his uninjured arm ; “Only
six.” And the other day came news that Com-
mandant Hans Botha had got his eighth and died
before Kimberley. One wonders if his old friend
and enemy, Wilkins, fired that one too.

The Big Prize Turkey.
A Merry Christmas, Bob,” said Scrooge, with
an earnestness that could not be mistaken, as he
clapped him on the back. “A merrier Christmas,
my good fellow, than I have given you for many a
year. 111 raise your salary, and we will discuss
your affairs this afternoon over a bowl of smoking
bishop. Make up the fires and buy another coal-
scuttle, Bob Cratchit.”
And as to the big prize turkey which the regene-
rated Scrooge sent to Camden Town, do we not all
know what sort of bird it was? That turkey never
could have stood upon its legs, Mr. Dickens says.
I hey must have snapped short off like sticks of
sealing-wax. How they managed to cook and cat
that phenomenon the author doesn’t say, as it
was twice the size of Tiny Tim. Yet they did it
somehow, and it was a mercy if the family
(previously half starved) didn’t all fall ill of the
unwonted gorging of that wonderful Christmas
day.
Even assuming (as we easily may) that the
transmutation of an old skinflint like Scrooge into
a high priest of charity was quite beyond the com-
mon run of modern miracles, and that dinners from
such sources are as rare as the y are welcome, it
still remains true chat human nature is apt to he
at its best at Christmas, especially in England,
where, of all countries in Christendom, most is
made of that gentle and kindly holiday.
Yet, sad to tell, the very jollity and generosity
of Christmas leads to regretable excesses. People
eat and drink too much, and suffer accordingly.
The roast and the boiled, the mince pies, the plum
puddings, the tremendous flushings of wine, beer,
and other beverages, these things overload the
stomach and give the digestion more work than it
can do, and punishment follows. For Nature has
no favourites; she treats those who violate her
laws on the 25th December exactly as though they
had committed the offence on the first Monday in
August.
And as to the chronic dyspeptics, of whom there
are enough in this old island to cram all the parks
in London, they catch it fearfully about Christmas-
time. For, in spite of all warnings, they will go in
for heavy meals and take the chances. Often the
penalty is an acute attack which may threaten life,
and nearly always weeks of pain and digestive
disorders, whereof there are as many kinds as the
body has organs and functions.
To them Christmas maybe “merry”for an hour
or two ; then their merriment turns to misery, as
October fogs and rains succeed a hot summer.
Now the writer is far from advising a free in-
dulgence in the gustatory and bibulous festivities
of the country’s most popular holiday, but there
is no use protesting against the fixed resolution of
the people to enjoy themselves on that occasion.
The customs and traditions of centuries are behind
it, and you might as well whistle in the teeth of a
north-east gale.
Asa prophylactic and a cure for the evils alluded
to, I can only recommend the universal use of
Mother Seigel’s Syrup, known to all as the best
stimulant and digestant in the world.
In a letter dated January 19, 1899, a gentleman
of Birmingham, who loves the pleasures •of the
table, and has money enough to buy whatever he
wants, writes thus “It is only fair that I should
thank you, as I did with all my heart, for the good
done me by Mother Seigel’s Syrup. For years I
have been vexed with indigestion, and always had
a tormenting spell of it after any public banqueb
or uncommon intake of rich food. Still, I was
seldom able to resist temptation, although I under-
stood perfectly well what yielding to it was sure to
cost me. I have sought a preventive or a cure in
vain at the hands of the best physicians, all of
vyhom assured me that there was but one way
namely, to live on a low diet, and let luxuries
alone.
“ But this did not suit me, and I broke through
the regulations frequently, with the inevitable
results. However, when I began to use Mother
Seigel’s Syrup I had (almost) a license to do as I
pleased. After an inviting but most risky dinner
last Christmas I took a double dose of ‘ Mother
Seigel,’ and scarcely suffered at all. I told my
experience, and a. dozen friends adopted this
amazing remedy right off. lam convinced that,
with a little caution, and a dose of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup now’ and then, one may bid defiance to
dyspepsia and the abominable ailments it sets up.
Thanking you a thousand times, lam, yours truly,
John Macfarlane.”
With these facts we wish a merry (and a
healthy) Christmas to the British people.
In the days 'when John (say) Smith was
member for a certain N.S.W. constituency, a
butcher, whose name we’ll say was Jones, took
the show prize offered for best round of beef.
Smith bought the bit of prize bullock, and took
it home to Sydney. Some years afterwards, when
addressing his constituents, and enumerating the
blessings contained in a new Land Bill he ua~.
helped to pass, he was interrupted by Jones,
with, “But what about the old Bill?” John
pointed out its defects, all of which he assured
them were remedied in the new one. “ That’s all
right, Jack,” said Jones, “but what about the
other old Bill ?’’ “What other old Bill ? ” “Why,
my old bill—for that prize round.” “Oh, I’m
real glad you reminded me of that,” said Jack,
“and I'll send you a cheque in the morning—
9s. Od., wasn’t it?” “No fear, Jack, one
pound nineteen / ” “ Oh, very well,” answered
John, with his painful smile, “but your beef
wasn’t like your bill; it didn’t increase with age.”
A deputation of clergymen and others
waited upon N.S.W. 001. Sec. See the other day
to request him to shut up, or regulate, or do
something very severe and drastic with the
“darnee” saloons of Sydney. A letter from
Archbishop Smith wa3 read. That astute prelate
stated that he thought the saloons might be
allowed to continue their existance if placed
under police supervision. How would it work ?

A National Cash Register protects
the cash
The register is a place of safe deposit for the in-
coming cash, that can be opened only with sign and
signal. It is an automatic receiving and paying
cashier under mechanical bonds for faithfulness and
accuracy.
It protects the customer.
It shows instantly to every customer a record of
his transaction and gives him a printed voucher for
the same.
It protects the shopkeeper.
It makes an open record of every transaction and
preserves these records for inspection, furnishing
ready data for balancing the day’s work.
It provides the readiest means of discovering errors
and-locating responsibility for them.
It puts dishonesty in danger of detection.
It protects the books-
It is a witness against the one who “ forgot to make
the entry.”
It protects the assistant-
It safeguards the rights of employees by making
every assistant responsible for only his own trans-
actions and filing a record of the same which can be
referred to in case of doubt.
If an error appears, it is better for some one to be
convicted of a mistake than for anyone to be sus-
pected of crime.
It makes occasional purchasers
regular customers-
The paper check which it issues for each sale is a
drawing-card. The printed promise on this check of
a premium when cash purchasers have reached a
certain amount will bring the customer back in order
to reach that amount, until coming has become a
habit and tbe occasional customer has become a
regular patron.
It causes credit customers to buy
for cash-
With its check system offering a discount for cash,
it encourages cash buying and so reduces the risks of
accounts. The system may also be extended to favor
weekly payments instead of long accounts, and so
solve the somewhat difficult problem of the good
but slow credit customer.
Prices from £5 to £6B.
Full information
on application to
National Cash Register
COMPANY.
393 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
W. A. LING HAM, General Manager.

tion with the Vic. Lands Dept, helped to break

to the Post and Telegraph Dept, whence he came.
His path of glory led but to the Graves Enquiry
Board.
Cronjes force, which beat Methuen at
Magersfontein, is largely composed of boys. The
Boers have every male capable of bearing artrs to
front the invader, while the women and children
mind the cattle, sow the seed and gather the
crops.
Some disloyal ruffian visited the facade of
•,he Melb. Argl’S office last week and looted the
big war-map of all the little union-jacks pinned
on to show the positions of the British troops.
Next morning an astonished public found the
whole country in the possession of the Boers.
The Boers are fighting a War of Inde-
pendence, if ever there was one. The British, so
enthusiastic over Garibaldi and Kossuth, do not
jee it that way. But then patriotism is only
patriotism when it opposes some other nation. If
Garibaldi were a Boer, he would be a rebel and a
traitor.
The N.S.W. Council decided that Premier
Lyne’s Stamp Duties Bill required such an enor-
mous amount of thinking over it would be unfair
to pass it this session. So Plain Bill Lyne will
lose part of the revenue he has been counting on,
while his war expenditure will be at least <£30,000
more than originally expected, and the good old
ihortage looms ahead as usual.

Asked the Minister for Public Instruction what

Kruger, and not one knows enough about Trans-

Though the jingo party in Australasia
Cheerfully assumes that the extensive volunteering
for the Transvaal is a proof of this country’s
loyalty, it isn’t necessarily anything of the kind.
If anybody advertised for a man at the same
wages to undertake a plain, steady, dreary job at
pushing a barrow under a hot sun, with no glory,
no uniform, no excitement, no brass band, no
girls to see him off, no blessing of his country
bestowed upon him, no insertion of his name and
photo, in the paper, no Patriotic Fund, no
insurance on his life, no sea-trip anywhere and
back, no chance of seeing a new land, no possi-
bilities of Victoria Cross and promotion and
pension—even then the volunteering would be
very considerable in this miserably impoverished
land. A year ago the rush to paint Sydney
Domain railings was almost as large and wild as
the rush to the Transvaal is now.
In M.L. every candidate has to file a state-
ment of exfienses incurred during his candidature,
as a check on possible bribery. George Fisher, of
Wellington, sends in his statement thus:
“Receipts nil. Personal expenses. Os; one packet
cigarettes, 6d. The above include the whole of mv
expenses.”
Now reported that the British forces in
South Africa are to be raised to 150,000 men—
which, if it comes off, makes this the biggest
military effort in British history. The next
biggest effort was probably when England and
Scotland raised 130,000 men between them, and
battered each other at Bannockburn.
Christchurch (M.L.) constituency, after
three years trial of Prohibitionist Apostle Tommy
Taylor, has given the young man in a hurry a rest.
Prohibition hasn’t done well this election, and the
Clammy Bun Party has, to a great extent, with-
drawn the light of its small pale countenance and
its chastened shirt collar from the Legislature.
After the battle at Modder River, the
leading article in Newcastle (N.S.W.) Herald
spoke of Cronje’s army as being composed of the
‘gaol birds, cut-throats, and scoundrels of the
Western frontier.’ It would have been very
little credit to the British armies to have driven
an assemblage of “gaolbirds, out-throats, and
scoundrels ” before them like sheep, but when the
British forces could only fight at best a drawn
battle with this alleged mob of ruffians it is surely
a low-dovvn way to speak of the crack regiments
of the British army.
According to present appearances, the
George-street (Sydnej - ) electric tramway is going
to be a gorgeously profitable affair, and the earn-
ings, added to the profits of other lines, should
enable the Sydney trams in general to 6how a
large margin over expenses and interest. Under
these circumstances, perhaps the Government
will think hard over the advisability of using the
profits to pay off the debt incurred when the trams
were first constructed, that debt being still in a
fine state of preservation. Melbourne tramways
are steadily paying off their original cost out of
earnings, and if Sydney can’t do the same what is
the matter with Sydney ?
Remarkable race, the Boers ! Men who

haven’t got them. Nevertheless, the Imperial

actually engaged in fight-

with the Natal and Cape
volunteers. Either the
Boers have a telesco-
pic army ; or—can it

cooked.

S.A. Leg. Council defeated Household
Suffrage, last week, by 11 votes to 10. Two pro-
nounced opponents were away, or the majority
would have been three. Ex-Premier Kingston’s
latest move to secure his cherished reform is to
stand for the Council, and he has finally decided
to contest the Southern division against Tory
leader Stirling’ l , next March.
Solomon’s was the shortest-lived Cabinet
that even S.A. can show. Baker’s Ministry lasted
11 days. That of Torrens, who succeeded Baker,
held out 29 days, Dutton’s first Cabinet lasted
11 days ; and Ayers’ second Government 13 days.
Ayers’ third Government hung out 33 days;
Hart’s second Cabinet, 18 days ; and Ayers’ fifth
Ministry, 20 days. Strangways’ second govern-
ment held out 18 days. So Sudden Solomon
holds the record even in S.A.
Prendergast relied upon the opposition of
the Aok to put him in for Footscray, as it had
put in Hancock at last general election, but appa-
rently tile people are behind the Age again. Han-
cock, used to. tell a little fable of his own at his
election meetings: “The Age says I am insup-
portable, and I haven’t its support. I thank God
for that. Once an elephant saw a poor snail try-
ing to climb a pole. ‘Sir,’ he said, ‘ you have our
fullest support,’ and he gave the snail a push up
that wiped him right out. I don’t want to be
supported like that. If I don’t get to the top of
the poll, I want at least to be somewhere dis-
coverable when it’s all over.”
Cabled that many Boers are trekking to
Western South Africa, viz., Damaraland and
Naniaqua Land, the large German possession
where the unspeakable Kalahari Desert blasted
the Teutons’ early colonial aspirations. The
whole vast tract has, up to date, been nothing but
an expense to Germany, especially as the only
decent harbor, Walfish Bay, is, as per usual,
British-owned. But with material such as these
hardy voortrekkers would furnish, relatives of
the Germans, too, there is no saying what the
future of. the possession may be. Also,
German Last Africa ought to come in for
some valuable immigration, and on the
vvlude the present war will do much good
to Germany, by infusing sturdy blood into
her aiiceinic, artificially-fed dependencies.
The continental press jubilated over
recent smacks in the eye for the British,
and “ the Austrian press in particular
ridiculed the recklessness of Gen. Gat-
acre in allowing his small force to be
drawn into a trap.” Nevertheless the con-
tinental press is not an authority on youth
A friesn warfare, and the chances are that
the British, clumsy as they seem in contest
with the Boeys,could run rings round any sort
of mechanical German or Austrian force among
the rocky hills of Cape Colony and Natal.
Anyhow, the British seem to have captured
a few score Germans at Elands Laagte whilst
their more mobile comrades, the Boers, were
getting away.
The more one looks at the seizure of
Drake by the Philp cabinet the more unac-
countable the business becomes. Unless, the
converted politician above-mentioned could
take with him a section of his party to aid
the Government, his secession from the
Opposition . was absolutely .useless to the
Ministry, inasmuch as the seat he resigns
in the Lower House is a democratic stronghold
and almost sure to revert to the Labor Party.
It looks very much as if Philp and co. have
fallen in badly over the transaction. A general
election will undoubtedly take place next year—
the present position is insupportable.

The question of Alien labor, in relation to
the, Provincial and Federal Parliaments respec-
tively, is very imperfectly understood in Queens-
land. The facts (much misrepresented by the:
press) stand thusly: The Commonwealth Parlia-
ment can take it up; so can the State Par-
liament ; but, in case of difference, the de-
cision of the Federal Parliament will, of course,
override that of the Provincial Legislature.

is poor mottled Bananaland.
The M‘Lean (Vic.) Ministry has already
started crumbling. Ministerof Education M'Cay
went up for re-election on accepting office, and
Castlemaine refused, by 310 majority, to have
anything further to do with him. And Fink, the
“intelligent man,” who was to help Sfiiels at the
Treasury, after hearing the new Treasurer going
it strong at Bairnsdale, handed in a disgusted
resignation. Shiels’s large and shrieky way of
putting things, and his wild comparisons of the
plain, steady-going Turner to Cam and Judas
Iscariot and other blood-stained persons, con-
vinced Fink that his new colleague lacked repose
of manner, and was too loud a person for any
ordinary man to get on with.
One honest and decent act of the Lyne
Government is it® attempt to remedy a gross in-
justice done to many N.S. W. civil servants a few
years ago. Under the old law, every N.S.W.
public servant had to contribute to the Super-
annuation Fund, with the right to retire on pen-
sion at the age of 60. If he left before the age of
60 he lost his pension-rights. And, in Reid’s
time, a brilliant piece of economy was effected by
firing out a number of old public servants a few
days, or weeks, or months before they reached
the pension-age, and then denying them their
pensions, and putting them off with a compara-
tively small gratuity, because.they didn’t stay in
till they reached 60. It was a smellful piece of
economy, and it can’t be put right too soon.
Never before in Vic. history has a Pre-
mier’s policy-speech fallen so flat as the one de-
livered by Allan M‘Lean at Bairnsdale. The dull
thud with which he dumped down the wretched
thing lias made everybody tired. The dark
secrecy which surrounded the whole business
to the very last gave folk the impression
that a great and dashing stroke of policy was to
be disclosed. But it was only the cheap gloom
which stage-manager Shiels adores. All that
M'Lean said could be condensed, without unfair-
ness, into a sentence. “I intend to take up the
legislation of Turner just where I snapped it off
by my want of-confidence motion.” There is noth-
ing in the M'Lean programme which Turner has
not already half finished. The most apparent dif-
ferenfce is that the strong man has gone, and the
two weak men have come.

Edwards, proprietor of Bendiuo Inde-
pendent, is a local J.P., and never misses a
morning at the police-court. Before him recently
came one of Bendigo’s mining kings, charged.
with riding a bicycle on the footpath. In vain
did the reefer (who conducted his own case) claim
that there was no footpath within the meaning of
the Act on - that particular road ; Edwards
recorded a fine of ss. The man of money shelled
out growlingly, and waited for Edwards, whom
he knew well enough in private life. “ That's all
right,” he said to the court, “ you fined me ss.
and now you’re going to pay that fine yourself. I
knock off the Independent to-morrow until the
money’s made up.” And for 60 days no news-
paper was deli vered at his house.
The private office of a certain monstrously
holy Melbourne magnate, who distributes Bibles
among his factory-girls, is an object lesson in
piety. Prayer-books jostle against price-lßts on
the desk, tracts are sandwiched among the latest
patterns, while over the chair of the head of the
firm hangs an illuminated text, “ Heaven First.”
One day X. was seated in his sanctum when
another softgnods man, just ns big in his way,
was ushered in a trifle “under the influence.”
The bibulous bagman glanced round the apart-
ment until he caught sight of the text oyer X.’s
head. Then he extended a jerky forefinger at it,
and scandalised the pious warehouseman by de-
manding huskily, “ Weren’t there any other
starters ? ”
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
