The Bulletin gladly receives the last two
volumes of the Victorian Statutes, edited by Mr,
Horwitz, from the publishers Messrs. Sands and
M'Dougall, Limited. Vol. VIII. is devoted en-
tirely to the Supreme Court Act and the rules
thereon—and there are a host of them. Some are
dead, but most of them are living, and the man
who has read this volume of the Statutes can
paralyse any Victorian Judge by firing off at him
2000 cases. From which it will be seen that the
vol. is a sort of legal maxim gun. Without the
“cases” it was a formidable weapon, but with
2000 of them —well, it’s invincible. Vol. IX.
covers the Acts from Temperance Halls to
Wrongs. Wrongs is divided into two parts. Part
I.—Defamatory Words and Libel. Part ll.
Wrongful Act or Neglect Causing Death. It is a
pleasant thing to find that neglect causing death
is a wrong. Vol. IX. also contains an index of
Privy Council appeals, a historical table of Legis-
lation for and in Victoria (including old 4-ots
passed in N.S.W.) with a note opposite each as to
whether it is still living, and if not what Act
killed it. Then there is an analysis of contents
of Acts contained in Vols. I to IX. with the re-
pealed sections carefully omitted. The work con-
cludes with a general index of all the cases cited
in the whole nine vols. Altogether it is a great
work, and it is pleasant to learn that the Vic.
Government has assisted by purchasing 100 ifets
for use in the courts and departments. The Bul-
letin now rises to carefully lock its set in its book-
case, and to wonder how so gigantic an under
taking was put through in so short a time.

In the ’6o’s, the late Lachlan M*Gowan once took part
at the old Victoria Theatre in Sydney, in a performance
of Richard the Third, which rejoiced in no less ihan five
Richards -one for each act; to wit, M’Gowan himself, J.
P. West, J. J. Walsh, Win. Andrews, and Janies Simmons!
There were not, however, five Richmonds in the field, but
only one, the thunder-toned “Ned” Holloway.
Veteran Cathcart has one excellent precept that he
dins into his pupils : “ Remember that somewhere up at
the hack of the gallery on the last seat, amongst the cob-
webs, there is a small boy who has paid sixpence to hear
the lines. Think of that small boy while you are on and
speak up.”
A rather well-known Australian manager lately pro-
duced “ His Natural Life ” at a southern town. There
was no property man, but the actor, who had the pro-
perty-man’s duties thrown on him got this list of essen-
tials : Prologue: Furniture for room —carpet, letter
cup of tea, pocket-book.and papers, hunting-crop. Act
1 : Heap of stones, napping-hammers, pickaxes, tobacco,
handcuffs, revolver, knife, flask. Act. 2 : Rock-piece,
book, basket of provisions, loaf of bread, rum-bottle!
piece of wood, twigs, large stone, axe, bag of sawdust!
frame of boat, hides. Act S: Wicker chairs and table,
stick, ribbon, newspaper, pair handcuffs. Act 4 : Furni-
ture, carpet, decanter of alleged whisky and glasses. Act
5 : Triangle, pickaxe, rack, ropes, flask, cat-o’-nme-tails,
shpath-knife. The management reluctantly gave him a
shilling with which to buy anything he couldn’t borrow,
and told him not'to “blew” the bob recklessly. He
spent Id. on a bit of liquorice to represent the tobacco
that Parson. North divides among the convicts.
The foreman of a road-repairing job lent the picks
and hammers in return for a free pass, and. the hotel
landlady lent some tables and chairs, a decanter, and
a basket. Some real, rocks were carried in from
a paddock. The. carpet was cut out, also the victuals,
handcuffs and most other things. A cart-whip was
borrowed to flog Kirkland with, and for. want of the
triangles that victim howled in the wings while the whip
cracked. Rufus Dawes wasn’t brought in on the rack;
he was carried in and dropped on the floor. The boat
was left out, and old D vine, having no hunting-crop,
threatened his son with one Of the chairs. And'when the
lid. was handed back the management complained
bitterly that one of the pennies was battered.
“ Melb.” :■ An ex-Orangeman writes in the Catholic
Advocate on the ceremonies of Orange initiation
as he says they are practised in Australia. He says the
candidate for the Royal Arch Purple Degree is divested
of all clothing but. his trousers. He is blindfolded, and
has to walk barefoot oh broken road me;tal. A crash of
swords on the floor denotes .the “ wrath of God on the
children of Disobedience.” The trembling oandidate
feels the point of a sword at his breast. He is severely
beaten with green boughs. While walkitrg'the Milestone
he is tripped up by a man lying across, and conies down
with various degrees of severity and bruise according to
his height and weight. He has to stand on a table, off
which he is pushed suddenly into a tarpaulin, wherein
a number of brethren toss him, and finally let him
down on the floor with a thud. He has to take an oath
that he will not betray “the wife, sister, or daughter
of a brother Arch Purpleman.” Sundry other mum-
meries are detail- d by the writer, who appears to be
taking a very cheerful aiid interesting rise out of a
credulous generation.

Brough had a very different tale to tell when the cur-
tain fell on the last performance of his latest Melbourne
Season. On the previous similar occasion the people
called up the plush, and Robert, looking down and de-
pressed, stepped forward and dropped a few meaningless
words, thinking he was done with the city for ever and a
day. This time he was buoyant and smiiing, and talked
of a quick return. R.B. did not talk on this occasion of
shaking the dust of the city from his feet, having a large
quantity of it in his pocket.
Now that the “Three Musketeers” is done to death,
someone should dramatise Dumas’ sequel, “ Twenty
Years After." Miladi’s son would make a blood-curdling
villain, and some of the scenes are highly dramatic. But
D’Artagnan and his mates at the wrong side of 40 would
probably fail to draw.
When Lane (husband of the .well-known Sarah, recently
deceased in London), first opened the Hoxton Britannia
Theatre, he instituted, “No re-admission without repay-
ment,” and on that account- aimed at furnishing, inside
the building, everything his patrons could possibly want.
One Saturday night he saw an elderly woman re-admitted
without a ticket, and on enquiring reason, was told by
the door-keeper that the woimn had forgotten to provide
her kindling-wood for next day, and that, as she was
a regular customer, he had strained a point when she re-
turned from getting it. “ What! ” said Lane, “ have we
no kindling-wood?” “No, Sir.” “Then, get in a couple
of loads at once.”
Cabled lately that at a Parisian bull-fight Taurus
leaped in among the audience and routed them with great
slaughter. According to mail accounts, the bull merely
wished to get away from the two-legged fiends who
were chasing him with swords and 1.-inces. He cleared
the palisade at a bound, entered a gangway, and climbed
two rows of seats. The panic was awful. The audience
climbed over each other, and screeched so that the hull,
unable to believe that he w-as the cause of it all, stopped
to try and reason it out. He was looking round him in
a scared sort of way when a woman seized him by one
horn and tried to poke her parasol into his eye. The hull
shook her oT, fell down two rows of seats, and finally
got away i.ito the paddock in which the arena stands. It
was nearly filled with bicycles, and his lordship amused
himself by tossing them up into the air and watching
them come down whack. He then proceeded to make a
light lunch off the short green grass, when he found
himself attacked by a horde of picadors, chulas, mata-
dors, toreros, gendarmes, and policemen armed with
revolvers. He was promptly wounded in many places,
got tangled up in the fence, and was stabbed to death.
Cow-prodding seems to be taking root in France—
another sign, probably, of national decadence.
In the good old “ Pinafore ” days the Josephine of a
certain co. was travelling as Sir Joseph’s wife, and great
was the public surprise when it was announced that she
was about to wed Capt. Corcoran (at that time Clement
Scott had not written his painful remarks about mum-
mers’ morality). A large crowd assembled at the
registry-office, and, owing to the bride’s peculiar posi-
tion, the C.P.S. determined to be very strict in his
inquiries. “Ever been married before?” he asked the
groom. “ Yesh, losh times.” “How many?” “Can’t
’member ’em all now. Jus’ put er down at a few—hie—
shever’l times,” said the groom. After an average had
been struck of the groom’s previous marriages, the
bride’s turn came. “ Ever been married before?” “ No,
sir.” “What! Never?” said the C.P.S. “Well,
h-h-hardly ever,” stammered the smiling bride. That
night, she and Sir Joe went through a trunk they had
used in common, and divided their “prop’s” quite
amicably. There was a packed house, and “ Pinafore ”
ran an extra week, thanks to the change of husbands and
the artless Josephine’s little joke.

Victor Trnmper is, without doubt, the best
batsman in Australia at the present time. His 165
against S.A. in Adelaide was one of the finest specimens
of cricket skill the writer has seen, quite rivalling Hill’s
display against England in Adelaide last March. Clem,
bv the way, is in no form at all just now ; he took 80
minutes to make 46 in S.A.’B first innings. Noble is, un-
doiihtedly second only to Trumper in batting. New
South Wales is profiting from her wise policy of intro-
ducing young players into her team. Her five juniors in
the 'natch against S.A. are, without exception, fine all-
round players. Already S.A. critics are advising the
younger province to follow the example. Lyons has
clearly shot his bolt. He is an unreliable batsman and a
very weak point. F. Jarvis could also be dropped with
advantage. Young players like Claxton, Leak, and
teams® 1, * lave simply had to force their way into S.A.

George Giffen’s average of eight wickets for 287
out of a total of 807 again proves what wonderful stamina
the veteran possesses. He is S.A.’s only bowler once

• • • •
With young players like Trumper, Hopkins,
Noble, P,\e and Collins coming on, it would seem that
Australia has no need to fear the assaults of England for
a year or two. Cricket was never in such a strong
position as it is in Australia just now. Men like Iredale,
Jones, Worrall and Gregory will have to maintain all
their old form, or they will be pushed out by the
youngsters next season.

Critics are discussing the policy of inviting a
veteran like Lord Hawke to lead the next English cricket
team to Australia. But the question is not altogether
one of mere capability on the cricket field. This paper
quite expected that most of the next English cricket
team for Australia would come from Woolwich and would
be led by Wolseley, Buller, or “ Bobs.” Kanji and Stod-
dart went Ome with such tales of Australian barracking
and insults that one would have expected the next com*
bination to fetch along a battery of field artillery and
maxun guns in order to bring the crowd to reason at the
beginning of each innings.

Parthian, at Randwick, on Saturday, further
proved his claim to be considered one of the best three-
year-olds of his year by running away with the Grand
btand Stakes. Report said the colt had not been doing
over well on the training-track, and, as the party did not
support hi:n in the betting, the rumor carried weight
and he started at 7to 1. Voyou started an equal favorite
with Gauleon, but neither showed prominentlv, and Par-
thian won easily from Flodden. Voyou broke‘down, and
has probably run his last race.

News that Goldsbrough—Ethel horse Brock-
leigh impaled himself on a fence atTumut (N.S.W.) and
died, brings to memory the sensation he created in
Sydney six years ago. Starting a hot 6to 1 favorite for
Epsom mile, with Jim Gough up and 8.10 in the saddle,
Broc-kleigh easily got first past the post by 3 lengths in
1.403 from a field of 24 ; Joe Abrahams’Little Bernie was
second. Owner of winner raked in three thousand, which
he had to take at a comparatively short price. Things
looked blue for the ring, as. Brockleigh was coupled ?n
doubles with almost everything in the Metrop. One
big punter and patron of Kelso’s stable, now (like
many who preceded and followed him) gone to
the wall, netted a tidy sum straight out and
had the same horse (as the second leg of a double)
going in the mile and a-half event for £6OOO. The night
before the Metrop. he backed Brockleigh for several
thousands more from sixes down to two’s. Ikeys
followed him to his suburban hotel offering 6 to 4 against
the hot pot getting a place, and booked further big
wagers. Most punters who had won over the Epsom
plunged on Brockleigh for the Metrop., until it looked as
if the breaking up of the ring was going to be accom-
plished at last. Brockleigh started, 6 to 4 and 2 to 1
against, and, after being interfered with twice, was
brought down by Pharamond pulling back on to him ;
Little Bemie and Salutation also fell. All sorts of ugly
reports were in circulation regarding the occurrence.
The big hacker referred to was never seen on a racecourse
afterwards. Paris (100 to 3 against) won, bringing much
jubilation and sacks of shekels to the camp of the leather-
lunged.

Maoriland trotting authorities were puzzled a
while back when tracing the antecedents of an alleged
“ringer.” They ultimately discovered that after the
moke had won a trot at one town he scored a galloping
race at another, the following week, in his proper name.
General-utilitv horses of tnis description are scarce.

The tot.i lisa tor—and let all its advocates stick
to this point -is essentially a democratic institution. It
puts the cunning trickster on the same level with the
simple, honest sport. “ One Man one Price ”in the tote.
It spares the punter the trouble of rushing around from
one noxious bookmaker to another, and the subsequent
anguish of discovering that he didn’t invest his coin at
the best available odds—for not once in a dozen tries does
he get on “ top of the market ” with Ikey Mo. The tote
deals out a fair chance to every player, and devotes the
41 kitty” to the encouragement of honest sport.

As a rule the turf pigeon stands plucking with-
out a squeal, but an exception has turned up in the
author of a 70-page booklet, entitled “ Australian Turf
Infamies, published in Sydney.' The characters are
easily recognised in the persons of well-known Sydney
horsey men, and the writer at this late day tells a heart-
rending story of alleged turf experiences, which were
common history amongst turfmen during the whole
process of the plucking.

* * • *
Interprovincial bruising-match between crack
bantams, Wal. M‘Kay (Vic.) and Bert Weekes (N.S W )
drew upwards of 800 people to Golden Gate Club, Sydney,
Saturday night. Both shaped up in great trim, M’Kay’s
hard chivvy and squat figure contrasting strongly with
Weekes’ boyish face (he is yet in his teens) and lathy,
streaky-built frame. It was the bull-d g and the finely-
drawn terrier. 44 It’s a shame to match ’em,’’ said several
near the writer; “what chance can that boy have
against weather-beaten, battle-worn M‘Kay ?” But they
didn’t know. Wally came the hero of 70 - battles, in not
one of which was he knocked out. Weekes, just on the
threshold of the game, had only two or three little local
affairs as credentials, but he got on so well that his
friends summed him up a champion, and they were
right. Though Weekes (7.9J4) was the lighter hv lUlb.,
he had a couple or three inches to the good in height.
Right from “Time!” Wally rushed viciously and
strongly and appeared like going through his man in
very short order : he hutted, shouldered, bored, roughed,
hung on and hit—in short, resorted to all the dodges of
the ring acquired during years of experience, and still
failed to annihilate the fragile-framed youngster in front
of him. Weekes shook from neck to heel frequently
through the fury of M 4 Kay’s onslaughts, hut never once
did he allow a knock-down punch to reach him. Mao. tried
the famous kidney blow—seen for the first time since
George Dawson left for Yankeedom seven years ago—
with palpable effect, and soon a glaring red blotch
appeared over the spot visited ; still the bov weathered
the gale, and kept jabbing with the left and crossing;
with the right while the crowd shrieked with excitement.
At the close of the twentieth round the referee called the
match a draw, but writer would have given it to Weekes,
whose work was cleverer and fairer. Persistent foul
doings should have taken away considerably from the
other chap’s score.

News from Honolulu is that erstwhile .Sydney
“Pelican Pet” Martin Denny, who has been globe trot-
ting for eight years, figured in a peculiar affair there re-
cently. He met one Armstrong, and while hashing him
about, and when just on the verge of administering the
quietus, police interference stopped hostilities. Denny
got the verdict, but a charge of collusion in getting- at a
local sport for £l6O, which he wagered on Armstrong,
caused the arrest of both principals, as well as others said
to be implicated.

From the cabled reports of the Jeffries-Sharkey
fight, for the championship it was supposed Jeffries had
knocked Sharkey out in 25 rounds, a battered, broken-
ribbed wreck, but accounts to hand show that at the end
of the 25th round, when the men were ordered to shake
hands. Jack was as good as his master so far as strength
was concerned, and the fight was awarded to Jeffrieson
“ points,” an ever-unsatisfaotory termination to a chnni-
pionship fight. Sharkey posted £lOOO for a return
match, and under the circumstances Jeffries can scarcely
refuse him another meeting.
* * • •
Writer has waded through half-a-dozen different
accounts of the recent Sharkey-Jeffries slaughtering
match for the world's pug. championship, and can arrive
at no other conclusion than that the referee made a big
mistake in giving Jeffries the palm. How anv conscien-
tious man who understood the game could decide that
way is beyond comprehension. Had a draw been
declared the hottest Jeffriesite might not have cavilled.
Sharkey led throughout the first 20 rounds, and, though
they pounded each other unmercifully at times, Jeffries
husbanded his forces and made the pace a cracker for the
last five of the stipulated 25. Sharkey fought back
like a demon, but Jeffries had too much in reserve,
and scored the greater number of points. This
influenced the referee; he forgot all about Sharkey’s
distinct lead during four-fifths of the term and was, like
many others similarly placed, carried away by what
occurred at the end. Had it been a finish fight Sharkey
might have won decisively, as his marvellous powers of
recuperation would have stood to him. It was the most
severe and punishing battle of recent times, and demon-
strated unmistakably that strong-framed, well-trained
men can stand a fearful amount of battering without
suffering serious effects. Worth mentioning that one
wager taken by a Wall-street (N.Y.) broker, who backed
Sharkey, was £5300 to £3OOO.
* • * •
It is supposed that in the old raw-meat, P.R
style Sharkey would beat any pug. now before the
public.
• * * *
“Pro.”: Presence of bookmakers at amateur
athletic meetings looks like causing a big rift in N.S W.
association. Already one enthusiastic supporter has cut
the painter, and others will surely follow. To such a pass
have things come that it is known certain athletes have
been taking it in turn to win by arrangement. T’other
day the executive met, tajked gravely concerning the
trouble, appointed a special committee to investigate
and passed resolutions providing for penalties most
severe if such things were. Truth is, the A.A.A. will not
do anything if it can be avoided. Consequently amateur
athletics have pone from bad to worse for years, until
now a very low ebb has been reached. A glaring
instance of this was the Parramatta Harriers’ case a
little while ago. They wanted to include a race for league
cyclists (professionals) in their programme. The A. A. A.
said it must not be done, otherwise direful measures would
be adopted. Parramatta Harriers took no notice, brought
the event off, and, worse still, one of the A.A.A. council-
lors started it. At its next meeting the executive
solemnly and lengthily discussed the matter, talked
determination and non-consideration of persons, dwelt
upon the duty they had to perform, &c., &c., and wound
up by formulating a resolution “severely ” censuring the
offending club. This was mild enough, but the Parra-
matta delegate kicked. He wouldn’t stand the censure,
and insisted upon it being withdrawn ; whereupon the
obliging and pliant A.A.A. legislators eliminated the
objectionable portion of their remarks and didn’t even
severely censure the Harriers.
» * * *
Percy Beauchamp, who won the Austral at 4 to
1, and started half the grandstand running for home,
and the other half pursuing it, is one of the three
brothers who carried all before them in Tasmanian
rowing a few years back. With the assistance of an out-
sider who was changed several times, the Beauchamp
brothers won the championship of the Flyspeck for the
Tamar club, and retained it by winning over 30 races.
They crossed to the mainland several times, but could do
nothing against Vic. oarsmen, and returned to take up
the more profitable sport of cycling. Percy Beauchamp
is a lank}' six-footer of 23, and has a good cycling record
in his own land. Until winning the Austral, however,
he had never finished first in a race north of the Straits.
In addition to the £3OO stake, the Beauchamps win over
£lOOO on the race.
* * « *
A few smashes on the track play the deuce with
a cyclist’s nerves as a rule. “ Chinny ’’Bill Martin is an
exception, but nobody who knows Sweet William ever
suspects him of having any nerves at all. The average
man after being smashed two or three times gets into a
way of looking for trouble, and scheming to save his neck,
and it is only the man who never recollects his neck who
wins races. The nervous one is said to be “ paced by the
undertaker.”
* * * *
A Vic. cyclist brought down in the second lap
of his heat for a big event the other day was carried off,
and recovered consciousness smin. later in the dressing-
room. “By Jove! I pulled it off all right, Jim,” were
his first words on coming round, and it was with difficult}'
he was convinced that he had not finished and come
in first.
* * * *
Australasian champion natationist Lane still
keeps his end up “down under.” He was the draw at
Edinburgh (Scotland) City Police big gala, Nov. 16, giv-
ing exhibition swims over 220 and 440 vds. courses. In a
100 yds. Invitation Handicap Lane displayed great form ;
conceding starts up to 14 sec,., he won and was clocked to
do 1 min. 2 3-5 sec. At Manchester (Eng.), Nov. 15,
British ex-amateur champion Tyers essayed the task of
eclipsing Lane’s 3 min. 47 sec. for 300 yds.. but finished
9 2-5 sec. outside. The “pub.’’ business is evidently
doing the whilom English wonder no good.
» * • «
In Frank Gailey Q. has a comer who’ll be
heard of among the top-notch swimmers of the provinces
ere along ; he already ranks with the best of the sprint-
ing division bar “Mud” Bishop (Sydney). At Q.
Swimming Association Carnival in Brisbane t’other day,
young Gailey got away with the li oyds. supremacy of
Bananaland, in lmin. s|seo., beating previous holder,
Hogan, bv 2yds. Prior to that he secured the champion-
ship of Valley Club, over same distance, in lmin. 4 4-ssec.
More than likely that Gailey will represent the Northern
province in Australasian Premiership, at Sydney, next
month.

Charge emanating from N.S.W. Public Schools
A.A.A. makes status of Australian amateur-champion
swimmers and world’s record-holder- “ Mud ” Bishop and
Fred Lane look very shaky. Said that thev each
accepted a fiver for swimming their match at All-Schools’
Carnival in Sutherland Dock, Cockatoo Island, last
season. Governing body of natation will probe the
matter this week. Question suggests itself—if the crack
swimmers are brought under the ban, where do the pro-
fessedly amateur officials who winked at, in fact were
parties to, this breach of the amateur laws come in ?

The widow of the Crown Prince of Austria
is about to make a love-match with a Hungarian
count, whose devotion is largely due, no doubt, to
the lady’s exquisite figure. Princess Stephanie is
the eldest daughter of the King of the Belgians,
the monarch who conferred the honor of knight-
hood upon the patentee of P.D. corsets. Born
and bred within the first sphere of P.D. influence,
so to speak, the Princess’s figure has been trained
on P.D. lines, and is famous for its beauty.*

Mat Reid’s win at Enoggera tallies three
bye-elections recently gained by Q. Labor Party—
Turley, at S. Brisbane, and Bowman at Warrego,
won the other two. Enoggera is the constituency
Drake resigned ; and the Labor majority shows
plainly what Drake’s old supporters think of his
treachery.
When an Adelaide broker received a cable,
the other day gravely reporting that Buller had
walked into Ladysmith and captured 6000 Boers,
each and several of the local brokers started out
to smash in each other’s hat, and an extra
humorous dealer employed a German Band to play
patriotic airs. The Mayor stayed up until mid-
night ready to ring the Town Hall bells directly
the joyful news was confirmed, but even the
reliable daily papers failed to come up for once.
The only people who have not been kicking them-
selves ever since are the German Band.
Australia is still laboring heavily over
getting its second Transvaal contingent together,
and already there is.talk about a third one. Also,
it is proposed to keep the rejected men together
in case the country may have used up its available
supply of the best physical material, and may he
darned glad to send the cripples and weeds next
time. And already the tired N.S. W. Government
is weakening on its valiant resolve to pay the bill
out of current revenue, and all manner of strange
military items are appearing in the loan estimates.
Even the military ardour of this country goes on
credit, and future generations are to pay for this
generation’s “glory.”
Military advertising-expert Lieut. -Colonel
Jawbone Neild dished his brother officers by
arranging a special parade of his (7th) regiment
at Sydney Town Hall last Sunday, when Arch-
bishop Smith and War-Minister See made
speeches. The Archbishop’s was about the
“strongest” religious utterance since Bishop
Stanton, at Newcastle, remarked that the local
streets were “consecrated” when a Transvaal
contingent tramped over them. The Bul-
letin’s exalted brother in the Lord reasoned in so
many words that because Christ was the Prince
of Peace Australians should go to Africa to fight
Boers. And this was his climax
There is a poem we have heard a good deal of lately.
Its refrain is “ For your credit’s sake, pay, pay, pay !"
But I say to you, for Christ’s sake, pay, pay, pay !
Christ is put to many remarkable uses nowadays,
but it has been reserved for an Australian Arch-
bishop to make him play second fiddle to Kipling.
“Then the collection was taken up. Weird
creatures, these religionists!
The cable-fakir of Sydney Daily Tele-
graph is an important personage just now. When
a brief message conies through like Three
hundred British surrendered," his duty is to
smooth over things by inserting “overwhelming
numbers of Boers ” (the Boers are always in over-
whelming numbers even though they haven t got
tnem), or “sanguinary struggles,” or “heroicde-
fence.” The fake-matter is mixed up with the
genuine news, so that ninety-nine people in a hun-
dred don’t know the difference. For example, in
regard to Buller’s defeat at Tugela, the D.l.
declares—
The infantry made heroic, but vain efforts, in support
of the artillery. All accounts agree in stating that the
British soldiers fought like lions, but the Boers (who ap-
peared to have crossed the river, under protection of
their heavy puns) surrounded their enemies in over-
whelming numbers and captured the survivors after a
sanguinary struggle. .
Judging by a comparison made with S.M. Herald
account (which, as a rule, sticks creditably close
to the cables), the words in italics were seemingly
about all that were actually oabled. Ihe re-
mainder. or most of it, was apparently embroidery
by the D.T. cable-fakir.
A sidelight on the efficacy-of-prayer dis-
cussion is thrown by the cable which states that a
lyddite shell fell into a Boer prayer-meeting and
killed 20 persons.
Leeds (England) Mercury reports an in-
terview with a returned Englishman long resident
in Johannesburg -
Is it not a fact (asked the interviewer) that Englishmen
have been subjected continuously to insults from the
Boers ?
That all depends upon what you call an insult.
Would you call spitting in a man’s face, for instance,
an insult ?
Certainly. I never heard of that being done, though it
is quite possible. But there are vulgar brutes amongst
all communities. Nobody in Johannesburg ever thought,
however, of going out in a morning with a handkerchief
round his physiognomy. I remember being in a bar par-
lor, and bearing a Dutchman singing out that he could
fight ten Rooineks. A mate of mine just stepped out and
tapped him on the chin, sending him sprawling. And
there’s plenty of Englishmen who’ve been on this sort of
bragging tack—many of them puny creatures who
couldn’t fight a pussy cat—and they’ve had their faces
scraped by the Boers in consequence.
What about the grievances of the Outlanders ?
I didn’t know I had any really serious grievance till I
started taking the newspapers. The Star said I had
some, and the Lkader told me the same so often that I
came to the conclusion I must have a lot which I hadn’t
noticed.
Then you think now you had serious grievances ?
I must have had. I don’t know exactly what they
were, but now I shan’t be happy till they’re righted.
But the taxes were very heavy ?
If I had been in England during the time I have been
out there, I should have paid to the benevolent British
Government nearly £5 in tobacco and whisky duties.
While I have been in the Transvaal, Oom Paul’s crowd
have collected 18s. 4d. a vear from me for poll-tax ; I gave
them 10s. for a special tax for small-pox expenses, and
they vaccinated me for another 2s. 6d.
The houses are heavily taxed ?
I believe they are, but if the householders were paying
twice as much as in any other country, they earned twice
as much.
But the courts of justice are corrupt?
Well, if an Englishman, s iy, got run in for breaking
china and killing a dog, they’d bail him out for about a
sovereign, and he would hear no more about it. If a man
got six months, before the expiration of the time the
governor would generally happen to walk round, and,
hearing the nature of the prisoner’s wickedness, would
say, “ Oh, get you out of this; you have been here too
long already.” They did not do their time often, unless
the'eorporation had plenty of work on hand.
Life, I believe, has not been too secure ?
Well, if a gun happened to go off by accident, and
killed any one, they never troubled much about it. They
have never, I believe, hanged a white man in the Trans-
vaal. Oom Paul, I believe, has a pious objection to it.
Guns have “accidentally” gone off rather often, have
they not?
No ; it is one of the quietest places in the world, com-
paratively speaking. They have often gone off accidentally
and killed niggers, but nothing much has been thought
about that. The dead nigger has just been allowed to lie
where he dropped till carried off by the officials, and no
inquiry has been made. I am speaking of the niggers
that go around at night time and tackle you.

An indispensable adjunct to the toilet
arsenal of beauty is Scrubb’s Fluid Ammonia. It
is an invaluable softener of “ hard ” water ; a
half- teaspoon fill in the wash hand basin, or two
tablespoonfuls in the bath, gives the water a feel-
ing of softness and a capacity for removing dust
and perspiration from the skin that are at once
refreshing and important. The same makers
supply a good “Antiseptic” toilet soap to use
with the ammonia, or as a solid substitute.*
Mr. Harry Franks, of Vickery’s Buildings,
82 Pitt-street, Sydney, is now advertising liisnew
wares that he secured the sole agency of in his
last travels round the world. He is still repre-
senting the wonder of this century—viz., the
linotype machine, which has had a wonderful sale
in the Australian provinces in proportion to their
population. His new labor-saving and checking
machine is the “Hey Register,” for checking
the time of employes going in or out of the em-
ployer’s warehouses or workshops. This is a
most ingenious contrivance, and no employer of
fifty hands and upwards should be without one of
these machines. They range from checking 50
employes to 200, there being four different sizes
of machines, and there is no degradation to the
employes in this system of check. Mr. Franks
also wishes to draw the attention of the Australian
public to his “ Hamilton Casli Register,” which
is a clever checking-machine for all cash taken
over the counter in all classes of business. It is
of the very best make, and as good as any other
machine of the kind on the market ; and it has
this in its favor: he can sell it very much cheaper
than any opponents can sell at in ■' * market.
He has other patents to show, and is n .■> position
to supply any printer, newspaper-proprietor,
or those in the allied trades, with all classes of
machinery, sundries and requisites in connection
with these trades.*

A Melbourne paper sums up new Minister
Salmon : “ A fluent but ineffective debater, who
can be very emphatic about nothing.”
A small schoolboy’s essay on the war, as
reported in Cassell’s Saturday Journal:—
Kruagfr and Rannerbulism is one. He is a man of
bind. air. Chaniberling has wrote to him savin come out
and fite or else give up the blud of the English you have
Tjij-.t. 18 a boar-dutchman and a wicked heethin.
Lord Kitchener has been sent for his goary blud and to
bring back his scandrelus head ded or alive.
A Victorian Leg. Councillor tells this
story. When a young man he was travelling in
Italy, and one night, having a bad cold, be retired
early at his hotel. Feeling chillv he rang the
bell m tendingto ask for another “blanket. The
landlady, a young and good-looking woman, pre-
sented herself. The Australian, not knowing a
word of Italian, relied on pantomime. He struck
his hand on his chest to indicate that he had a
cold, and shook the bedclothes at the side to shew
that he wanted more of them. The landlady
became angry and called for her husband. Her
familiarity with Italian opera led her to believe
that when a man placed his hand on his heart he
was making a declaration of love. Fortunately
the husband spok ze Inglees.
It is now reported by cable that after
General Gatacre’s defeat his guides were “mys-
teriously shot” during the retreat, and certain
perfectly loyal Australian papers assume that the
British Tommy Atkins took it for granted he had
been a victim of treachery, and executed these
persons without trial. If this is true, and the
same sort of thing is going to be taken for granted
frequently, the crop of guides will probably run
short, and Gatacre will have to find the Boers all
by himself.
A Melbourne gallery-hand once made an
extraordinary slip. Reporting Mr. Higinbotham,
then Attorney-General, he made the speaker say
that there was hereditary insanity in the family of
the Governor, Viscount Canterbury. A complaint
came to the newspaper office from Govt. House,
and the offending scribe was ordered to go there
and apologise for the error. H. Ex. good-humor-
edly accepted the amende, and after offering his
crest-fallen visitor a glass of sherry, added : “To
the best of my belief there is no lunacy in my
family, but if the newspapers go on in this way 1
shall soon be a gibbering idiot.”
A Sydney ex-skipper relates how he once
had a literary passenger much given to whisky
and Shakespeare who suddenly developed severe
d.t., and one day darted below muttering ominous
oaths. Two revolver reports sounded from the
cabin, and the skipper rushed down and met
the jim-jammer on the steps with the smoking
pistol in his hand. “ What’s up ?” demanded the
mariner. “ What’s up !—why, I’ve shot Shake-
peare ! ” “Shot whom?” “Shakespeare ; the
—. Bored two holes in his carcass!”
And in the cabin lay a 6-guinea edition of the bard
punctured, by two bullet-holes. Said skipper re-
tains this interesting relic of the tragedy.
A lineal descendant of the Iron Duke
exists in India as a travelling photographer.
Melbourne University law students relate
many a weird yarn of barrister Gregory’s eccen-
tricity. On one occasion he entered the leeture-
room in a stately manner accompanied by his lec-
ture-notes, which he had attached to his wrist by
a chain. Applause freely expressed at his em-
barrassment in detaching the refractory articles
seemed to rankle in his mind, for next day he
carefully laid a brace of revolvers on the lecture-
table and made a neat speech, in which he expressed
a hope that there would be no more stamping on
the floor. There wasn’t. The students slunk
silently away without waiting for the lecture.

SUFFERERS
The Case of
RHODA McGUIRE.
(Reported for the “Star.”)
Miss Rhoda McGuire resides at 123
Bourk e-street, Darlinghurst. A reporter
was despatched to see her, and he was
fortunate in finding the lady at home. In
answer to an interrogation as to the
truth of a report that had reached him
from Nyngan “ That she, Miss McGuire,
was one of the lucky many who had been
saved from fearful pain and continued
suffering by the timely use of Clements
Tonic,” Miss McGuire said:—“That is
quite true. I have unbounded proof of the
value of Clements Tonic, for when I was ill
1 tried I do not know how many so-called
remedies, and nothing did any lasting
good but Clements Tonic.”
“ Would you please tell me how your
llness began ? ” enquired the reporter.
“ I have never been very strong,”
answered Miss McGuire, “and four years
ago I received a most serious nervous shock,
-so serious that it appeared to upset my
'-‘"tire nervous system and derange the
normal-functions of my body. I became so
agitated and shaken with unknown fears
that I was frightened to leave the house
"ven in the day time. ' A feeling of
mysterious trouble continually oppressed
me. A sudden noise would make me start !
with trembling fear and nearly drive me i
mad. ft Solitude was unbearable ; I would 1
nBt have stopped alone even in my own I
Jbome on atfy consideration ; I dare not. |
All the brightness in my life seemed
darkened, my mind passing unceasingly
from one dismal thought to another; and,
finally, I reached that point of despair that
life did not seem to me worth living, and I
truly believe that had it not been for my
religious training and my fear of the world
to come, I should have committed suicide.
Frequently I was a victim to fits of hysteria ;
at other times I would sit crying over my
work for half the day together. Joined
with, this mental collapse were agonising
physical pains. My head was a delirium
and full of screeching noises, and when I
went to bed or tried to resb it would be
awful. I had sharp, frequent neuralgic
pains darting about my face. They would
commence on the right cheek-bone, and from
there spread upwards and downwards to
the forehead and the neck. My nerves
would throb so and I became so terribly
agitated that at times I could not put a foot
to the ground. I noticed that the faceache
increased in intensity at night time and
during the hot weather, and these attacks
would endure from three days to as long a
time as six weeks. Then I had intermittent,
stabbing pains in the back, under the left
shoulder; and after my meals I suffered
from distressing indigestion, a feeling of
weight, oppression, and tightness across the
chest, and often the food would rise in my
throat or be brought up again. This want
of nourishment further helped to weaken
my debilitated system. I was a burden to
myself, fit for nothing. In the latter days
of my sickness my heart began to trouble
me by loud palpitation.” _ - -
“Surely, Miss McGuire,” enquired the
reporter, “ you did not let yourself get so
bad by neglect ? ”
No, I tried all sorts of pills and prepara-
tions, and all without benefit. I had lost all
hope, and was sure I should never get better,
when a friend of mine strongly advised me
to take Clements Tonic, because she had
herself seen its wonderful curative power in
her own family. So strongly did she
persuade me that I agreed to try it. The
chemist was very reluctant to give me what
I asked for, enquiring my symptoms, and
then assuring me that a nerve tonic of his
own compounding would be far better and
more suited to my case ; but I remembered
my friend’s assurance, and would have
nothing else but Clements Tonic—[Wise
girl! he only wanted to sell you something
on which he got more profit—therefore
cheaper—therefore inferior.— Ed.]— and it
was well for me that I did. For even that
first bottle did me a world of. good. It
seemed to act like a concentrated food, and
to nourish my debilitated system. At the
same time it gave strength to my disordered
nerves, and built and braced me up all over.
My fits of hysteria were banished, the fear-
ful depression and melancholy mind passed
away. I was no longer afraid to go out or
to be left alone. The neuralgic pains in the
face and the screeching noises in head
troubled me no more. My digestion
steadily improved, my meals remained in
my stomach, and were not followed by the
heavy, oppressive feeling in the chest. The
pains under the left shoulder ceased to
trouble me, and in all my general
health took a wondrous turn for the better.
I was in every sense a new woman. ' And I
am quite sure that this revolution in my :
deplorable plight was all owing to Clements
lonic. X never weary of telling every one
so and trying to persuade others who are
sick to try it. You are at liberty to publish
what I say in any way you like if you think
it will be of use; but it seems to me s -
many persons have tried and proved the
undoubted value of Clements Tonic
what I have said must be merely an echo c
an oft repeated tale.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Rhoda McGuire, of 123 Bourke St., Darlinghurst,
near Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully-
read the annexed document, consisting of four
folios, and consecutively numbered from one to-
four, and that it contains and is a true and faith-
ful account of my illness and cure by Clements
Tonic, and also contains my full permission to publish
the same in any way; and I make this solemn
declaration, voluntarily and without receiving anv
payment, conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act
made and passed in the ninth vear of the reign of
her present .Majesty, intituled ‘'An Act for the more
effectual abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken
and made in the various Departments of the Govern-
ment of New South Wales, and to substitute
Declarations in lieu thereof, and for the suppression
of voluntary and extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits."

The Story of the Oracle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“We young fellows,” said “ Sympathy Joe ” to
Mitchell, after tea, in their first camp west the
liver—“ and you and I are young fellows, com-
paratively think we know the world. There are
plenty of young chaps knocking round in this
country who reckon they’ve been through it all
before they’re 30. I’ve met cynics and men-o -
the-world aged 21 or thereabouts, who’ve never
been further than a trip to Sydney. They talk
about ‘ this world ’ as if they’d knocked around in
half-a-dozen other worlds before they came across
here—and they are just as off-hand about it as
older Australians are when they talk about this
colony as compared with the others. They say :
‘My oath !—same here.’ ‘ I’ve been there.’ ‘My
oath ! —you’re right.’ ‘ Take it from me ! ’ and all
that sort of thing. They understand women, and
have a contempt for ’em ; and chaps that don’t
talk as they talk, or do as they do, or see as they
see, are either ‘soft’ or ‘ratty.’ A good many
reckon that ‘ life ain’t blanky well worth livin’ ’;
sometimes they feel so blanky somehow that they
wouldn’t give a hang whether they chucked it
or not; but that sort never ‘chuck it.’ It’s
mostly the quiet men that do that, and if they’ve
got any complaints to make against the world
they make ’em at the head-station. Why, I’ve
known healthy single young fellows under 25
who drank to drown their troubles —some because
they reckoned the world didn’t understand or
appreciate them—as if it could l ”
“If the world don’t understand or appreciate
you,” said Mitchell solemnly, as he reached for a
burning stick to light his pipe—“ make it! ”
“To drown their troubles!” continued -Toe.
“The Oracle must be well on towards the sixties ;
he can take his glass with any man, but you never
saw him drunk.”
“ What’s the Oracle to do with it ? ”
“ Did you ever hear his history ? ”
“ No. Do you know it ? ”
“Yes ; though I don’t think he has any idea that
I do. Now, we were talking about the Oracle a
little while ago. We know he’s an old ass ; a good
many outsiders consider that he’s a bit ‘soft’or
‘ratty’; and, as We’re likely to be mates together
for some time on that fencing contract, if we get
it, you might as well know what sort of a man he
is and was, so’s you won’t get uneasy about him if
he gets deaf for awhile when you’re talking, or
does funny things with his pipe or pint-pot, or
walks up and down by himself for an hour or so
after tea, or sits on a log with his head in his
hands, or leans on the fence in the gloaming and
keeps looking in a blank sort of way, straight
ahead, across the clearing. Eor he’s gazing at
something a thousand miles across country, south-
east, and about 20 years back into the past, and
no doubt he sees himself (as a young man) and a
Gippsland girl, spooning under the stars along
between the hop-gardens and the Mitchell River.
And, if you get licit of a fiddle or a concertina,
don’t rasp or swank too much on old tunes, when
he’s round, for the Oracle can’t stand it. Play
something lively. He’ll be down there at that
surveyor’s camp yarning till all hours, so we’ll
have plenty of time for the story —but don’t you
ever give him a hint that you know.
“My people knew him well; I got most of the
story from them—mostly from Uncle Bob, who
knew him better than any. The rest leaked out
through the women—you know how things leak
out amongst women ? ”
Mitchell dropped his head and scratched the
back of it. He knew.
“It was on the Cudgegong River. My Uncle
Bob was mates with him on one of those ‘ rushes ’
along there—the ‘Pipeclay,’ I think it was, or the
‘ Log Paddock.’ The Oracle was a young man
then, of course, and so was Uncle Bob (he was a
match for most men). You see the Oracle now,
and you can imagine what he was when he was a
young mail. Over six feet, and as straight as a
sapling, Uncle Bob said ; clean-limbed, and as
fresh as they made men in those days ; carried his
hands behind him, as he does now, when he hasn’t
got the swag —but his shoulders were back in
those days. Of course, he wasn’t the Oracle then,
he was young Tom Marshall but that doesn’t
matter. Everybody liked him—especially women
and children. He was a bit happy-go-lucky and
careless, but he didn’t know anything about ‘this
world,’ and didn’t bother about it; he hadn’t
‘been there.’ ‘And his heart was as good as
gold,’ my aunt used to say. He didn’t understand
women as we young fellows do nowadays, and
therefore he hadn't any contempt for ’em.
Perhaps he understood, and understands, them
better than any of us, without knowing it. Any-
way, you know, he’s always gentle and kind where
a woman or child is concerned, and doesn’t like to
hear us talk about women as we do sometimes.
“ There was a girl on the goldfields ; a fine lump
of a blonde, and pretty gay. She came from
Sydney, I think, with her people, who kept
shanties on the fields. She had a splendid voice,
and used to sing ‘ Madeline.’ There might have
been one or two bad women before that, in the
Oracle’s world, but no cold-blooded, designing
ones. He calls the bad ones ‘ unfortunate.’
“ Perhaps it was Tom’s looks, or his freshness,
or his innocence, or softness —or all together—
that attracted her. Anyway, he' got mixed up
with her before the goldfield petered out.
“No doubt it took a long while for the fact to
work into Tom’s head that a girl might sing like
she did and yet be thoroughly unprincipled. The
Oracle was always slow at coming to a decision,
but when he does it’s generally the right one.
Anyway, you can take that for granted, for you
won’t move him.
“ I don’t know whether he found out that she
wasn’t all that she pretended to be to him, or
whether they quarrelled, or whether she chucked
him over for a lucky digger. Tonynever had any
luck on the goldfields. Anyway, he left and went
over to the Victorian side, where his people were,
and went up Gippsland way. It was there for
the first time in his life that he got what you
would call ‘ properly gone on a girl ’; he got hard
hit—he met his fate.
“ Her name was Ruth Wilson, I remember.
Aunt Bob saw her afterwards. Aunt Bob used to
say that she was ‘ a girl as God made her ’ a
good, true, womanly girl—one of those sort of
girls that only love once. Tom got on with her
father, who was packing horses through the
ranges to the new goldfields—it was rough country
and there were no roads ; they had to pack every-
thing there in those days, and there was money in
it. The girl’s father took to Tom —as almost
everybody else did —and, as far as the girl was
concerned, I think it was a case of love at first
sight. They only knew each other for about six
months and were only ‘courting’ (as they called
it then) for three or four jnonths altogether, but
she was that sort of girl ’that can love a man for
six weeks, and lose him for ever, and yet go on
loving him to the end of her life —and die with
his name on her lips.
“ Well, things were brightening up every way
for Tom, and he and his sweetheart were begin-
fling to talk about their own little home in future,
when there came a letter from the ‘Madeline’
girl in N.S. W.
“She was in terrible trouble. Her baby was to
be born in a month. Her people had kicked her
out and she was in danger of starving. She
begged and prayed of him to come back and marry
her if only for his child’s sake. He could go then,
and he free ; she would never trouble him any
more—onlv come and marry her for the child s
sake. '
“ The Oracle doesn’t know where he lost that
letter, but I do. It was burnt afterwards by a
woman who was more than a mother to him in his
trouble—Aunt Bob. She thought he might carry
it round with the rest of his papers, in his swag,
for years, and come across it unexpectedly when
he was camped by himself in the bush and feeling
dull. It wouldn’t have done him any good then.
“He must have fought the hardest fight in his
life when he got that letter. No doubt he walked
to and fro, to and fro, all night, with his hands
behind him, and his eyes on the ground, as he does
now sometimes. Walking up and down helps you
to fight a thing out.
“ No doubt he thought of things pretty well as
he thinks now : the poor girl’s shame on every
tongue, and belled round the district by every hag
in the township ; and she looked upon by women
as being as bad as any man who ever went to
Bathurst in the old days, handcuffed between two
troopers. There is sympathy, a pipe and tobacco,
a cheering word, and, maybe, a whisky now and
then, for the criminal on his journey ; but there
is no mercy, at least as far as women are con-
cerned, for the poor foolish girl who has to sneak
out the back Way and round by back streets and
lanes after dark, with a cloak on to hide her
figure.
“ Tom sent what money he thought he could
spare, and next day he went to the girl he loved
and who loved him, and told her the truth, and
showed her the letter. She was only a girl—but
the sort of girl you could go to in a crisis like
that. He had made up his mind to do the right
thing, nd she loved him all the more for it. And
so they parted.
“ When Tom reached ‘ Pipeclay,’ the girl’s re-
lations, that she was stopping with, had a parson
readied up, and they were married the same day.
“And what happened after that?” asked Mit-
cbe ]]
“ Nothing happened for three or four months;
then the child was born. It wasn’t his ! ”
Mitchell stood up, with an oath.
“The girl was thoroughly bad. She’d been
carrying on with God knows how many men, both
before and after she trapped Tom.”
“ And what did he do then ? ”
“ Well, you know how the Oracle argues over
things, and I suppose he was as big an old fool
then as he is now. He thinks that, as most men
would deceive women if they could, when one
man gets caught, he’s got no call to squeal about
it; he’s bound, because of the sins of men in
general against women, to make the best of it.
What is one man’s wrong counted against the
wrongs of hundreds of unfortunate girls.”
“ It’s an uncommon way of arguing—like most
of the Oracle’s ideas—but it seems to look all
right at first sight.”
“Perhaps he” thought she’d go straight; per-
haps she convinced him that he was the cause of
her first fall; anyway he stuck to her for more
than a year, and intended to take her away from
that place as soon as he’d soraped enough money
together. It might have gone on up till now, i
the father of the child—a big reel mm
named Redmond—hadn’t come sneaking >ac <
the end of a year. He—well, lie came hanging
round Mrs. Marshall w .lpl e Urf Tom^was
work—and she encouraged h i.
forced to see it.
“Tom wanted to fight out his own battle w
out interference, blit the chaps wouldn tie 11111
they reckoned that he’d stand very little s >o\
againsi -dinond, who was a very rough cus oine
and a lighting man. My uncle Bob, who was
there still, fixed it up this way :1 he Oracle was
to fight Redmond, and if the Oracle got lie u
Uncle Bob was to take Redmond on. 1 ?
rnond whipped Uncle Bob, that was to sett e l ,
but if Uncle Bob thrashed Redmond, then lie via
also to fight Redmond s mate, another big, 1 o l ig
Paddy named Duingan. Then the affair won e
finished—no matter which way the last bou
went. You see, Uncle Bob was reckoned more o
a match for Redmond than the Oracle was, so i
thing looked fair enough at first sight,
“ Redmond had his mate, Duingan, and one or
two others of the rough gang that used to ter-
rorise the fields round there in the roaring days of
Gulgong. The Oracle had Uncle Boh, of couise,
and long Dave Regan, the drover a good-near et,
sawny kind of chap that d break the devil s own
buck-jumper, or smash him, or get smasher nn-
self—and little Jimmy Nowlett, the bullocky, anc
one or two of the old, better-class diggers tna
were left on the field.
“There’s a clear space among the saplings m
Specimen Gully, where they used to pitch cir-
cuses, and here, in the cool of a summer evening,
the two men stood face to face. Redmond was a
rough, roaring, foul-mouthed man ; he stripped to
his shirt, and roared like a bull, and swore, and
sneered, and wanted to take the whole of loms
crowd while he was at it, and make one clean job
of 'em. Couldu. t waste time fighting them a
one after the other, because he wanted to get away
to the new rush at Cattle Creek next day. ine
fool had been drinking shanty-whisky,
“Tom stood up in his clean white moles and
white flannel shirt—one of those sort with no
sleeves, that give the arms play. He had a sort
of set expression and a look in his eyes that Uncle
Bob—nor none of them had ever seen there
before. ‘ Give us plenty of room . roared
Redmond, ‘one of us is going to hell, now . ah is
is going to be a fight to a finish, and a
short one ! ’ And it was ! ”
“Go on,” said Mitchell “go on .
Joe drew a long breath.
“ The Oracle never got a mark! He was top-
dog right from the start. Perhaps it was his
strength that Redmond had underrated, or his
want of science that puzzled him, or the awful
silence of the man that frightened him (it made
even Uncle Bob uneasy). Or, perhaps, it was
Providence (it was a glorious chance for
Providence), but, anyway, as I say, the
Oracle never got a mark, except on his knuckles,
After a few rounds Redmond funked and wanted
to give in, but the chaps wouldn’t let him, not
even his own mates—except Duingan. I’hey made
him take it as long as he could stand on his feet.
He even shammed to be knocked-out, and roared
out something about having broken his —— ankle
—hut it was n 0 use - And the Oracle ! The chaps
that knew thought that he’d refuse to fight, and
never hit a man that had given in. But he did.
He just stood there with that quiet look in his
eyes and waited, and, when he did hit, there

“ Mr. PALMER, Agent for Webber’s Vitadatio.
“Dear Sir, —About eleven months ago I took a long bicycle journey, and when camping out
caught a severe cold, resulting in a painful cough. I got worse, and ultimately became unable to
work. I consulted a noted doctor, who thoroughly examined me, and then sent for my parents (I
am 20 years of age). He told me that I had serious catarrh of the right lung, and that if I did not
at once cease work, and take the greatest care, I would not live longer than three months. I took
his medicine, but it did me no good, as I could not go away. I saw several other noted doctors
(all their names are available). Webber’s Vitadatio was just then attracting great attention
through its extraordinary cures, and I was induced by a friend to try it, although I must confess
that it took him a long time to persuade me, as I had tried many things without benefit. I took
six bottles altogether." The first bottle made me feel worse, but after that I made rapid progress
towards recovery. After the fourth bottle I resumed work as usual, and EVER SINCE COM-
PLETING MY SIXTH BOTTLE I HAVE BEEN QUITE WELL—the catarrh all gone, and
my breathing perfect. Vitadatio must be very good to have done in so short a time what it has
done for me—built me up and made me strong and happy. _ It is about three months since I took
my last dose. lam town traveller for a well-known firm in Brisbane, and am well known here
myself. It is always a pleasure to me to induce others to try Vitadatio, and I voluntarily offer
this testimonial for publication or to be used in any way you please. Refer any ‘doubting
Thomas ’ to me, and I will remove his doubts.
“Yours faithfully and gratefully,
“FREDRIC MILLER,

SOLE AGENT:


wasn’t any necessity for Redmond to pretend to
be knocked down. You’ll see a glint of that old
light in the Oracle’s eyes even now, once in a
while; and when you do it’s a sign that you or
some one are going too far and had better pull up,
for it’s a red light on the line, old as he is.
“Now, Jimmy Nowlett was a nuggety little
fellow, hard as cast-iron, good-hearted but very
excitable ; and when the bashed Redmond was
being carted off (poor Uncle Bob was always
pretty high-strung, and was sitting on a log sob-
bing like a great child from the reaction), Duingan
made some sneering remark that only Jimmy
Nowlett caught, and in an instant he was up and
at him!
“Perhaps Duingan was demoralised by his
mate’s defeat, or by the suddenness of the attack ;
but, at all events, he got a hiding, too. Uncle
Bob used to say that it was the funniest thing he
ever saw in his life. Jimmy kept yelling: ‘ Let
me get at him ! By the Lord, let me get at him ! ’
And nobody was attempting to stop him, he was
getting at him all the time —and properly, too ;
and, when he’d knocked Duingan down, he’d dance
round him and call on him to get up ; and every
time he jumped or bounced, he d squeak like an
india-rubber ball, Uncle Bob said, and he would
nearly burst his boiler trying to lug the big man
on to his feet so’s he could knock him down again.
It took two of Jimmy’s mates all their time to
lamb him down into a comparatively reasonable
state of mind after the fight was over.
“The Oracle left for Sydney next day, and
Uncle Bob went with him. He stayed at Uncle
Bob’s place for some time. He got very quiet,
they said, and gentle ; he used to play with the
children, and they got mighty fond of him. The
old folks thought his heart was broken, but it
went through a deeper sorrow still, after that,
and it ain’t broken yet. It takes a lot to break
the heart of a man.”
“And his wife,” asked Mitchell—“what became
of her ? ”
“I don’t think he ever saw her again. She
dropped down pretty low after he left her—l’ve
heard she’s living somewhere quietly. _ The
Oracle’s been sending someone money ever since I
knew him, and I know it’s a woman. I suppose
it’s she. He isn’t the sort of a man to see a woman
starve —especially a woman he had ever had any-
thing to do with.”
“And the Gippsland girl?” asked Mitchell.
“That’s the worst part of it all, I think. The
Oracle went up North somewhere. In the course
of a year or two his affair got over Gippsland way
through a mate of his who lived over there, and
at last the story got to the ears of this girl, Ruth
Wilson. She must have written a dozen letters
to him, Aunt Bob said. She knew what was in
’em, but, of course, she’d never tell us. The
Oracle only wrote one in reply. Then, what
must the girl do but clear out from home and
make her way over to Sydney—to Aunt Bob’s
place, looking for Tom. She never got any
further. She took ill—brain-fever, or broken-
heart, or something of that sort. All the time
she was down her cry was —‘I want to see him !
I want to find Tom. I only want to see Tom ! ’
“When they saw. she was dying, Aunt Bob
wired to the Oracle to come—and he came. When
the girl saw it was Tom sitting by the bed, she
just gave one long look in his face, put her arms
round his neck, and laid her head on his shoulder
—and died. . . Here comes the Oracle now.”
Mitohell lifted the tea-billy on to the coals.
HENRY LAWSON.
A Psalm of Labor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Riding into Town,
In the morning early,
Gazing blithely down
On the hurly-burly,
Throned upon a ’bus,
Big as a two-roomer,
Life is glorious—
When you catch its humor.
Witticisms fly—
Borak, slang, and guyver—
What a wag am I !
What a wit the driver 1
“ Hi! you corpses, Hey 1 ”
With a yell uncanny,
Thus we clear the way
For the “Gentle Annie.
Plunge and lurch along,
Grind, and roll, and rattle,
She is going strong—
See her smoking c ittle !
Driver with a whip
Them to speed importunes;
They, upon this trip,
Bear me and my fortunes.
Here above the dust,
Sitting like a god, I,
With a gaze august,
All the passing squad eye :
Mournful seems that squad ;
I,' uplifted, lonely,
Am indeed a god—
This size—twopence only.
Other gods there are,
Some of them abusive —
This triumphal car
Is not too exclusive.
So we travel on,
While the road is rolled up,
Like a Pantheon
Going to be sold up.
Like a rope outfrayed
Winds a long procession ;
Folk of ev’ry trade,
Calling and profession :
Some with dull and dim
Eyes that know not laughter ;
Some with features grim—
Features of the Grafter.
In this caravan,
Stern and sad, of Labor,
Hardly any man
Speaks unto his neighbor.
Hirers and the hired
All their thought of pence is—
Lord 1 They make me tired,
Ere my work commences 1
In parenthesis
I may make the statement —
I was not for this
Sort of thing by Fate meant.
Sumptuously to shirk
Work was I intended,
Yet, you see, to work
I have condescended.
Lo, my country called —
Wonderful her nerve is ! —
Me to be installed
In her Civil Service,
At the call I went,
Just as if for pleasure;
Now the Government
Owns, indeed, a Treasure.
But by some weird plan
I was placed, with sniggers,
Like a waxworks man,
Midst a maze of figures !
Still I did not let
Figures make me solemn—
See me pirouette
Up and down a column !
See me flout with ease
All their tricks—good Heavens !
Fives that looked like threes,
Nines disguised as sevens!
See me ohase a nought—
Soon is it outwitted,
On my pen-point caught
Like a partridge spitted !
On they now may come
One, or all together,
Every blessed sum
Underneath the weather
They will find the same
Party very willing :—
Now I know the game,
It is simply thrilling.
Take it now from me,
All you melancholy
Persons, sour to see,
Work is simply jolly—
So, for God’s sake, smile
On your toiling neighbor;
In a little while
Done will be your Labor.
CBEEVE ROE.
The Mussel-Hawker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His dress was not “respectable,” and be gathered
in his rounds the dust of highways and byways,
which the sweat of the dog-days drifted into all
his corners, or washed into long ridges along his
bony jaws and down his tough, leathery neck.
He was small but wiry, and the common constable
and equally-intelligent reporter would have had
no difficulty in immediately classifying him.
Niok was of the larrikin type. Besides, Nick
pushed a barrow, and amongst a numerous class
of fat and fairly-influential gentlemen who never
push anything, to push a barrow is to accept the
moral responsibility of all acts of small burglary
and minor assault recently committed in the
neighborhood in which the barrow circulates.
More: it is held that pushing a barrow, however
loaded, is a mere insolent deceit on the part of a
born loafer.
Mussels were Nick’s speciality. Somewhere
and at some time in the dim hours of early morn-
ing, the “ lad ” gathered many loads of shellfish
off the pier-piles and the sea.rocks, and through-
out the rest of the day and well on into the night
he hawked them with desperate energy in all the
poor quarters of two dozen suburbs. There were
other mussel-men, but Nick was at the head of his
profession, and had come to that proud eminence
by a quality of industry that was deserving of the
respectful emulation of great statesmen, just then
doing much less for the people than the indurated
“ peb ” who carried cheap shellfish to impoverished
families in unpleasant back streets.
I met him everywhere, plugging along, head
down, through driving dust, pelting rain, or
withering heat, rattling a sort of devil’s tattoo on
an old kerosene-tin, and uttering his particular
cry, his exclusive right in which was preserved by
an unwritten law of the profession.
Nick covered amazing distances, and appealed
to a wide circle. It had to be done when mussels
were commanding only a “ thrum ” a bucket, and
I saw him but once when he was not nobly sus-
taining my belief in him as the worthiest hustler
on a whole continent.
When I entered the Court, the Bench, repre-
sented by a stout man, with a blotched face and
purple neck, was saying, in the manner of a
Methody parson :
“ This Court does not believe your story, sir.
You cannot deceive this Court. We know your
sort, and we have every confidence in our police.
You belong to a class that is a disgrace to this
colony ; a worthless class ; a class of loafers and
wasters, who despise honest toil, and prey upon
the respectable and industrious portion of the com-
munity. Sixty shillings or fourteen days 1 ”
“ I’ll take it out, mister,” said the prisoner,
wearily.
The prisoner was Niok, the mussel man.
